<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The priest <b>would bring an earthenware</b> drinking <b>vessel [<i>peyalei</i>] and he would pour into it half a <i>log</i> of water from the basin</b> in the Temple. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> The priest would pour only <b>a quarter</b>-<i>log</i> of water. <b>Just as</b> Rabbi Yehuda <b>minimizes the writing,</b> as he requires that less be written on the scroll of the <i>sota</i> than do the Rabbis, <b>so too he minimizes the</b> amount of <b>water</b> to be taken from the basin for the erasing of the text.
The priest <b>would enter the Sanctuary and turn to his right. And there was a place there,</b> on the Sanctuary floor, with an area of <b>a cubit by a cubit, and a marble tablet [<i>tavla</i>]</b> was there, <b>and a ring was fastened to</b> the tablet to assist the priest <b>when</b> he <b>would raise</b> it. <b>And</b> the priest <b>would take</b> loose <b>dust from underneath it and place</b> the dust into the vessel with the water, <b>so that</b> the dust <b>would be visible upon the water, as it is stated:</b> “And the priest shall take holy water in an earthen vessel; <b>and of the dust that is on the floor of the Tabernacle the priest shall take, and put it into the water”</b> (Numbers 5:17).
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Sages <b>taught:</b> It must be <b>a new earthenware vessel;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yishmael.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reasoning of Rabbi Yishmael?</b> The Gemara answers: Rabbi Yishmael <b>derives</b> this by means of a verbal analogy between <b>“vessel”</b> in the case of the <i>sota</i> (Numbers 5:17) and <b>“vessel” from</b> the case of <b>a leper</b> (Leviticus 14:5). <b>Just as there,</b> with regard to the leper, <b>a new earthenware</b> vessel is required, <b>so too here,</b> a <b>new earthenware</b> vessel is required.
<b>And there,</b> with regard to the leper, <b>from where do we</b> derive that a new vessel is required? The Gemara answers: <b>As it is written: “And he shall slaughter one of the birds in an earthen vessel over running water”</b> (Leviticus 14:5). <b>Just as running water has not been used</b> beforehand <b>for work,</b> i.e., once used it is no longer considered to be running, <b>so too</b> the <b>vessel</b> must <b>not have been used for work.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>If</b> this verbal analogy is extended, then <b>just as there, running water</b> from a flowing spring is required, <b>so too here,</b> with regard the <i>sota</i>, <b>running water</b> from a spring should be required for the water of a <i>sota</i>.
The Gemara answers: <b>According to Rabbi Yishmael</b> it is <b>indeed</b> so, <b>as Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> With regard to <b>the water of the basin</b> in the Temple <b>Rabbi Yishmael says: It is</b> collected from <b>spring water. And the Rabbis say: It</b> may also be <b>from</b> any <b>other</b> type of <b>water</b> and need not be collected from spring water.
The Gemara asks: The verbal analogy <b>can be refuted:</b> One cannot apply <i>halakhot</i> stated with regard to a leper to a <i>sota</i>, <b>as what</b> is unique <b>about a leper</b> is <b>that he requires cedar wood, and the hyssop, and the scarlet</b> wool for the performance of his purification ritual, and these are not required of the <i>sota</i>. Why, then, should a new vessel be required by the <i>sota</i>?
<b>Rabba said: The verse</b> cited in the mishna <b>states:</b> “And the priest shall take holy water <b>in an earthen vessel”</b> (Numbers 5:17). The Torah makes no prior mention of the need for the priest to bring with him an earthenware vessel. Therefore, the verse must mean that the water should be placed in <b>the vessel that I have already told you</b> about, i.e., the vessel used for the leper.
<b>Rava says:</b> Even according to the opinion that a new vessel is not required, <b>they taught</b> that the earthenware vessel is taken <b>only when its exterior was not blackened</b> from usage. <b>But if its exterior was blackened, then it is unfit</b> for use by the <i>sota</i>. <b>What is the reason</b> for this? Its requirements are <b>similar</b> to those <b>of water: Just as</b> the <b>water</b> must be clear and <b>unchanged</b> in <b>appearance, so too</b> the <b>vessel</b> must <b>be unchanged</b> in appearance.
<b>Rava raised a dilemma:</b> If the vessel’s exterior <b>was blackened, and it was returned to the furnace and became white</b> again, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? <b>Do we say</b> that <b>once it has been disqualified, it is disqualified</b> forever and can never be rendered fit for use? <b>Or perhaps since it has returned</b> to a white appearance <b>it has returned</b> to a state of fitness.
<b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> evidence from that which <b>Rabbi Elazar says: If</b> the leper <b>tied</b> the <b>cedar wood, and</b> the <b>hyssop, and</b> the <b>scarlet</b> wool for his purification <b>to his basket behind him,</b> so as to carry it on his back, they are <b>disqualified,</b> since their form has changed. <b>But there,</b> after those items have been tied, they can be <b>smoothed out again</b> as if they had never been used, and still they are unfit. Evidently, after being disqualified an item cannot become fit again.
The Gemara answers: <b>There,</b> the items are permanently unfit <b>because they are peeled</b> due to tying and can never truly return to their original appearance. That case does not provide proof.
The mishna states: The priest <b>would enter the Sanctuary and turn to his right.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason</b> that he should do so? The Gemara responds: <b>As the Master said: All turns that you turn should be only to the right.</b>
The mishna continues: <b>There was a place there,</b> on the Sanctuary floor, with an area of <b>a cubit</b> by a cubit, and a marble tablet was there and a ring was fastened to the tablet so that it could be raised. When the priest would raise the tablet, he would take loose dust from underneath it and place the dust into the vessel with the water, so that the dust would be visible upon the water, as it is stated: “And the priest shall take holy water in an earthen vessel; and of the dust that is on the floor of the Tabernacle the priest shall take, and put it into the water” (Numbers 5:17). <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If the verse had stated only: <b>“And of the dust that is</b> on the floor,” one <b>might</b> have thought that the priest could <b>prepare</b> the concoction from dust <b>from outside</b> the Sanctuary <b>and bring it in</b> afterward.
Therefore, <b>the verse states:</b> “The dust that is <b>on the floor of the Tabernacle,”</b> indicating that the dust must be from inside the Sanctuary. <b>If</b> the verse had stated only: <b>“On the floor of the Tabernacle,”</b> one <b>might</b> have thought that the priest may <b>dig with axes</b> to loosen the dust there. Therefore, <b>the verse states:</b> “And of the dust <b>that is</b> on the floor of the Tabernacle,” indicating that the dust must be lying there loosely. <b>How so?</b> If <b>there is</b> already loose earth <b>there</b> on the Sanctuary floor, <b>bring</b> it; if <b>there is none there,</b> then <b>place</b> loose dust <b>there</b> from elsewhere, and then pick it up and use it.
<b>It is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: <b>“And of the dust that is</b> on the floor of the Tabernacle”; the fact that the verse does not explicitly state to take the dust from the floor of the Tabernacle <b>teaches that</b> the priest <b>would prepare</b> dust <b>from outside and bring it into</b> the Sanctuary. When the verse states: “The dust that is <b>on the floor of the Tabernacle,” Isi ben Yehuda says</b> that this phrase serves <b>to include the floor of</b> the Tabernacle when it stood in

Daf 16a

<b>Shiloh, Nob, and Gibeon, and</b> also <b>the Eternal House,</b> i.e., the Temple in Jerusalem. The dust for the <i>sota</i> is still brought from the ground of the Sanctuary wherever it is located, even after the Jewish people are no longer in the wilderness.
<b>Isi ben Menaḥem says: There is no need</b> to derive this <i>halakha</i> from the verse. It may be learned by an <i>a fortiori</i> inference: <b>With regard to</b> the prohibition against entering the Sanctuary in a state of <b>impurity, a lenient</b> matter for which there is no court-imposed capital punishment, <b>the Torah does not differentiate.</b> It is prohibited for an impure person to enter the Tabernacle no matter where it stands. Therefore, <b>with regard to the impurity of a married woman,</b> which is <b>stringent</b> and carries the penalty of strangulation, <b>all the more so</b> is it <b>not</b> clear that the Torah does not differentiate? The dust must be brought from the Sanctuary no matter where it stands. <b>If so, what</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states:</b> “And of the dust that is <b>on the floor of the Tabernacle”?</b> It teaches <b>that one should not bring</b> dust <b>from his</b> own <b>basket</b> and place it directly into the water; he must first place it on the floor.
<b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: If <b>there is no dust there, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? May one <b>place ashes</b> in the vessel instead? The Gemara responds: There is <b>no</b> need to <b>raise the dilemma</b> if one holds <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Beit Shammai, as they say: We never find ashes referred to as dust</b> in the Torah.
<b>When you raise the dilemma,</b> it is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Beit Hillel, who say: We</b> do <b>find ashes referred to as dust</b> in the context of the red heifer (Numbers 19:17). They likewise hold that ashes may also be used instead of dust to cover the blood of a slaughtered bird or undomesticated animal (see Leviticus 17:13). <b>What</b> is the <i>halakha</i> here, with regard to the water of a <i>sota</i>? May ashes replace dust? <b>Although</b> elsewhere ashes may be <b>referred to as dust, here it is written: “On the floor of the Tabernacle,”</b> indicating that dust in particular is required, since dust comes from the ground. <b>Or perhaps</b> the phrase <b>“on the floor of the Tabernacle” comes</b> only to teach that the <i>halakha</i> is <b>in accordance with</b> the teaching of <b>Isi ben Yehuda or in accordance with</b> the teaching of <b>Isi ben Menaḥem.</b> If so, perhaps ashes are acceptable as well.
<b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> evidence from <b>that</b> which <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says in the name of Rabbi Yishmael: In three instances the <i>halakha</i> supersedes the verse,</b> i.e., the tradition alters the straightforward meaning of the verse.
<b>The Torah states:</b> “And whatsoever man…that takes in hunting any beast or fowl that may be eaten, he shall pour out the blood thereof, and cover it <b>in dust”</b> (Leviticus 17:13), <b>but the <i>halakha</i> is</b> that the blood may be covered <b>in anything</b> similar to dust. <b>The Torah states</b> with regard to the nazirite: “All the days of his vow of naziriteship there shall be no <b>razor</b> come upon his head” (Numbers 6:5), <b>but the <i>halakha</i> is</b> that the nazirite may not remove his hair <b>with anything. The Torah states:</b> “That he writes her a <b>bill [<i>sefer</i>]</b> of divorce” (Deuteronomy 24:1). The word <i>sefer</i> denotes a scroll, <b>but the <i>halakha</i> is</b> that the husband may inscribe the bill of divorce <b>on anything</b> that is detached from the ground and suitable to be written upon, not only on a scroll.
<b>And if it is so</b> that ashes may be placed in the water of a <i>sota</i> despite the verse’s stipulation of dust, <b>consider this</b> fourth case <b>as well</b> to be a <i>halakha</i> that supersedes the verse. Since it is omitted from Rabbi Yishmael’s statement, it seems that ashes may not be used.
The Gemara responds: Rabbi Yishmael <b>taught</b> some cases <b>and omitted</b> others; his list is not exhaustive. The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> else <b>did he omit that he omitted this?</b> It is not reasonable that he would provide a list lacking only one item. The Gemara answers: <b>He omitted the leper, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Nega’im</i> 1:9): In the verse: <b>“And it shall be on the seventh day, that he shall shave off all his hair”</b> (Leviticus 14:9), the phrase “all his hair” is <b>a generalization.</b> The phrase that follows: <b>“His head and his beard and his eyebrows,”</b> is <b>a detail.</b> And with the following phrase: <b>“Even all his hair he shall shave off,”</b> the verse <b>then generalized</b> again. In any case of <b>a generalization, and a detail, and a generalization, you may deduce</b> that the verse is referring <b>only</b> to items <b>similar to the detail. Just as the detail is explicitly</b> referring to <b>areas</b> where there is <b>a collection of hair which is visible, so too all areas</b> on the leper that have <b>a collection of hair which is visible</b> must be shaven.
To <b>what</b> otherwise excluded case does this <i>baraita</i> <b>extend</b> the <i>halakha</i>? It <b>extends</b> the <i>halakha</i> of hair to include <b>pubic hair. What</b> does the <i>baraita</i> <b>exclude? It excludes armpit hair,</b> which is not visible, <b>and body hair</b> that is not collected. This is the straightforward meaning of the verse.
<b>And</b> yet <b>the <i>halakha</i> is:</b> The leper <b>shaves like a gourd,</b> i.e., his entire body must be shaved. <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Nega’im</i> 14:2): When the priest <b>comes to shave the leper, he passes a razor over all of his flesh. And</b> in <b>the latter clause,</b> the mishna <b>teaches: On the seventh day he shaves</b> the leper again. <b>The second shaving is</b> just <b>like the first shaving.</b> The verse previously analyzed is referring to the second shaving, and its straightforward meaning is that not all of the leper’s flesh needs to be shaved. However, the mishna states that the leper must shave all of his flesh in the second shaving as well. This is another instance where the <i>halakha</i> supersedes the straightforward meaning of the verse, yet it is omitted from Rabbi Yishmael’s list.
<b>Rabbi Naḥman bar Yitzḥak said:</b> Rabbi Yishmael consciously omitted the <i>halakha</i> of the leper because he <b>counted</b> only instances where the <b><i>halakha</i> supersedes</b> the straightforward meaning of the <b>verse. This</b> <i>halakha</i> of the leper, however, <b>is</b> an instance where the <i>halakha</i> <b>supersedes</b> only an exegetical interpretation <b>of the Sages.</b>
<b>Rav Pappa said:</b> Rabbi Yishmael <b>counted</b> only cases where the <b><i>halakha</i></b> both <b>supersedes and uproots</b> the straightforward meaning of the verse. <b>This,</b> however, is an instance where the <i>halakha</i> <b>supersedes and adds.</b> The <i>halakha</i> does not overrule the verse but rather adds an additional requirement, i.e., that the whole body must be shaved.
<b>Rav Ashi said: This <i>baraita</i>,</b> which teaches that only certain parts of the body must be shaved, <b>is</b> in accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yishmael, who interprets</b> verses by means of the principle of <b>generalizations and details.</b> According to this interpretation, only collected areas of hair that are visible must be shaven.

Daf 16b

By contrast, the mishna states that the leper must be shaven <b>like a gourd.</b> In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion is this? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva, who interprets</b> verses by means of the principle of <b>amplifications and restrictions. As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: In the verse: <b>“And it shall be on the seventh day, that he shall shave off all his hair,”</b> the phrase “all his hair” is an <b>amplification.</b> The phrase that follows: <b>“His head and his beard and his eyebrows,”</b> is a <b>restriction.</b> In the final phrase: <b>“Even all his hair he shall shave off,”</b> the verse <b>then amplified</b> again. The verse is therefore an instance of <b>amplification and restriction and amplification,</b> which <b>includes everything.</b>
<b>What does it include? It includes</b> the hair <b>of all of the body. What does it</b> nevertheless <b>exclude? It excludes nose hairs,</b> which do not need to be shaved. Since the mishna presents only the opinion of Rabbi Akiva, it poses no challenge to Rabbi Yishmael’s list.
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> conclusion <b>was</b> reached <b>about it,</b> i.e., whether ashes may be used instead of dust for the water of the <i>sota</i>? <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> evidence from that <b>which Rav Huna bar Ashi says</b> that <b>Rav says: If there is no dust</b> available for the <i>sota</i> water, the priest <b>brings decomposed vegetable</b> matter, <b>and he consecrates</b> the water with it. This indicates that it is permitted to substitute other substances for dust.
The Gemara responds: <b>But</b> that <b>is not so. Decomposed vegetable</b> matter <b>is</b> permitted because it will <b>become dust,</b> but <b>ashes will not become dust.</b>
§ The mishna states: He would take loose dust from underneath the tablet and place it into the vessel with the water, <b>so that</b> the dust <b>would be visible upon the water. The Sages taught</b> (<i>Tosefta</i> 1:8): <b>Three</b> items <b>are required to be seen: The dust of the <i>sota</i></b> must be visible in the water, <b>the ashes of the</b> red <b>heifer</b> must be visible when placed in the waters of purification, <b>and the spittle of a woman whose husband, who has a brother, died childless [<i>yevama</i>]</b> must be visible. The <i>yavam</i>, brother-in-law of the <i>yevama</i>, is bound by Torah law to marry her, and this bond is dissolved through the ritual of <i>ḥalitza</i>, in which she spits before him in the presence of judges. <b>In the name of Rabbi Yishmael they said: Even the blood of the bird</b> used in a leper’s purification ritual is required to be visible in the vessel.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reasoning of Rabbi Yishmael? As it is written</b> with regard to the process of the purification of a leper: “And he shall take the cedar wood, and the hyssop, and the scarlet, and the living bird, <b>and dip them in the blood of the slain bird,</b> and in the running water” (Leviticus 14:51). <b>And it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: Had the verse stated only the phrase <b>“in the blood,”</b> one <b>might</b> have thought that these items must be dipped only <b>in</b> the <b>blood and</b> should <b>not</b> be dipped <b>in</b> the <b>water</b> at all. Therefore, <b>the verse</b> also <b>states: “In the</b> running <b>water.” If</b> the verse had stated only the phrase “in the running <b>water,”</b> one <b>might</b> have thought they should be dipped only <b>in</b> the <b>water and not</b> be dipped <b>in</b> the <b>blood</b> at all. Therefore, <b>the verse</b> also <b>states: “In the blood.” How</b> can <b>these</b> texts be reconciled? One must <b>bring</b> little enough <b>water</b> so <b>that the blood of the bird</b> will still <b>be recognizable within it. And how much</b> water is this? It is <b>a quarter-</b><i>log</i>.
<b>And the Rabbis,</b> who do not require that the blood of the bird be visible in the water, how do they understand the verse? <b>That</b> verse is necessary <b>for its own</b> sake, <b>as this is what the Merciful One is saying: Dip</b> the objects both <b>in blood and in water</b> together. The blood need not be visible.
<b>And</b> why does <b>Rabbi Yishmael</b> reject this straightforward understanding of the verse? It is because <b>if</b> this understanding were <b>so,</b> then <b>the Merciful One should have written</b> simply: <b>And dip in them,</b> i.e., dip the cedar wood, hyssop, scarlet wool, and live bird in the blood and water, as the blood and water have already been mentioned beforehand. <b>Why do I</b> need the verse to list explicitly: “And dip them <b>in the blood</b> of the slain bird, <b>and in the</b> running <b>water”?</b> This is <b>to</b> teach that the blood must be <b>recognizable</b> in the water.
<b>And</b> what would <b>the Rabbis</b> respond to this? <b>If the Merciful One had written</b> simply: <b>And dip in them,</b> then <b>I would say</b> the items should be dipped in <b>this</b> liquid <b>separately and</b> in <b>that</b> liquid <b>separately.</b> Therefore, <b>the Merciful One writes:</b> “And dip them <b>in the blood</b> of the slain bird, <b>and in the</b> running <b>water,” to</b> teach that one must <b>mix them</b> together.
<b>And</b> from where does <b>Rabbi Yishmael</b> derive that one must <b>mix them</b> together? <b>Another verse is written: “And he shall slaughter one of the birds</b> in an earthen vessel over running water” (Leviticus 14:50). This indicates that the blood of the bird must fall directly into the water below, and the blood and the water will become mixed together.
<b>And</b> why do <b>the Rabbis</b> not learn it from that verse? <b>If</b> one were to learn it <b>from that</b> verse, <b>I would say</b> that one should <b>slaughter</b> the bird <b>adjacent to the vessel</b> holding the water, <b>and</b> one should <b>grasp the</b> opened <b>veins</b> to ensure that no blood escapes immediately <b>and</b> then <b>collect the blood in a different vessel.</b> The blood and water would therefore be in separate vessels. Therefore, <b>this</b> first verse <b>teaches us</b> that the blood and water must be mixed together.
<b>Rabbi Yirmeya raised a dilemma before Rabbi Zeira:</b> If the bird is <b>big and</b> contains such a large amount of blood that it <b>effaces the water,</b> rendering it indistinguishable, or if the bird is <b>small and</b> contains so little blood that its blood <b>is effaced due to the water</b> and indistinguishable, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>?
Rabbi Zeira <b>said to him: Haven’t I told you not to take yourself out</b> of the bounds <b>of the</b> practical <b><i>halakha</i>?</b> Do not ask questions about impossible eventualities. <b>The Sages measured</b> the ratio of blood to water specifically <b>with regard to a sparrow. There is no</b> sparrow <b>big enough to efface the water, nor is there one small enough to be effaced due to the water.</b>
§ <b>The Rabbis taught</b> (<i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Para</i> 6:6): If one <b>places the dust</b> in the vessel <b>before the water,</b> the mixture is <b>unfit; but Rabbi Shimon deems it fit.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reasoning of Rabbi Shimon?</b>
It is <b>as it is written</b> with regard to the red heifer: <b>“And for the unclean they shall take of the dust of the burning of the purification from sin,</b> and running water shall be put thereto in a vessel” (Numbers 19:17). <b>And it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Shimon said: Is it dust [<i>afar</i>]</b> that is taken? <b>But isn’t it</b> really <b>ashes [<i>efer</i>]?</b> Evidently <b>the Torah altered its usage</b> and referred to ashes as dust <b>in order to derive a verbal analogy from it. Dust is stated</b> in the verse <b>here, and dust is stated there,</b> with regard to the <i>sota</i>. <b>Just as there,</b> with regard to the <i>sota</i>, the verse teaches that the <b>dust</b> must be placed <b>on top of water, so too here,</b> with regard to the red heifer, one learns that the <b>dust,</b> i.e., ashes, must be placed <b>on top of the water.</b>
<b>And</b> likewise, <b>just as here,</b> with regard to the red heifer, if one <b>places the dust</b> in the vessel <b>before the water, it is fit</b> after the fact, <b>so too there,</b> with regard to the <i>sota</i>, if one <b>places the dust</b> in the vessel <b>before the water, it is fit.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And there,</b> with regard to the red heifer, <b>from where do we</b> derive that the mixture is fit even if the dust is placed first? <b>Two phrases are written</b> in the verse. <b>It is written</b> that the water must be put: <b>“Thereto.”</b> Therefore, <b>apparently,</b> the <b>ashes</b> should be placed <b>first. And it is written</b> that the <b>running water</b> must be placed: <b>“In a vessel.” Apparently,</b> the <b>water</b> should be placed in the vessel <b>first,</b> while it is still empty. <b>How</b> can <b>these</b> texts be reconciled? If <b>he desires</b> to place the water first <b>he places it,</b> and if <b>he desires</b> to place the ash first <b>he places it.</b>
<b>And</b> how do <b>the Rabbis,</b> who deem the mixture unfit, interpret the verse? The verse states: <b>“In a vessel,” specifically.</b> The water must be placed first. When the verse states: <b>“Thereto,”</b> it teaches only that it is required <b>to mix</b> the ashes with the water.
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> one could just as easily <b>say</b> the opposite: <b>“Thereto”</b> should be understood <b>specifically,</b> and the ashes must be placed first. The phrase “running water shall be put…<b>in a vessel,”</b> should indicate only <b>that</b> the water <b>must run</b> directly <b>into the vessel</b> and that it may not be brought from the spring by means of another vessel.
The Gemara answers: <b>Just as we find in every instance</b> that the <b>facilitating item</b> goes <b>above</b> the primary item, e.g., in the case of a <i>sota</i> the dust goes on top of the water, <b>so too here,</b> in the case of the red heifer, the <b>facilitating item</b> must go <b>above</b> the primary item. The water must be placed first, and only then the ashes.

Daf 17a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>When</b> the priest <b>comes to write the scroll</b> of the <i>sota</i> that is to be placed in the water, <b>from what place</b> in the Torah passage concerning the <i>sota</i> (Numbers 5:11–31) <b>does he write?</b>
He starts <b>from</b> the verse: <b>“If no man has lain</b> with you, and if you have not gone astray to defilement while under your husband, you shall be free from this water of bitterness that causes the curse” (Numbers 5:19); and continues: <b>“But if you have gone astray while under your husband,</b> and if you are defiled, and some man has lain with you besides your husband” (Numbers 5:20).
<b>And</b> then <b>he does not write</b> the beginning of the following verse, which states: <b>“Then the priest shall cause the woman to swear</b> with the oath of cursing, and the priest shall say to the woman” (Numbers 5:21), <b>but he does write</b> the oath recorded in the continuation of the verse: <b>“The Lord shall make you a curse and an oath</b> among your people when the Lord will cause your thigh to fall away, and your belly to swell. <b>And this water that causes the curse shall go into your bowels, and cause your belly to swell, and your thigh to fall away”</b> (Numbers 5:21–22); <b>but he does not write</b> the conclusion of the verse: <b>“And the woman shall say: Amen, amen”</b> (Numbers 5:22).
<b>Rabbi Yosei says: He does not interrupt</b> the verses but rather writes the entire passage without any omissions.
<b>Rabbi Yehuda says: He writes nothing other</b> than curses recorded in the final verses cited above: <b>“The Lord shall make you a curse and an oath</b> among your people when the Lord will cause your thigh to fall away, and your belly to swell. <b>And this water that causes the curse shall go into your bowels,</b> and cause your belly to swell, and your thigh to fall away.” <b>And he does not write</b> the conclusion of the verse: <b>“And the woman shall say: Amen, amen.”</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>With regard to what</b> issue <b>do</b> the Sages in the mishna <b>disagree?</b> What is the source of their disagreement? <b>They disagree concerning the</b> proper interpretation of the <b>verse: “And the priest shall write these [<i>ha’eleh</i>] curses [<i>et ha’alot</i>] in a scroll”</b> (Numbers 5:23).
<b>Rabbi Meir,</b> the first <i>tanna</i> of the mishna, <b>reasons:</b> The word <b>“<i>alot</i>,”</b> curses, is referring to <b>actual curses.</b> The prefix <i>ha</i>, meaning: The, in the word <b>“<i>ha’alot</i>”</b> serves <b>to include curses that come on account of</b> the <b>blessings,</b> i.e., the curses that are inferred from the phrase: “You shall be free from this water of bitterness that causes the curse” (5:19). The word <b>“<i>eleh</i>,”</b> meaning these, is a limiting term that serves <b>to exclude the</b> long list of <b>curses that are</b> recorded <b>in <i>Mishne Torah</i>,</b> the book of Deuteronomy (chapter 28). Although these curses are also referred to as “<i>alot</i>,” the priest does not write them. The addition of the definite article in the word <b>“<i>ha’eleh</i>”</b> serves <b>to exclude the commands</b> recorded in the <i>sota</i> passage <b>and the acceptances</b> by the word <b>“amen”</b> recorded there as well. The priest need not write these sections of the passage.
<b>And Rabbi Yosei</b> interprets it: <b>It</b> would <b>all</b> be <b>as you,</b> Rabbi Meir, <b>said;</b> however, the additional word <b>“<i>et</i>”</b> in the verse amplifies its scope. It serves <b>to include</b> both <b>commands and acceptances,</b> as they must be written in the scroll as well.
<b>And</b> why does <b>Rabbi Meir</b> disagree? As a rule, he <b>does not interpret</b> the additional word <b><i>et</i></b> as amplifying a verse’s scope.
<b>And</b> as for <b>Rabbi Yehuda, he interprets all of</b> the terms in the verse <b>as exclusionary:</b> The word <b>“<i>alot</i>”</b> is referring specifically to the <b>actual curses</b> recorded in the verses. The definite article in the word <b>“<i>ha’alot</i>”</b> serves <b>to exclude curses that come on account of blessings.</b> The word <b>“<i>eleh</i>”</b> serves <b>to exclude the curses</b> recorded <b>in</b> the <b><i>Mishne Torah</i>.</b> And the definite article in the word <b>“<i>ha’eleh</i>”</b> serves <b>to exclude</b> the <b>commands and acceptances</b> recorded in the verses.
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> according to <b>Rabbi Meir, what is different</b> about <b>this</b> letter <b><i>heh</i></b> at the beginning of the word “<i>ha’alot</i>” such <b>that it amplifies</b> the <i>halakha</i> to include curses that come on account of the blessings, <b>and what is different</b> about <b>that</b> letter <b><i>heh</i></b> in the word “<i>ha’eleh</i>” such <b>that it excludes</b> the commands and acceptances by the word “amen”? Why should one amplify while the other excludes?
The Gemara answers: The letter <b><i>heh</i> when</b> written <b>near an amplifier is an amplifier.</b> The word “<i>alot</i>” itself amplifies the <i>halakha</i>, and the definite article extends that amplification; and a <b><i>heh</i> when</b> written <b>near</b> a <b>restrictor is a restrictor.</b> The word “<i>eleh</i>” itself restricts the <i>halakha</i>, and the definite article before it extends that restriction.
The Gemara asks: <b>But Rabbi Meir does not</b> accept the principle that <b>from a negative</b> statement <b>you</b> can <b>infer a positive</b> statement. What is to be gained by writing the blessings if one cannot infer the curses from them?
<b>Rabbi Tanḥum says: It is written:</b> “If no man has lain with you…<b>you shall be free [<i>hinnaki</i>]”</b> (Numbers 5:19). The word “<i>hinnaki</i>” should be interpreted as if it were in fact <i>ḥinnaki</i>, meaning: You shall choke. When read with the beginning of the next verse, it then forms the sentence: You shall choke… if you have gone astray while under your husband. Therefore, Rabbi Meir understands the blessings themselves to have a dimension of a curse.
§ <b>Rabbi Akiva taught:</b> If <b>a man [<i>ish</i>] and woman [<i>isha</i>] merit</b> reward through a faithful marriage, <b>the Divine Presence</b> rests <b>between them.</b> The words <i>ish</i> and <i>isha</i> are almost identical; the difference between them is the middle letter <i>yod</i> in <i>ish</i>, and the final letter <i>heh</i> in <i>isha</i>. These two letters can be joined to form the name of God spelled <i>yod</i>, <i>heh</i>. But if due to licentiousness <b>they do not merit</b> reward, the Divine Presence departs, leaving in each word only the letters <i>alef</i> and <i>shin</i>, which spell <i>esh</i>, fire. Therefore, <b>fire consumes them.</b>
<b>Rava said: And</b> the fire <b>that</b> consumes <b>the woman is stronger</b> and more immediate <b>than that</b> which consumes <b>the man. What is the reason</b> for this? The letters <i>alef</i> and <i>shin</i> in the word <i>isha</i> <b>are</b> adjacent, <b>joined</b> together, <b>but</b> in the word <i>ish</i> <b>they are not joined,</b> as the letter <i>yod</i> is written between them.
Additionally, <b>Rava says: For what</b> reason <b>did the Torah say: Bring dust for the <i>sota</i>?</b> It is because if <b>she merits</b> to be proven faithful after drinking the water of the <i>sota</i>, <b>a child like our Patriarch Abraham will emerge from her, as it is written with regard to</b> Abraham that he said: “I am but <b>dust and ashes”</b> (Genesis 18:27). But <b>if she does not merit</b> to be proven faithful after drinking the water of the <i>sota</i>, <b>she shall</b> die and <b>return to her dust,</b> the soil from which mankind was formed.
And <b>Rava</b> further <b>taught: As reward</b> for that <b>which our Patriarch Abraham said: “And I am but dust and ashes”</b> (Genesis 18:27), <b>his children merited two mitzvot: The ashes of the</b> red <b>heifer</b> (see Numbers, chapter 19) <b>and the dust of the <i>sota</i>.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But there is also</b> another mitzva involving dust: <b>The dust</b> used for <b>covering the blood</b> of a slaughtered undomesticated animal or fowl (see Leviticus 17:13).
The Gemara answers: <b>There,</b> the dust <b>does</b> serve as an <b>accessory to the mitzva</b> of covering the blood, but <b>there is no benefit</b> imparted by it. It occurs after the animal has been slaughtered and does not itself render the meat fit for consumption.
<b>Rava</b> further <b>taught: As reward</b> for that <b>which our Patriarch Abraham said</b> to the king of Sodom: <b>“That</b> I will not take <b>a thread nor a shoe strap</b> nor anything that is yours” (Genesis 14:23), distancing himself from anything not rightfully his, <b>his children merited two mitzvot: The thread of sky-blue</b> wool worn on ritual fringes <b>and the strap of phylacteries.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Granted, the strap of</b> the <b>phylacteries</b> impart benefit, <b>as it is written: “And all the peoples of the earth shall see that the name of the Lord is called upon you;</b> and they shall be afraid of you” (Deuteronomy 28:10). <b>And it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Eliezer the Great says: This is</b> a reference to <b>the phylacteries of the head,</b> upon which the name of God is written. Phylacteries therefore impart the splendor and grandeur of God and are a fit reward.
<b>But what is</b> the benefit imparted by <b>the thread of sky-blue</b> wool? The Gemara answers: <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Meir would say: What is different about sky-blue from all other colors</b> such that it was specified for the mitzva of ritual fringes?
It is <b>because sky-blue</b> dye <b>is similar</b> in its color <b>to the sea, and the sea is similar to the sky, and the sky is similar to the Throne of Glory, as it is stated: “And they saw the God of Israel; and there was under His feet the like of a paved work of sapphire stone, and the like of the very heaven for clearness”</b> (Exodus 24:10). This verse shows that the heavens are similar to sapphire, <b>and it is written:</b> “And above the firmament that was over their heads was <b>the likeness of a throne, as the appearance of a sapphire stone”</b> (Ezekiel 1:26). Therefore, the throne is similar to the heavens. The color of sky blue dye acts as an indication of the bond between the Jewish people and the Divine Presence.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The priest <b>does not write</b> the scroll of the <i>sota</i> <b>upon</b> a wooden <b>tablet, and not upon paper</b> made from grass, <b>and not upon</b>

Daf 17b

<b><i>diftera</i>,</b> a hide that is only partially processed, as it is salted and treated with flour but not gallnuts; <b>rather,</b> it must be written only <b>on a scroll</b> of parchment, <b>as</b> it <b>is stated:</b> “And the priest shall write these curses <b>in a scroll”</b> (Numbers 5:23).
<b>And</b> the scribe <b>may not write with gum [<i>komos</i>], and not with iron sulfate [<i>kankantom</i>], nor with any substance that makes a mark</b> and cannot be completely erased, <b>but</b> only <b>with ink</b> made from soot, <b>as it is stated</b> in the continuation of the same verse: <b>“And he shall blot</b> them out into the water of bitterness” (Numbers 5:23). This indicates that the scroll must be written with <b>a writing that can be erased</b> in water.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rava says: A scroll of a <i>sota</i> that one wrote at night is unfit. What is the reason</b> for this? It is <b>derived</b> by verbal analogy between one instance of the word <b>“law”</b> and another instance of the word <b>“law.” It is written here,</b> with regard to a <i>sota</i>: <b>“And the priest shall execute upon her all this law”</b> (Numbers 5:30), <b>and it is written there,</b> with regard to judgment: <b>“According to the law, which they shall teach you, and according to the judgment,</b> which they shall tell you” (Deuteronomy 17:11). <b>Just as judgment</b> may be done only <b>by day, so too the scroll of a <i>sota</i></b> may be written only <b>by day.</b>
<b>If one wrote</b> the scroll <b>out of sequence, it is unfit, as it is written: “And</b> the priest <b>shall write these curses</b> in a scroll” (Numbers 5:23). They must be written in the scroll just <b>as they are written</b> in the Torah.
<b>If one wrote</b> the scroll <b>before</b> the <i>sota</i> <b>accepted the oath upon herself,</b> the scroll <b>is unfit, as it is stated: “Then</b> the priest <b>shall cause</b> the woman <b>to swear</b> with the oath of cursing” (Numbers 5:21), <b>and afterward</b> it states: <b>“And</b> the priest <b>shall write</b> these curses in a scroll” (Numbers 5:23).
<b>If one wrote</b> the scroll <b>as a letter,</b> i.e., without first scoring the lines onto the parchment, <b>it is unfit,</b> as <b>the Merciful One states</b> in the Torah: <b>“In a scroll,”</b> indicating that it must be written like a Torah scroll, in which the parchment must be scored.

Daf 18a

<b>If one wrote</b> the scroll <b>on two</b> unattached <b>pages, it is unfit,</b> as <b>the Merciful One states</b> in the Torah: “Scroll,” in the singular. It must be written on <b>one scroll and not</b> on <b>two or three scrolls.</b>
<b>If one wrote one letter and erased</b> that <b>one letter</b> in water, <b>and</b> he then <b>wrote one</b> more <b>letter and erased</b> that <b>one letter, it is unfit, as it is written: “And the priest shall execute upon her all this law”</b> (Numbers 5:30). The entire passage must be written completely and only then erased, all at once.
§ <b>Rava raised a dilemma: If one wrote two scrolls for two</b> separate <b><i>sota</i> women but</b> then <b>erased</b> both of the scrolls <b>in one cup, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Do <b>we require</b> that only the <b>writing</b> be performed <b>for the sake of</b> a specific woman, in which case <b>that is</b> accomplished here? <b>Or perhaps we require</b> that <b>also</b> the <b>erasure</b> be performed <b>for the sake of</b> a specific woman, which is not accomplished here, since both scrolls are erased together?
<b>And if you say</b> that <b>we require</b> that <b>also</b> the <b>erasure</b> be <b>for the sake of</b> each specific woman, then if the priest <b>erased them in two</b> different <b>cups and</b> afterward <b>mixed</b> the water from both together <b>again, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? <b>Do we require</b> that only the <b>erasure</b> be <b>for the sake of</b> a specific woman, in which case <b>that is</b> accomplished here? <b>Or perhaps</b> since <b>this</b> <i>sota</i> <b>does not drink</b> from only <b>her own</b> water <b>and that</b> <i>sota</i> <b>does not drink</b> from only <b>her own</b> water, the water is disqualified?
<b>And</b> furthermore, <b>if you say</b> that the water is disqualified because <b>this one does not drink</b> from only <b>her own</b> water <b>and that one does not drink</b> from only <b>her own</b> water, what if after mixing the two cups of water together the priest <b>divided them again</b> into two cups and gave one to each? <b>What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> then? <b>Is there</b> retroactive <b>clarification,</b> in which case one may claim that each woman drank her own water, <b>or is there no</b> retroactive <b>clarification?</b> The Gemara responds: The dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
<b>Rava raised a dilemma: If</b> the priest <b>administered</b> the bitter water to the <i>sota</i> <b>to drink through</b> a palm <b>fiber, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Or if he administered it <b>through a tube, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? <b>Is this</b> considered <b>a normal manner of drinking, or is it not</b> considered <b>a normal manner of drinking,</b> in which case the act is invalid? The Gemara responds: The dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
<b>Rav Ashi raised a dilemma:</b> If some <b>of the</b> water of the <i>sota</i> <b>spilled</b> out <b>and some of it remained</b> in the cup, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is it sufficient for the woman to drink some of the water in which the scroll has been erased or must she drink all of it? The Gemara responds: The dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
§ <b>Rabbi Zeira says</b> that <b>Rav says:</b> With regard to <b>the two oaths that are stated with regard to the <i>sota</i>:</b> “And the priest shall cause her to swear” (Numbers 5:19), and: “Then the priest shall cause the woman to swear with the oath of cursing” (Numbers 5:21), <b>why</b> are they both necessary? <b>One</b> must be administered <b>before the scroll is erased and one</b> must be administered <b>after it is erased.</b>
<b>Rava objects to this: Both of the</b> oaths <b>are written</b> in the Torah <b>before</b> any mention of <b>the scroll</b> being <b>erased.</b> What is the basis to claim that one oath was administered afterward? <b>Rather, Rava said:</b> While both oaths are administered before the <i>sota</i> drinks, the two oaths are different: <b>One is an oath that has a curse with it, and one is an oath that does not have a curse with it.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> of <b>an oath that has a curse with it?</b> What is the language of this oath? <b>Rav Amram says</b> that <b>Rav says:</b> The priest says: <b>I administer an oath to you</b> that you are honest in your claim <b>that you were not defiled, as, if you were defiled,</b> all these curses <b>will come upon you.</b>
<b>Rava said:</b> This is insufficient, as <b>the curse stands by itself and the oath stands by itself.</b> They are said in separate statements, and it cannot be considered to be an oath with a curse. <b>Rather, Rava said:</b> The priest says: <b>I administer an oath to you that if you were defiled,</b> all these curses <b>will come upon you.</b>
<b>Rav Ashi said:</b> Even this is insufficient, as <b>there is a curse</b> but <b>there is no oath</b> that she was not defiled. <b>Rather, Rav Ashi said:</b> The priest must say: <b>I administer an oath to you that you were not defiled and</b> that <b>if you were defiled</b> all these curses <b>will come upon you.</b> Here the oath itself includes the curse.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>With regard to what does she say: “Amen, amen”</b> (Number 5:22), twice, as recorded in the verse? The mishna explains that it includes of the following: <b>Amen on the curse,</b> as she accepts the curse upon herself if she is guilty, and <b>amen on the oath,</b> as she declares that she is not defiled. She states: <b>Amen</b> if I committed adultery <b>with this man</b> about whom I was warned, <b>amen</b> if I committed adultery <b>with another man. Amen that I did not stray</b> when I was <b>betrothed nor</b> after I was <b>married,</b>

Daf 18b

<b>nor as a widow waiting for my <i>yavam</i></b> to perform levirate marriage, since a woman at that stage is prohibited from engaging in sexual intercourse with any men, <b>nor</b> when <b>married</b> through levirate marriage to the <i>yavam</i>; <b>amen that I did not become defiled, and if I did become defiled, may</b> all these curses <b>come upon me.</b>
<b>Rabbi Meir says</b> that “amen, amen” means: <b>Amen that I did not become defiled</b> in the past, <b>amen that I will not become defiled</b> in the future.
<b>All agree that he may stipulate with her</b> through this oath <b>neither with regard to</b> what she did <b>before becoming be-trothed</b> to him, <b>nor with regard to</b> what she will do <b>after she becomes divorced</b> from him.
Similarly, if a husband divorced his wife, and while divorced <b>she secluded herself</b> with another man <b>and became defiled, and afterward</b> her husband <b>took her back</b> and remarried her, and he then warned her about a specific man, and she secluded herself, and she is now about to drink the water of the <i>sota</i>, <b>he cannot stipulate with her</b> that she take an oath that she did not become defiled during the period in which she was divorced. This is because her husband would become forbidden to her only if she had married another man after being divorced, not if she merely committed an act of promiscuity.
<b>This is the principle:</b> In <b>every</b> case <b>where</b> if <b>she would engage in sexual intercourse</b> with someone else <b>she would not become forbidden to</b> her husband due to this act, <b>he may not stipulate with her</b> that her oath include that act. The oath can include only cases in which she would be rendered forbidden to him.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav Hamnuna says:</b> In the case of <b>a widow awaiting her <i>yavam</i> who committed adultery, she</b> becomes <b>forbidden to her <i>yavam</i>. From where</b> is this derived? It is <b>from</b> the fact <b>that</b> the mishna <b>teaches: Nor as a widow waiting for my <i>yavam</i></b> to perform levirate marriage, <b>nor</b> when <b>married</b> through levirate marriage.
The Gemara explains: <b>Granted, if you say</b> that a woman awaiting levirate marriage who committed adultery <b>is forbidden</b> to the <i>yavam</i>, <b>due to that</b> reason a husband <b>may stipulate with</b> a <i>sota</i> that she take an oath that she did not commit adultery while she was awaiting levirate marriage with him. <b>But if you say that</b> such a woman <b>does not become forbidden, how can</b> a husband <b>stipulate</b> this <b>with her? But didn’t we learn</b> in the mishna that <b>this is the principle:</b> In <b>every</b> case <b>where</b> if <b>she would engage in sexual intercourse</b> with someone else <b>she would not become forbidden to</b> her husband due to this act, <b>he may not stipulate with her</b> that her oath include that act? Clearly she must become forbidden to the <i>yavam</i> if she commits adultery.
<b>They say in the West,</b> Eretz Yisrael: <b>The <i>halakha</i> is not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Hamnuna.</b> A woman awaiting levirate marriage who commits adultery does not become forbidden to the <i>yavam</i>.
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> with regard to <b>that which is taught</b> in the mishna: <b>Nor as a widow waiting for my <i>yavam</i></b> to perform levirate marriage, <b>nor</b> when <b>married</b> through levirate marriage, in accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this? It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva, who says: Betrothal does not take effect</b> even <b>for</b> those who would be merely <b>liable for</b> violating ordinary <b>prohibitions</b> were they to be married. All agree that betrothal does not take effect for forbidden unions that carry the punishment of <i>karet</i>, e.g., unions between siblings. However, according to Rabbi Akiva, in matters of personal status an ordinary Torah prohibition is equivalent to prohibitions that carry the punishment of <i>karet</i>. <b>And</b> he therefore holds that just as a wife who commits adultery, rendering her liable to receive <i>karet</i>, is forbidden to her husband and must be divorced, so too, a widow awaiting her <i>yavam</i> who commits adultery, violat-ing an ordinary Torah prohibition, <b>is considered to be a woman forbidden to</b> her <i>yavam</i>.
<b>Rabbi Yirmeya raised a dilemma:</b> If a man divorced his wife and then remarried her, and she then secluded herself and came to drink the water of a <i>sota</i>, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> as to <b>whether a man</b> in that situation <b>may stipulate</b> with her <b>concerning</b> their <b>first marriage?</b> May he compel her to include in her oath that she did not commit adultery then either? Similarly, may a <i>yavam</i> stipulate with his <i>yevama</i> <b>concerning the marriage of his brother,</b> requiring her to attest that she did not commit adultery prior to the brother’s death? <b>What is</b> the <i>halakha</i>?
The Gemara proposes: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> evidence from the conclusion of the mishna: <b>This is the principle:</b> In <b>every</b> case <b>where</b> if <b>she would engage in sexual intercourse</b> with someone else <b>she would not become forbidden to</b> her husband due to this act, <b>he may not stipulate with her</b> that her oath include that act. One may <b>therefore</b> infer <b>that he may indeed stipulate</b> with her concerning any case in which <b>she</b> would become <b>forbidden.</b> In both of the cases in question the woman would have become forbidden to her husband if she had been guilty. Therefore, the Gemara concludes: Indeed, <b>conclude from</b> the mishna that he is entitled to stipulate in these cases.
§ The mishna states: <b>Rabbi Meir says</b> that “amen, amen” means: <b>Amen that I did not become defiled</b> in the past, amen that I will not become defiled in the future. With regard to this <b>it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i> 2:2): <b>When Rabbi Meir said: Amen that I will not become defiled</b> in the future, he did <b>not</b> mean to say <b>that if</b> God knows that <b>she will become defiled</b> in the future, the <b>water</b> that she drinks now <b>evaluates</b> whether <b>she</b> will be unfaithful and passes judgment on her <b>from now. Rather,</b> he meant <b>that</b> in the event <b>that she becomes defiled</b> in the future, the <b>water</b> that she drinks now will <b>destabilize her and evaluate</b> then whether <b>she</b> was unfaithful.
<b>Rav Ashi raised a dilemma: What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> as to <b>whether a man may stipulate</b> with his wife <b>concerning a later marriage,</b> in the event that he would divorce her and then remarry her? Do we say: <b>Now, at least,</b> if she remains faithful during this marriage, <b>she is not forbidden to him? Or perhaps</b> this includes a later marriage, as <b>sometimes</b> a man <b>divorces</b> his wife <b>and remarries her,</b> and if she then commits adultery she will become forbidden to him.
The Gemara answers: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> evidence from the mishna: <b>All agree that he may stipulate with her</b> through this oath <b>neither with regard to</b> what she did <b>before becoming betrothed</b> to him, <b>nor with regard to</b> what she will do <b>after she becomes divorced</b> from him. And if while divorced she <b>secluded herself</b> with another man <b>and became defiled, and afterward</b> her husband <b>took her back</b> and remarried her, <b>he may not stipulate</b> with her with regard to the period in which she was divorced, since that act does not render her forbidden to him. The Gemara infers: <b>But</b> if <b>he remarries her and she</b> then <b>becomes defiled,</b> she would be forbidden to him. Therefore, <b>he may indeed stipulate</b> with her in advance with regard to this. The Gemara states: Indeed, <b>conclude from</b> the mishna that this is so.
§ <b>The Sages taught:</b> The verse states: <b>“This is the law of jealousy”</b> (Numbers 5:29), indicating that the same law is to be carried out in all cases of jealousy. This <b>teaches that the woman drinks and repeats,</b> i.e., she must drink a second time if she becomes a <i>sota</i> again.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> The word <b>“this”</b> in the verse is a restricting term, indicating <b>that the woman does not drink and repeat. Rabbi Yehuda said:</b> There was <b>an incident</b> in which <b>Neḥunya the ditch digger testified before us</b> in the name of his teachers <b>that the woman drinks and repeats, and we accepted his testimony with regard to two men, but not with regard to one man.</b> Even if she drinks the water of a <i>sota</i> while married to her first husband, she must drink again after violating a warning by her second husband. However, one husband cannot have his wife drink twice.
The <i>baraita</i> concludes: <b>And the Rabbis say: The woman does not drink and repeat, whether with regard to one man or with regard to two men.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> according to <b>the first <i>tanna</i></b> of the <i>baraita</i> <b>as well, isn’t it written</b> in the verse: <b>“This,”</b> restricting the number of times a woman must drink? <b>And</b> according to <b>the Rabbis</b> mentioned <b>later</b> in the <i>baraita</i> <b>as well, isn’t it written: “The law of</b> jealousy,” amplifying the number of times a woman must drink to include all cases of jealousy?
<b>Rava said:</b> Different <i>halakhot</i> apply to different cases: <b>With regard to one husband</b> who accused his wife twice about <b>one paramour, everyone agrees that the woman does not drink and repeat,</b> having been proven innocent once,

Daf 19a

<b>as it is written: “This</b> is the law of jealousy.” The word “this” is a restricting term and excludes that possibility. <b>With regard to two</b> different <b>husbands and two</b> different <b>paramours,</b> where her first husband suspected her with regard to one paramour during her first marriage and the second husband suspected her with regard to a different man during the second marriage, <b>everyone agrees that the woman drinks and repeats, as it is written:</b> “This is <b>the law of</b> jealousy,” in all cases of jealousy.
<b>They disagree when</b> there is <b>one husband and two paramours,</b> i.e., where one husband warned her with regard to a second paramour after she survived her first ordeal. They also disagree <b>in</b> a case of <b>two husbands and one paramour,</b> i.e., if her second husband accused her with regard to the same paramour on account of whom she was compelled to drink by her first husband.
The opinions are justified as follows: <b>The first <i>tanna</i> holds</b> that the phrase <b>“the law of</b> jealousy” serves <b>to include all of these</b> cases. In almost all cases the woman drinks and repeats. The word <b>“this”</b> serves <b>to exclude</b> only the case of <b>one husband and one paramour,</b> in which she does not drink and repeat.
<b>And the Rabbis</b> mentioned <b>later</b> in the <i>baraita</i> <b>hold</b> that the word <b>“this”</b> serves <b>to exclude all of these</b> cases. The woman almost never drinks and repeats. The phrase <b>“the law of</b> jealousy” serves <b>to include</b> only the case of <b>two husbands and two paramours,</b> in which she does drink and repeat.
<b>And Rabbi Yehuda</b> holds: The word <b>“this”</b> serves <b>to exclude two</b> of the cases, and the phrase <b>“the law of</b> jealousy” serves <b>to include two.</b> The word <b>“this”</b> serves <b>to exclude</b> the <b>two</b> cases of <b>one husband and one paramour</b> and <b>one husband and two paramours.</b> In neither of these cases does the woman drink and repeat. The phrase <b>“the law of</b> jealousy” serves <b>to include two</b> cases, i.e., <b>two husbands and one paramour,</b> and all the more so <b>two husbands and two paramours.</b> In both of these cases, the woman must drink and repeat.

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>He would take her meal-offering out of</b> the <b>Egyptian wicker basket</b> made of palm leaves in which it was lying <b>and would put it into a service vessel and</b> then <b>place it on her hand. And</b> the <b>priest</b> would then <b>place his hand underneath hers and wave it</b> together with her. The priest <b>waved</b> it <b>and brought</b> it <b>near</b> to the southwest corner of the altar, <b>removed a handful</b> from it, <b>and burned</b> the handful; <b>and the remainder was eaten by the priests.</b>
The priest <b>would force</b> the woman <b>to drink</b> the bitter water of a <i>sota</i>, <b>and afterward he would sacrifice her meal-offering. Rabbi Shimon says:</b> The priest would <b>sacrifice her meal-offering and afterward he would force her to drink, as it is stated:</b> “And the priest shall take a handful of the meal-offering, as the memorial part of it, and burn it upon the altar, <b>and afterward he shall make the woman drink the water”</b> (Numbers 5:26). But Rabbi Shimon concedes that <b>if</b> the priest first <b>forced her to drink and afterward sacrificed her meal-offering,</b> it is still <b>valid.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rabbi Elazar said to Rabbi Yoshiya of his generation,</b> i.e., his contemporary: <b>You shall not sit on your feet until you explain this matter</b> to me: <b>From where</b> is it derived that <b>the meal-offering of a <i>sota</i> requires waving?</b> The Gemara expresses surprise at the question: <b>From where do we</b> derive this? It is explicitly <b>written with regard to</b> the meal-offering of a <i>sota</i>: “And the priest shall take the meal-offering of jealousy out of the woman’s hand, <b>and shall wave</b> the meal-offering before the Lord, and bring it unto the altar” (Numbers 5:25). Rather, the question is as follows: <b>From where do we</b> derive that the waving is performed <b>by the owner,</b> i.e., the woman, and not only by the priest?
Rabbi Yoshiya answered: This is <b>derived</b> by means of a verbal analogy between the term <b>“hand”</b> written here and <b>“hand” from</b> the <b>peace-offering:</b> It <b>is written here,</b> with regard to the meal-offering of a <i>sota</i>: <b>“And the priest shall take</b> the meal-offering of jealousy <b>out of the woman’s hand”</b> (Numbers 5:25), <b>and</b> it <b>is written there,</b> with regard to the peace-offering: “He that offers his peace-offerings unto the Lord…<b>His own hands shall bring</b> the offerings…that the breast may be waved before the Lord” (Leviticus 7:29–30).
<b>Just as here,</b> in the case of the <i>sota</i>, the <b>priest</b> waves the offering, <b>so too there,</b> in the case of the peace-offering, the <b>priest</b> waves the offering. <b>And just as there,</b> in the case of the peace-offering, the <b>owner</b> waves the offering, <b>so too here,</b> in the case of the <i>sota</i>, the <b>owner</b> waves the offering. <b>How</b> is <b>this</b> accomplished? The priest <b>places his hand beneath the hands of the owner and</b> then <b>waves</b> the offering with the owner.
§ The mishna states: The priest <b>waved</b> it <b>and brought</b> it <b>near</b> to the southwest corner of the altar, <b>removed a handful</b> from it, and burned the handful. Yet the continuation of the mishna states: The priest <b>would force</b> the woman <b>to drink, and afterward he would sacrifice her meal-offering.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Didn’t</b> the mishna state in the previous phrase that the offering <b>was</b> already <b>sacrificed?</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>This is what</b> the mishna <b>is saying: What was the</b> sacrificial <b>order of meal-offerings</b> in general? The priest <b>waved</b> the meal-offering <b>and brought</b> it <b>near</b> to the southwest corner of the altar, <b>removed a handful</b> from it, <b>and burned</b> the handful, <b>and the remainder was eaten by the priests.</b>
<b>And</b> as <b>for</b> the correct order for sacrificing the meal-offering of the <i>sota</i> and <b>forcing</b> her <b>to drink,</b> this <b>itself</b> is a matter about which <b>Rabbi Shimon and the Rabbis disagree, as the Rabbis hold</b> that the priest would <b>force</b> the woman <b>to drink and afterward he</b> would <b>sacrifice her meal-offering; and Rabbi Shimon holds</b> that the priest would <b>sacrifice her meal-offering and afterward he</b> would <b>force her to drink, as</b> it <b>is stated:</b> “And the priest shall take a handful of the meal-offering, as the memorial part of it, and burn it upon the altar, <b>and afterward he shall make</b> the woman <b>drink</b> the water” (Numbers 5:26).
§ The mishna states: <b>But</b> Rabbi Shimon concedes that <b>if</b> the priest first <b>forced her to drink and afterward sacrificed her meal-offering,</b> the offering is still <b>valid.</b>

Daf 19b

<b>The Sages taught: What</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states</b> after the sacrifice of the meal-offering: <b>“And he shall make her drink</b> the water” (Numbers 5:27)? <b>But isn’t it already stated: “And he shall make</b> the woman <b>drink</b> the water of bitterness that causes the curse” (Numbers 5:24)? The <i>baraita</i> answers: The repetition teaches <b>that if the scroll was</b> already <b>erased and</b> then the woman <b>says: I will not drink, she is forced [<i>me’arerin</i>] to drink against her will.</b> This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Akiva.</b>
<b>Rabbi Shimon says: What</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states:</b> “And the priest shall take a handful of the meal-offering, as the memorial part of it, and burn it upon the altar, <b>and afterward he shall make</b> the woman <b>drink</b> the water” (Numbers 5:26)? <b>But isn’t it already stated</b> previously: <b>“And he shall make</b> the woman <b>drink”</b> (Numbers 5:24)? <b>Rather,</b> this verse indicates that the <i>sota</i> is given the bitter water to drink only <b>after all the actions that are stated above</b> are performed, i.e., erasing the scroll, sacrificing the meal-offering, and administering the oath. Therefore, this verse <b>teaches</b> that <b>three matters preclude her</b> from drinking: She does not drink <b>until the handful is sacrificed, and until the scroll is erased, and until she accepts the oath upon herself.</b>
The Gemara elaborates: She does not drink <b>until the handful is sacrificed. Rabbi Shimon</b> conforms <b>to his</b> line of <b>reasoning</b> stated earlier, <b>as he says</b> that the priest <b>sacrifices her meal-offering and afterward forces her to drink.</b>
The Gemara questions the second condition: She does not drink <b>until the scroll is erased.</b> Why does the <i>baraita</i> need to state this? <b>But what</b> could <b>he give her to drink</b> if the scroll was not yet erased into the water? <b>Rav Ashi says: No,</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>is necessary for</b> an instance <b>where</b> the scroll was erased, but <b>the impression</b> of the ink <b>is</b> still <b>discernible</b> on the parchment. The woman does not drink until the scroll is totally erased.
The Gemara discusses the third condition: She does not drink <b>until she accepts the oath upon herself.</b> One might infer from this statement that <b>it is</b> only <b>that</b> she <b>does not drink</b> before she accepts the oath; <b>however,</b> the scroll <b>is written for her</b> before she accepts the oath. <b>But didn’t Rava say:</b> With regard to <b>a scroll of a <i>sota</i> that was written before she accepted the oath upon herself,</b> whoever wrote it <b>did nothing,</b> and the scroll is rendered invalid. The Gemara responds: <b>This was cited for no reason,</b> as in fact the scroll is not even written before she accepts the oath upon herself, and nothing should be inferred.
The Gemara asks: <b>With regard to what do</b> the Rabbis and Rabbi Shimon <b>disagree</b> in the mishna? The Gemara answers: <b>Three verses are written</b> which pertain to drinking the bitter water: <b>The first</b> occurrence of the term is in the verse: <b>“And he shall make</b> the woman <b>drink”</b> (Numbers 5:24); the second: <b>“And afterward he shall make</b> the woman <b>drink</b> the water” (Numbers 5:26); and <b>the last</b> occurrence of the term is in the verse: <b>“And he shall make her drink”</b> (Numbers 5:27).
<b>The Rabbis hold</b> that <b>the first</b> occurrence of the term: <b>“And he shall make</b> the woman <b>drink,”</b> is written <b>to</b> teach the <i>halakha</i> <b>itself,</b> i.e., <b>that</b> the priest first <b>forces her to drink and afterward sacrifices her meal-offering.</b> The second instance: <b>“And afterward he shall make</b> the woman <b>drink,” is necessary</b> to teach <b>that</b> as long as <b>the impression</b> of the writing <b>is</b> still <b>discernible,</b> the <i>sota</i> is not given the bitter water to drink. The third verse, <b>the last</b> occurrence of the term: <b>“And he shall make her drink,”</b> teaches <b>that if the scroll was erased and</b> then the woman <b>says: I will not drink, she is forced to drink against her will.</b>
<b>And Rabbi Shimon holds</b> that the second verse: <b>“And afterward he shall make</b> the woman <b>drink</b> the water” (Numbers 5:26), is written <b>to</b> teach the <i>halakha</i> <b>itself,</b> i.e., <b>that</b> the priest first <b>sacrifices her meal-offering and afterward forces her to drink. The first</b> occurrence of the term: <b>“And he shall make</b> the woman <b>drink,”</b> teaches <b>that if he forced her to drink and</b> only <b>afterward sacrificed her meal-offering,</b> the offering <b>is</b> nevertheless <b>valid. The last</b> occurrence of the term: <b>“And he shall make her drink,”</b> teaches <b>that if</b> the <b>scroll was erased and</b> then <b>she said: I will not drink, she is forced to drink against her will.</b>
The Gemara explains the Rabbis’ opinion: <b>And the Rabbis</b> would respond to Rabbi Shimon that the <b>verse does not begin</b> the discussion <b>with</b> a <i>halakha</i> that is applicable only <b>after the fact,</b> and therefore the initial mention of the drinking is referring to the proper time for the ritual.
The Gemara asks: <b>But does Rabbi Akiva</b> in fact <b>hold</b> that the woman is <b>forced to drink against her will? But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i> 2:3) that <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: A hook [<i>kelabus</i>]</b> made <b>of iron is</b> forcibly <b>placed into her mouth,</b> so <b>that if the scroll was erased and she said: I will not drink, she is forced to drink against her will. Rabbi Akiva said:</b> It is not necessary to force her to drink. <b>Don’t we need</b> to force her to drink the water <b>only</b> in order <b>to evaluate her</b> fidelity? <b>And isn’t she established as</b> having been <b>evaluated</b> when she refuses to drink, as she is essentially admitting her guilt? <b>Rather,</b> Rabbi Akiva’s statement should be understood as follows: <b>Until the handful is sacrificed she can retract</b> her decision to drink the bitter water; however, <b>once the handful is sacrificed she cannot retract</b> her decision to drink.
The Gemara asks: <b>But according to your reasoning</b> in explanation of Rabbi Akiva’s statement, <b>this</b> explanation <b>itself should pose a difficulty for you.</b> Why <b>can’t she retract</b> her decision <b>once the handful is sacrificed? And isn’t she established as</b> having been <b>evaluated</b> when she refuses to drink?
The Gemara answers: This is <b>not difficult; this</b> case, where she is forced to drink, is referring to a situation <b>where she retracts</b> her decision to drink <b>due to fear,</b> as her refusal is not viewed as an admission of guilt, and it is possible that if she drinks she will be found undefiled. <b>And that</b> case, where she does not drink, is referring to a situation <b>where she retracts</b> her decision <b>in a state of</b> good <b>health.</b> Since she does not appear to be afraid, her refusal is viewed as an admission of guilt.
<b>And this is what</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>is saying:</b> In <b>any</b> case where she retracts her decision to drink <b>in a state of</b> good <b>health, she does not drink at all.</b> With regard to a <i>sota</i> who retracts her decision <b>due to fear,</b> if she retracts her decision <b>before the handful is sacrificed, when the scroll has not yet been erased; or</b> even if the <b>scroll was</b> already <b>erased,</b> since <b>the priests acted incorrectly when they erased</b> it beforehand; <b>she can retract</b> her decision. <b>Once the handful is sacrificed, in which</b> case the <b>priests acted correctly when they erased</b> the scroll, <b>she cannot retract</b> her decision, and she is forced to drink against her will.

Daf 20a

The Gemara asks: The statement <b>of Rabbi Akiva</b> is <b>difficult,</b> as it is contradicted <b>by</b> another statement <b>of Rabbi Akiva: There,</b> in the first <i>baraita</i>, <b>he said</b> that <b>erasure prevents</b> the authorities from compelling the woman to drink the water if she retracted her decision to drink, <b>and here he says</b> that the sacrifice of the <b>handful prevents</b> the authorities from compelling the woman to drink the water. In other words, according to the first <i>baraita</i> the woman can retract her decision to drink until the scroll is erased, whereas according to the second <i>baraita</i> she can retract her decision until the handful is sacrificed.
The Gemara responds: There is a dispute between <b>two <i>tanna’im</i>, and</b> they disagree <b>with regard to</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Akiva.</b> They disagree with regard to what point in time, according to Rabbi Akiva, is the final moment at which a woman can refuse to drink the bitter water without being forced to do so.
<b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: If <b>she</b> initially <b>said: I will not drink, while in a state of</b> good <b>health, and</b> then <b>she retracted</b> her statement <b>and said: I will drink, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Does one say that <b>when she said: I will not drink,</b> it is as if <b>she</b> confessed and <b>said: I am defiled, and since she established herself as defiled she cannot retract</b> her statement? <b>Or perhaps when she said: I will drink, she revealed her thoughts that it</b> was only <b>due to fear that she said</b> she will not drink? The Gemara concludes that the dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
<b>Shmuel’s father says:</b> It is <b>necessary</b> for one <b>to put a bitter</b> substance <b>into the water</b> that the <i>sota</i> drinks. <b>What is the reason</b> for this? It is <b>as the verse states:</b> “And he shall blot them out into <b>the water of bitterness”</b> (Numbers 5:23), indicating <b>that</b> they <b>are already bitter</b> before the scroll is erased.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If <b>before the scroll was erased she said: I will not drink, the scroll</b> that was written for <b>her is sequestered, and her meal-offering is</b> burned and <b>scattered over</b> the place of <b>the ashes, and her scroll is not fit to give</b> to <b>another <i>sota</i> to drink.</b> If <b>the scroll was erased and</b> afterward <b>she said: I am defiled, the water is poured out, and her meal-offering is scattered in the place of the ashes.</b> If <b>the scroll was</b> already <b>erased and she said: I will not drink, she is forced to drink against her will.</b>
When a guilty woman drinks <b>she does not manage to</b> finish <b>drinking before her face turns green and her eyes bulge, and her</b> skin becomes <b>full of</b> protruding <b>veins, and</b> the people standing in the Temple <b>say: Remove her,</b> so <b>that she does not render the Temple courtyard impure</b> by dying there.
The mishna limits the scope of the previous statement: <b>If she has merit, it delays</b> punishment <b>for her</b> and she does not die immediately. <b>There is a merit</b> that <b>delays</b> punishment for <b>one year, there is</b> a larger <b>merit</b> that <b>delays</b> punishment for <b>two years,</b> and <b>there is a merit</b> that <b>delays</b> punishment for <b>three years. From here Ben Azzai states: A person is obligated to teach his daughter Torah,</b> so <b>that if she drinks</b> and does not die immediately, <b>she will know that</b> some <b>merit</b> she has <b>delayed</b> punishment <b>for her. Rabbi Eliezer says: Anyone who teaches his daughter Torah is teaching her promiscuity [<i>tiflut</i>].</b>
<b>Rabbi Yehoshua says: A woman desires to</b> receive the amount of <b>a <i>kav</i></b> of food <b>and a sexual relationship [<i>tiflut</i>]</b> rather <b>than</b> to receive <b>nine <i>kav</i></b> of food <b>and abstinence. He would say: A foolish man of piety, and a conniving wicked person, and an abstinent woman [<i>perusha</i>], and those who injure</b> themselves out of false <b>abstinence;</b> all <b>these are</b> people <b>who erode the world.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Shmuel says in the name of Rabbi Meir: When I was studying Torah before Rabbi Akiva,</b> as his disciple, <b>I used to put iron sulfate into the ink</b> with which I wrote Torah scrolls, <b>and he did not say anything to me</b> in protest. Afterward, <b>when I came</b> to learn Torah <b>before Rabbi Yishmael, he said to me: My son, what is your vocation? I said to him: I am a scribe [<i>lavlar</i>]</b> who writes Torah scrolls. <b>He said to me: My son, be careful</b> in your work, <b>as your work is the work of Heaven, lest you omit a single letter</b> from the Torah scroll <b>or add a single letter,</b> and in this <b>you are found to be destroying the entire world</b> if the mistake alters the meaning of the verse and results in blasphemy.
Rabbi Meir continues: <b>I said to</b> Rabbi Yishmael: <b>I have one substance that I put into the ink, and it is called iron sulfate,</b> which prevents the writing from being erased. <b>He said to me: And may iron sulfate be put into the ink? The Torah</b> clearly <b>said</b> with regard to the scroll of the <i>sota</i>: “And the priest shall write these curses in a scroll, <b>and he shall blot</b> them out into the water of bitterness” (Numbers 5:23). This indicates that the Torah requires <b>writing that can be blotted out.</b>
Since Rabbi Meir’s remark about iron sulfate seems unrelated to Rabbi Yishmael’s previous statement, the Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> Rabbi Yishmael <b>saying to</b> Rabbi Meir, <b>and what is</b> Rabbi Meir <b>replying to</b> Rabbi Yishmael?
The Gemara explains: <b>This is what</b> Rabbi Meir <b>is saying to him: It is not necessary</b> to say that I do not err <b>in omissions and additions, as I am an expert. Rather,</b> there is not <b>even</b> any reason <b>for concern with regard to a fly lest</b> it <b>come and sit on the protrusion of</b> the letter <b><i>dalet</i> and erase it,</b> thereby <b>rendering it</b> the letter <b><i>reish</i>,</b> which could be a critical error. There is no concern of this erasure occurring, since <b>I have a certain substance that I put into the ink</b> and that prevents the writing from being erased, <b>and it is called iron sulfate.</b>
The Gemara questions the initial part of Rabbi Meir’s statement: <b>Is that so? But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Meir said: When I was studying Torah before Rabbi Yishmael, I used to put iron sulfate into the ink</b> with which I wrote Torah scrolls, <b>and he did not say anything to me.</b> Afterward, <b>when I came</b> to learn Torah <b>with Rabbi Akiva, he prohibited me</b> from doing <b>so.</b>
The Gemara points out that there are two separate contradictions between the two statements: Rav Yehuda’s statement with regard to Rabbi Meir first <b>serving</b> Rabbi Akiva as a disciple is <b>difficult,</b> as it is contradicted <b>by</b> the statement of the <i>baraita</i> with regard to his first <b>serving</b> Rabbi Yishmael. Furthermore, Rav Yehuda’s statement is <b>difficult,</b> since he states that it was Rabbi Yishmael <b>who prohibited</b> the addition of iron sulfate, and this is contradicted <b>by</b> the statement of the <i>baraita</i> that it was Rabbi Akiva <b>who prohibited it.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>Granted,</b> the apparent contradiction between Rav Yehuda’s statement with regard to Rabbi Meir’s <b>serving</b> Rabbi Akiva first, and the statement of the <i>baraita</i> with regard to <b>serving</b> Rabbi Yishmael first, poses <b>no difficulty. Initially, he came before Rabbi Akiva</b> to study, but <b>since he could not comprehend his</b> extremely complicated <b>method</b> of learning, <b>he came before Rabbi Yishmael and learned</b> the oral <b>tradition</b> from him. Afterward, <b>he returned</b> and <b>came before Rabbi Akiva</b> and <b>studied</b> his method of logical <b>reasoning</b> in order to understand the reasons behind the <i>halakhot</i> he had already learned.
<b>However,</b> the contradiction between Rav Yehuda’s statement that it was Rabbi Yishmael <b>who prohibited</b> the addition of iron sulfate and the statement of the <i>baraita</i> that it was Rabbi Akiva <b>who prohibited it</b> still poses a <b>difficulty.</b> The Gemara concludes: Indeed, the matter is <b>difficult.</b>
<b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Yehuda says</b> that <b>Rabbi Meir would say: Iron sulfate may be put into the ink</b> that is used <b>for all</b> sacred writings, i.e., Torah scrolls, phylacteries, and <i>mezuzot</i>,

Daf 20b

<b>except for</b> the ink used to write the Torah <b>passage about the <i>sota</i>,</b> even when written in a Torah scroll. <b>Rabbi Ya’akov says in the name of</b> Rabbi Meir: It is <b>except for</b> the ink used to write the scroll with the <b><i>sota</i> passage</b> used <b>in the Temple.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the difference <b>between these</b> two opinions? The Gemara replies: <b>Rabbi Yirmeya says</b> that there is a difference <b>between them</b> with regard to whether it is permitted <b>to erase</b> the passage <b>for</b> the <i>sota</i> <b>from a Torah</b> scroll. According to Rabbi Yehuda, Rabbi Meir holds that this is permitted, and therefore the passage in the Torah scroll must be written with ink that does not contain iron sulfate, so that it can be erased. By contrast, according to Rabbi Ya’akov, Rabbi Meir holds that it is prohibited to erase the passage from a Torah scroll, and therefore the passage may be written with ink containing iron sulfate.
The Gemara assumes that according to Rabbi Ya’akov it is prohibited to erase the passage from a Torah scroll, since he holds that the scroll must be written for the sake of the <i>sota</i>, whereas Rabbi Yehuda, who permits this, holds that the scroll need not be written for the sake of the <i>sota</i>. <b>And</b> therefore, the opinions of <b>these <i>tanna’im</i> are parallel</b> to the opinions of <b>those <i>tanna’im</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>The scroll</b> of one <i>sota</i> <b>is not fit</b> to be used in the preparation of the water <b>to give</b> to <b>another <i>sota</i> to drink,</b> as it was not written for the sake of the other <i>sota</i>. <b>Rabbi Aḥai bar Yoshiya says: Her scroll is fit</b> to be used in the preparation of the water <b>to give</b> to <b>another <i>sota</i> to drink,</b> since it does not need to be written for the sake of the <i>sota</i>.
<b>Rav Pappa said: Perhaps</b> that <b>is not so,</b> and the two disputes are not comparable.
