The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> to Rav Naḥman’s statement from a <i>baraita</i> in the <i>Tosefta</i> (5:4): With regard to <b>one who issues a warning to his betrothed, or to his</b> <i>yevama</i> while she is a <b>widow awaiting her <i>yavam</i>, if she secluded herself</b> with the other man <b>before</b> her husband <b>consummated</b> the marriage, <b>she neither drinks</b> the bitter water <b>nor collects</b> payment of <b>her marriage contract.</b>

Daf 26a

The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>A woman who was pregnant</b> with the child <b>of another</b> man at the time of her marriage <b>and a woman who was nursing</b> the child <b>of another</b> man at the time of her marriage <b>neither drink</b> the bitter water <b>nor collect</b> payment of their <b>marriage contracts,</b> as their marriages were prohibited by rabbinic law. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues: The reason for this is <b>as Rabbi Meir would say: A man may not marry a woman who is pregnant</b> with the child <b>of another</b> man <b>or a woman who is nursing</b> the child <b>of another</b> man, until twenty-four months pass after the baby’s birth, so as to ensure that the woman will not become pregnant while the child needs to nurse. <b>And if he married</b> her, <b>he must divorce</b> her <b>and may never remarry</b> her, as the Sages penalized him for transgressing the prohibition. <b>And the Rabbis say: He must divorce</b> her, <b>and when his time to marry</b> her <b>arrives,</b> i.e., twenty-four months after the baby’s birth, <b>he can marry</b> her again.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: In <b>the</b> case of a <b>young man who married a barren woman or an elderly woman, and he did not have a wife and children beforehand,</b> the woman <b>neither drinks nor collects</b> payment of her <b>marriage contract,</b> as it is prohibited for him to marry a woman with whom he cannot procreate. <b>Rabbi Elazar says:</b> This marriage is not forbidden, as <b>he can marry another</b> woman <b>and procreate through her,</b> and therefore she can drink the bitter water.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>However,</b> in the case of <b>one who issued a warning to his betrothed, or to his</b> <i>yevama</i> while she was a <b>widow awaiting her <i>yavam</i>, and she secluded herself</b> with the other man <b>after he consummated</b> the marriage, <b>she either drinks</b> the bitter water <b>or does not collect</b> payment of her <b>marriage contract.</b> If <b>his own pregnant or nursing</b> wife becomes a <i>sota</i>, then despite the concern that the bitter water may harm the fetus, she <b>either drinks</b> the bitter water <b>or does not collect</b> payment of <b>her marriage contract.</b> In <b>the</b> case of a <b>young man who married a barren woman or an elderly woman, and he</b> already <b>had a wife and children</b> and was therefore permitted to marry his barren or elderly wife, the woman <b>either drinks</b> the bitter water <b>or does not collect</b> payment of her <b>marriage contract.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> concludes: With regard to <b>the wife of a <i>mamzer</i></b> who is married <b>to a <i>mamzer</i></b> in a permitted marriage, <b>and the wife of a Gibeonite</b> who is married <b>to a Gibeonite</b> in a permitted marriage, <b>and the wife of a convert or an emancipated slave, and a sexually underdeveloped woman,</b> if any of these women becomes a <i>sota</i> she <b>either drinks</b> the bitter water <b>or does not collect</b> payment of her <b>marriage contract,</b> as the marriage is permitted. After citing the entire <i>baraita</i>, the Gemara explains the difficulty: <b>In any event,</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches</b> that <b>a sexually underdeveloped woman</b> can drink the bitter water if the marriage is permitted, and this is <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Rav Naḥman.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>Rav Naḥman</b> could have <b>said to you:</b> There <b>is</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i></b> with regard to this matter, <b>and I state</b> my opinion <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>this <i>tanna</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Shimon Ben Elazar says: A sexually underdeveloped woman neither drinks nor collects</b> payment of her <b>marriage contract, as it is stated: “And she shall be cleared, and shall conceive seed”</b> (Numbers 5:28), indicating that the <i>sota</i> ritual pertains only to <b>one whose way is to bear seed</b> and give birth, <b>excluding this</b> sexually underdeveloped woman, <b>whose way is not to bear seed.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> as for <b>the Rabbis, what do they do with this</b> verse: <b>“And she shall be cleared, and shall conceive seed”?</b> Since they hold that a sexually underdeveloped woman drinks the bitter water, what do they derive from the verse? The Gemara answers: <b>They require</b> it <b>for that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse: <b>“And she shall be cleared, and shall conceive seed”</b> (Numbers 5:28), indicates <b>that if she was barren, she will be remembered</b> and conceive a child; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Akiva. Rabbi Yishmael said to him: If so, all the barren women will seclude themselves</b> with other men, <b>and they will be remembered</b> and conceive after drinking the bitter water and being found innocent; <b>but that</b> virtuous barren woman, who does not transgress the prohibition of seclusion, <b>since she does not seclude herself</b> with other men, <b>she loses</b> the opportunity to receive this blessing.
Rabbi Yishmael continues: <b>If so, what</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states: “And she shall be cleared, and shall conceive seed”</b> (Numbers 5:28)? This means <b>that if</b> in the past she <b>would give birth in pain,</b> from then on <b>she will give birth with ease;</b> if she gave birth to <b>females, she will</b> now <b>give birth to males;</b> if her children were <b>short, she will</b> now <b>give birth to tall</b> children; if her children were <b>black, she will give birth to white</b> children.
§ The <i>baraita</i> in the <i>Tosefta</i> cited above states: <b>The wife of a <i>mamzer</i></b> who is married <b>to a <i>mamzer</i></b> in a permitted marriage… either drinks the bitter water or does not collect payment of her marriage contract. The Gemara asks: <b>Isn’t</b> that <b>obvious?</b> Since their marriage is permitted, why should the <i>sota</i> ritual not apply? The Gemara answers: It is necessary <b>lest you say</b> that she should not drink, since if she drinks and is found to be innocent of adultery, she is permitted to her husband. This is undesirable since their offspring are also <i>mamzerim</i>, and <b>we do not cause</b> the number of individuals <b>of flawed lineage to proliferate.</b> The <i>baraita</i> in the <i>Tosefta</i> therefore <b>teaches us</b> that this is not a concern, and the wife of a <i>mamzer</i> is permitted to drink.
The aforementioned <i>baraita</i> in the <i>Tosefta</i> states: <b>The wife of a convert or an emancipated slave, and a sexually underdeveloped woman</b> can drink the bitter water. The Gemara asks with regard to the wife of a convert or an emancipated slave, who also has the status of a convert: <b>Isn’t</b> that <b>obvious?</b> Since their marriage is permitted, why should the <i>sota</i> ritual not apply?
The Gemara answers: It is necessary <b>lest you say</b> that she does not drink, as the verse states: <b>“Speak to the children of Israel,</b> and say unto them: If the wife of any man goes astray, and acts unfaithfully against him” (Numbers 5:12). One might have inferred from this verse that the <i>sota</i> ritual applies only to those born as Jews <b>and not</b> to <b>converts;</b> the <i>baraita</i> in the <i>Tosefta</i> therefore <b>teaches us</b> that this is not so. The Gemara asks: Why not <b>say</b> that <b>indeed</b> the verse excludes converts? The Gemara answers: The subsequent term: <b>“And say</b> unto them” (Numbers 5:12) <b>is an amplification,</b> which serves to include converts.
§ The mishna states: <b>The wife of a priest drinks</b> the bitter water, and if she is found to be innocent of adultery she is permitted to her husband. The Gemara asks: Why does the mishna state: <b>The wife of a priest drinks? Isn’t</b> that <b>obvious?</b> The Gemara answers: It is necessary <b>lest you say</b> that she does not drink, as the verse states: “And a man lay with <b>her…neither was she seized”</b> (Numbers 5:13). This indicates that if the <i>sota</i> was not seized <b>she is forbidden; however,</b> if she was <b>seized,</b> i.e., raped, she is <b>permitted</b> to her husband. <b>And</b> with regard to <b>this</b> woman, the wife of a priest, <b>since</b> even if she was <b>seized</b> she is <b>forbidden</b> to her husband, as a priest may not remain married to his wife if she was raped while they were married, one might <b>say</b> that the <i>sota</i> ritual does not apply to her, and she <b>does not drink.</b> Therefore, the mishna <b>teaches us</b> that she does drink.
§ The mishna states: The wife of a priest drinks, <b>and</b> if she is found to be innocent of adultery, <b>she is permitted to her husband.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Isn’t</b> that <b>obvious? Rav Huna says:</b> The mishna is referring to a case <b>where</b> the woman’s health <b>deteriorates</b> after she drinks the bitter water, and one might have thought that she is defiled. The Gemara asks: In the case of a woman whose health <b>deteriorates, hasn’t the</b> bitter <b>water</b> already <b>evaluated</b> that <b>she</b> was unfaithful? The fact that her health deteriorates indicates that she is defiled and forbidden to her husband, and her death is delayed due to her merit in other matters.
The Gemara answers: The mishna is referring to a case <b>where her</b> health <b>deteriorates,</b> but not in the manner of a <i>sota</i>, who is afflicted in her belly and thighs (see Numbers 5:27). Rather, she is afflicted <b>by way of</b> other <b>limbs. Lest you say: This</b> woman <b>engaged in licentious intercourse, and</b> the fact <b>that the</b> bitter <b>water did not evaluate her in</b> the <b>usual manner</b> is <b>because she engaged in licentious intercourse under duress, and with regard to a priest,</b> even rape <b>renders her forbidden to her</b> to her husband, the mishna therefore <b>teaches us</b> that the woman’s deteriorating health does not indicate anything.
§ The mishna states: <b>The wife of a eunuch drinks.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Isn’t</b> that <b>obvious?</b> Since their marriage is permitted, why should the <i>sota</i> ritual not apply? The Gemara replies: It is necessary <b>lest you say</b> that she does not drink, since <b>the Merciful One states</b> with regard to the <i>sota</i>: “But if you have gone astray while under your husband, and if you are defiled, and some man has lain with you <b>besides your husband”</b> (Numbers 5:20). This indicates that her husband had lain with her, <b>and this</b> husband, the eunuch, <b>is not capable of that.</b> The mishna therefore <b>teaches us</b> that the wife of a eunuch does drink the bitter water.
§ The mishna states: A husband <b>can issue a warning</b> to his wife <b>with regard to all those with whom relations are forbidden,</b> e.g., her father or brother. The Gemara asks: <b>Isn’t</b> that <b>obvious?</b>

Daf 26b

The Gemara answers: It is necessary, <b>lest you say</b> that the <i>sota</i> ritual does not apply with regard to forbidden relatives, as the Torah states: “And she <b>was defiled”</b> (Numbers 5:13), “And she <b>was defiled”</b> (Numbers 5:14), <b>two times. One</b> verse teaches that she is defiled and forbidden <b>to</b> her <b>husband, and one</b> verse teaches that she is forbidden <b>to</b> her <b>paramour.</b> One might understand that the <i>sota</i> ritual applies only <b>where she is forbidden</b> to the paramour <b>due to this licentious intercourse; however,</b> with regard to <b>that</b> woman, who secluded herself with a forbidden relative, <b>since</b> the woman already <b>stands prohibited</b> to him due to the prohibition of incest, one might <b>say</b> that the <i>sota</i> ritual <b>does not</b> apply. The mishna therefore <b>teaches us</b> that one can issue a warning even with regard to forbidden relatives.
§ The mishna states: A husband can issue a warning to his wife with regard to all those with whom relations are forbidden, <b>with the exception of a minor</b> and of one who is not a man. The Gemara cites the source for this <i>halakha</i>: <b>The Merciful One states</b> in the Torah: “And <b>a man</b> lay with her” (Numbers 5:13), indicating that one can warn his wife with regard to a man <b>but not</b> with regard to <b>a minor.</b> The Gemara asks: The phrase: <b>And</b> of one <b>who is not a man,</b> serves <b>to exclude what? If we say</b> that it serves <b>to exclude a sick man who lacks the ability to complete intercourse [<i>shaḥuf</i>], but didn’t Shmuel say: One can issue a warning with regard to a <i>shaḥuf</i>, and</b> if a <i>shaḥuf</i> engages in sexual intercourse with the daughter of a priest, he <b>disqualifies</b> her <b>from</b> partaking of <b><i>teruma</i>.</b>
With regard to Shmuel’s statement that <b>one can issue a warning with regard to</b> a <i>shaḥuf</i>, the Gemara asks: <b>Isn’t</b> that <b>obvious?</b> The Gemara answers: It is necessary, <b>lest you say</b> that one cannot issue a warning with regard to a <i>shaḥuf</i>, as <b>the Merciful One states</b> in the Torah: <b>“And a man lay with her carnally [<i>shikhvat zera</i>]”</b> (Numbers 5:13), literally, a lying of seed, <b>and this</b> man <b>is not capable of that,</b> as he cannot ejaculate. Shmuel therefore <b>teaches us</b> that one can issue a warning with regard to a <i>shaḥuf</i>.
The Gemara asks with regard to Shmuel’s statement that a <i>shaḥuf</i> who engages in sexual intercourse with the daughter of a priest <b>disqualifies</b> her <b>from</b> partaking of <b><i>teruma</i>: Isn’t</b> that <b>obvious?</b> The Gemara answers: It is necessary <b>lest you say</b> that a <i>shaḥuf</i> does not disqualify the daughter of a priest from partaking of <i>teruma</i>, as <b>the Merciful One states</b> in the Torah with regard to a priest: “And <b>he shall not disqualify his offspring</b> among his people” (Leviticus 21:15). One might infer from this verse that one <b>who</b> can <b>have offspring disqualifies</b> a woman from partaking of <i>teruma</i>, through forbidden sexual intercourse, and that one <b>who cannot have offspring does not disqualify</b> a woman from partaking of <i>teruma</i>. Shmuel therefore <b>teaches us</b> that this is not so. In any event, Shmuel states that one can issue a warning on account of a <i>shaḥuf</i>, unlike the initial interpretation of the mishna.
Since Shmuel’s statement contradicts the suggestion that the mishna excludes a <i>shaḥuf</i>, the Gemara suggests another explanation: <b>Rather,</b> the mishna serves <b>to exclude a gentile,</b> and teaches that one cannot issue a warning with regard to him. The Gemara asks: <b>But didn’t Rav Hamnuna say: One can issue a warning with regard to a gentile, and</b> if a gentile engages in sexual intercourse with the daughter of a priest, he <b>disqualifies</b> her <b>from</b> partaking of <b><i>teruma</i>.</b>
The Gemara asks with regard to Rav Hamnuna’s statement that <b>one can issue a warning with regard to</b> a gentile: <b>Isn’t</b> that <b>obvious?</b> The Gemara answers: It is necessary <b>lest you say</b> that one cannot issue a warning in this case, as the verse states: “And she <b>was defiled”</b> (Numbers 5:13), “And she <b>was defiled”</b> (Numbers 5:14), <b>twice. One</b> verse teaches that she is defiled and forbidden <b>to</b> her <b>husband, and one</b> verse teaches that she is forbidden <b>to</b> her <b>paramour.</b> One might understand that the <i>sota</i> ritual applies only <b>where she is forbidden</b> to the paramour <b>due to this licentious intercourse; however,</b> with regard to <b>that</b> woman, who engaged in sexual intercourse with a gentile, <b>since</b> she already <b>stands prohibited</b> to him, one might <b>say</b> that the <i>sota</i> ritual <b>does not</b> apply. Rav Hamnuna therefore <b>teaches us</b> that one can issue a warning even with regard to a gentile.
The Gemara asks with regard to Rav Hamnuna’s statement that a gentile who engages in sexual intercourse with the daughter of a priest <b>disqualifies</b> her <b>from</b> partaking of <b><i>teruma</i>: Isn’t</b> that <b>obvious?</b> The Gemara answers: It is necessary <b>lest you say</b> that he does not disqualify her, as <b>the Merciful One states</b> in the Torah: <b>“And if a priest’s daughter should be unto a strange man,</b> she shall not eat of that which is set apart from the holy things” (Leviticus 22:12), indicating that if a woman engages in sexual intercourse with one who is unfit for her, he disqualifies her from partaking of <i>teruma</i>. Since the term “should be unto” denotes marriage, one might say <b>that one who is eligible for betrothal, yes,</b> he disqualifies the woman; but a gentile, <b>who is not eligible for betrothal, does not</b> disqualify her. Rav Hamnuna therefore <b>teaches us that</b> a gentile <b>disqualifies</b> the woman from partaking of <i>teruma</i>, as one can learn <b>from</b> the ruling <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan.</b>
This is <b>as Rabbi Yoḥanan says in the name of Rabbi Yishmael: From where</b> is it derived <b>that a gentile or a slave who engaged in sexual intercourse with the daughter of a priest or with the daughter of a Levite or with</b> the <b>daughter of an Israelite, disqualified her</b> from marrying a priest and from partaking of <i>teruma</i>? This is derived <b>as</b> it <b>is stated: “But if a priest’s daughter should be a widow or a divorcée…</b>she returns to her father’s house…she may eat of her father’s bread” (Leviticus 22:13). This indicates that the daughter of a priest returns to eat of her father’s bread, i.e., <i>teruma</i>, if she engaged in sexual intercourse with <b>one whose</b> marriage <b>to her has</b> the potential to end in <b>widowhood or divorce,</b> i.e., a Jew whom she is permitted to marry. This <b>excludes a gentile and a slave, whose</b> marriage <b>to her does not have</b> the potential to end in <b>widowhood or divorce,</b> as their betrothal is invalid.
Since Rav Hamnuna’s statement contradicts the suggestion that the mishna excludes a gentile, the Gemara asks: <b>Rather, what</b> does the term: And of one who is not a man, serve <b>to exclude? Rav Pappa says:</b> This serves <b>to exclude an animal, as</b> the concept of <b>licentiousness does not</b> apply <b>with regard to an animal.</b> Therefore, the <i>halakhot</i> of a <i>sota</i> do not apply in this case.
<b>Rava of Parzakya said to Rav Ashi: From where is this matter that the Sages stated</b> derived, that <b>licentiousness does not</b> apply <b>with regard to an animal?</b> Rav Ashi replied that it is <b>as it is written: “You shall not bring the hire of a harlot, or the price of a dog,</b> into the house of the Lord your God for any vow; for both of them are an abomination to the Lord your God” (Deuteronomy 23:19). This verse prohibits one from sacrificing an animal that served as payment to a prostitute or as payment for the purchase of a dog.
<b>And it is taught</b> in a mishna (<i>Temura</i> 30a): In the converse cases, <b>the hire of a dog,</b> i.e., a kosher animal that was given to the owner of a dog as payment for engaging in intercourse with it, <b>and the price of a prostitute,</b> i.e., a kosher animal which served as payment in the purchase of a maidservant acquired for prostitution, <b>are permitted</b> to be sacrificed, <b>as</b> it <b>is stated</b> in the aforementioned verse: <b>For both of them.</b> This term indicates that only those <b>two</b> animals may not be sacrificed, i.e., those which served as the hire of a harlot and as the price of a dog; <b>and not four</b> animals, as the reverse cases are excluded from this <i>halakha</i>. This indicates that the concept of licentiousness does not apply with regard to animals, as the payment for intercourse with a dog is not considered payment for prostitution.
The Gemara asks: Shmuel states that one can issue a warning with regard to a <i>shaḥuf</i> even though he is unable to discharge semen. <b>But rather, why do I</b> need the verse to state: “And a man lay with her <b>carnally [<i>shikhvat zera</i>]”</b> (Numbers 5:13)? The Gemara answers: It is <b>necessary for that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The term <b>“<i>shikhvat zera</i>” excludes something else.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> meant by the term: <b>Something else? Rav Sheshet said:</b> This <b>excludes</b> a case <b>where</b> the husband <b>issued a warning to</b> his wife not to engage in sexual intercourse <b>in an atypical manner,</b> i.e., anal intercourse, with another man, and teaches that this is not considered a valid warning. <b>Rava said to</b> Rav Sheshet: Intercourse <b>in an atypical manner</b> is considered sexual intercourse, as <b>it is written: “The cohabitations of a woman”</b> (Leviticus 18:22), indicating that there are two forms of sexual intercourse with a woman, vaginal and anal, and there is no halakhic differentiation between them.
<b>Rather, Rava said:</b> It <b>excludes</b> a case <b>where</b> the husband <b>issued a warning to</b> his wife not to engage in intimate contact with another man <b>by way of</b> other <b>limbs,</b> as this is not considered sexual intercourse. <b>Abaye said to</b> Rava: That <b>is merely licentious</b> behavior, <b>and does the Merciful One render</b> a woman <b>forbidden</b> to her husband on account of merely <b>licentious</b> behavior, without sexual intercourse? Since this does not render her forbidden to her husband, it is obvious that if the husband issues a warning in this manner, violating the warning does not cause her to become a <i>sota</i>. The verse is therefore not required to exclude this case.
<b>Rather, Abaye said:</b> The verse <b>excludes</b> a case <b>where</b> the husband <b>issued a warning to</b> his wife <b>with regard to</b> engaging in genital <b>contact</b> without actual penetration. The Gemara asks: <b>This works out well according to the one who says</b> that the definition of the <b>initial stage of intercourse is the insertion of the corona but</b> that genital <b>contact is nothing; this is</b> the reason <b>that the verse came to exclude</b> genital <b>contact. However, according to the one who says</b> that the definition of the <b>initial stage of intercourse is</b> genital <b>contact, what is there to say?</b> Why should this case be excluded from the <i>halakhot</i> of a <i>sota</i>?
The Gemara answers: <b>Actually,</b> the verse serves <b>to</b> exclude a case <b>where</b> the husband <b>issued a warning to</b> his wife not to engage in intimate contact with another man <b>by way of</b> other <b>limbs. And</b> the verse explicitly excludes this case from the <i>halakhot</i> of a <i>sota</i>, <b>lest you say that</b> the woman is rendered a <i>sota</i> due to this warning, as <b>the Merciful One made</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>dependent on</b> the <b>husband’s objection, and</b> the <b>husband objects</b> to contact of this nature. The verse therefore <b>teaches us</b> that this is not considered a warning, as it does not involve sexual intercourse.
§ <b>Shmuel says:</b> It is better that <b>a man marry</b>

Daf 27a

<b>a woman whose suspected promiscuity is publicly spoken of [<i>duma</i>], and not marry the daughter of a woman suspected of promiscuity, as this</b> woman, who is herself suspected of promiscuity, <b>comes from seed</b> of <b>unflawed</b> lineage; <b>but that</b> woman, the daughter of a woman suspected of promiscuity, <b>comes from seed</b> of <b>flawed</b> lineage, as she might be a <i>mamzeret</i>.
<b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> It is better that <b>a man marry the daughter of a woman suspected of promiscuity, and not marry a woman suspected of promiscuity, as this</b> daughter <b>maintains</b> the <b>presumptive status of virtuousness, but that</b> woman suspected of promiscuity <b>does not maintain</b> the <b>presumptive status of virtuousness.</b>
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> to Rabbi Yoḥanan’s statement from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>A man</b> may <b>marry a woman suspected of promiscuity. Rava said</b> in response: <b>And</b> how can <b>you understand</b> the <i>baraita</i> at face value? The <i>baraita</i> states that a man <b>may marry</b> her <b><i>ab initio</i>,</b> yet clearly it is undesirable to do so. <b>Rather,</b> the wording of the <i>baraita</i> is imprecise, and one must render it: <b>If one married</b> a woman suspected of promiscuity, she is permitted to him. Since the <i>baraita</i> is imprecise, one should <b>also</b> correct it and <b>teach: The daughter of a woman suspected of promiscuity.</b>
The Gemara concludes: <b>And the <i>halakha</i></b> is that it is better that <b>a man marry the daughter of a woman suspected of promiscuity, and not marry a woman suspected of promiscuity.</b> This is <b>as Rav Taḥalifa, from the West,</b> i.e., Eretz Yisrael, <b>taught before Rabbi Abbahu:</b> If a married <b>woman engages in adulterous sexual intercourse, her children are</b> considered of <b>unflawed</b> lineage, as <b>most</b> instances of <b>sexual intercourse are attributed to the husband,</b> and consequently it is presumed that the children were conceived by the husband and not by the paramour. Therefore, the daughter of a woman suspected of promiscuity is not suspected of being a <i>mamzeret</i>.
<b>Rav Amram raises a dilemma: What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> if the woman <b>was extremely promiscuous</b> and one cannot reasonably attribute most instances of intercourse to the husband? Are her children considered of unflawed lineage, or is their lineage uncertain? <b>According to</b> the opinion of <b>the one who says</b> that <b>a woman becomes pregnant only close to</b> the time of the onset of <b>her menstruation, you do not</b> need to <b>raise this dilemma, as</b> the husband <b>does not know</b> the time of <b>this,</b> i.e., the time of her menstruation, in advance, <b>and</b> he <b>does not watch her</b> actions in order to prevent her from conceiving from another man. Her children are therefore of uncertain lineage.
<b>When do you</b> need to <b>raise this dilemma?</b> It is necessary <b>according to</b> the opinion of <b>the one who says</b> that <b>a woman becomes pregnant only close to</b> the time of <b>her immersion. What</b> is the <i>halakha</i>? Can it be assumed that <b>since he knows</b> the time of <b>this,</b> i.e., of her immersion, <b>he watches her</b> actions on that day in order to prevent her from conceiving from another man; <b>or perhaps, since</b> she <b>is extremely promiscuous,</b> her husband <b>cannot</b> adequately watch her and her children are considered of uncertain lineage? The Gemara concludes: The dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
§ The mishna states (24a): <b>And these</b> are the women <b>to whom the court issues a warning</b> in place of their husbands: One whose husband became a deaf-mute or became an imbecile, or if he were incarcerated in prison. <b>The Sages taught:</b> The verse states: “If the wife of any man goes astray” (Numbers 5:12). As the verse could have said: The wife of <b>a man, what</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states:</b> “The wife of <b>any man”?</b> It serves <b>to include the wife of a deaf-mute, and the wife of an imbecile, and the wife of an insane person, and</b> one <b>whose husband went overseas, and</b> one <b>whose</b> husband <b>was incarcerated in prison;</b> and it teaches <b>that the court issues a warning to</b> these women in order <b>to disqualify them from</b> receiving payment of <b>their marriage contract.</b>
One <b>might</b> have thought that the court’s warning is effective <b>even to have</b> the women <b>drink</b> the bitter water; therefore, <b>the verse states: “Then the man shall bring his wife</b> to the priest” (Numbers 5:15), indicating that only the warning issued by the husband causes his wife to drink. <b>Rabbi Yosei says:</b> The court’s warning is effective <b>even to have her drink, and when her husband is released from prison he has her drink.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>With regard to what do they disagree?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>The Rabbis hold</b> that <b>we require</b> the same person who issues the warning to bring the woman to the Temple, as the verse states: <b>“And he warned</b> his wife…<b>then</b> the man <b>shall bring</b> his wife to the priest” (Numbers 5:14–15). <b>And Rabbi Yosei holds</b> that <b>we do not require</b> that the actions stated in the verse, i.e., <b>“And he warned…then</b> the man <b>shall bring,”</b> be performed by the same person; and although only the husband may bring the woman to the priest to drink the bitter water, the warning may be issued by the court.
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> that the verse: “This is the law of jealousy, <b>when a wife, while under her husband, goes astray</b> and is defiled” (Numbers 5:29), is superfluous, and serves <b>to compare a man to a woman and a woman to a man.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>With regard to what <i>halakha</i></b> is this comparison necessary? <b>Rav Sheshet says:</b> This teaches that <b>just as if</b> the husband <b>was blind he would not have her drink, as it is written: “And it was hidden from the eyes of her husband”</b> (Numbers 5:13), indicating that the <i>sota</i> ritual applies only if the husband was capable of seeing her infidelity but did not do so; <b>so</b> too, with regard to the woman, <b>if she were blind, she would not drink. Rav Ashi</b> also <b>says: Just as a lame woman and a woman without hands would not drink, as it is written:</b>

Daf 27b

<b>“And the priest shall stand the woman before the Lord…and place</b> the meal-offering of memorial <b>in her hands”</b> (Numbers 5:18), indicating that if she is unable to stand up straight or if she does not have hands with which to accept the offering, then she does not drink; <b>so</b> too, <b>if</b> the husband <b>were lame or without hands, he would not cause</b> his wife <b>to drink. Mar Bar Rav Ashi says: Just as a mute woman would not drink, as it is written: “And the woman shall say: Amen, Amen”</b> (Numbers 5:22), indicating that she must be able to speak; <b>so</b> too, <b>if</b> the husband <b>were mute,</b> he <b>would not cause</b> his wife <b>to drink.</b>

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>Just as the water evaluates her</b> fidelity, <b>so too, the water evaluates his,</b> i.e., her alleged paramour’s, involvement in the sin, <b>as it is stated: “And</b> the water that causes the curse <b>shall enter</b> into her” (Numbers 5:24), and it is stated again: <b>“And</b> the water that causes the curse <b>shall enter</b> into her and become bitter” (Numbers 5:27). It is derived from the double mention of the phrase “and…shall enter” that both the woman and her paramour are evaluated by the water.
Furthermore, prior to her drinking the water, <b>just as she is forbidden to</b> her <b>husband, so</b> too <b>is she forbidden to</b> her <b>paramour, because</b> in contrast to the verse stating: <b>“Is defiled [<i>nitma’a</i>]”</b> (Numbers 5:14), a superfluous conjoining prefix <i>vav</i> is added to a later verse, rendering the phrase: <b>“And is defiled [<i>venitma’a</i>]”</b> (Numbers 5:29). The addition indicates another prohibition, that of the woman to her paramour. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Akiva.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yehoshua said: That was how Zekharya ben HaKatzav would interpret</b> it, i.e., he also derived from the superfluous <i>vav</i> that the woman is forbidden to her paramour. <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says</b> an alternate source: The <b>two times that</b> the defilement of the wife <b>is stated in the passage,</b> namely: “And he warns his wife, and <b>she is defiled”</b> (Numbers 5:14), and the later verse: “When a wife, being under her husband, goes astray <b>and is defiled”</b> (Numbers 5:29), indicate that her defilement results in two prohibitions. <b>One</b> is that she is forbidden <b>to</b> her <b>husband and one</b> is that she is forbidden <b>to</b> her <b>paramour.</b>
§ <b>On that same day</b> that Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya was appointed head of the Sanhedrin, <b>Rabbi Akiva interpreted</b> the verse: <b>“And every earthen vessel into which any of them falls, whatever is in it shall be impure [<i>yitma</i>],</b> and you shall break it” (Leviticus 11:33), as follows: The verse <b>does not state: Is impure [<i>tamei</i>],</b> but <b>rather: “Shall be impure,”</b> in order <b>to</b> indicate that not only does the vessel itself become ritually impure, but it can now <b>render other</b> items <b>ritually impure.</b> This <b>teaches with regard to a loaf</b> that has <b>second-</b>degree ritual impurity status due to its being placed inside an earthenware vessel that had first-degree impurity, <b>that it can render</b> other food with which it comes into contact <b>impure</b> with <b>third-</b>degree impurity status.
After hearing Rabbi Akiva’s statement, <b>Rabbi Yehoshua said: Who will remove the dirt from your eyes, Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai,</b> so that you could live and see this? <b>As you would say:</b> In the <b>future, another generation</b> is destined <b>to deem pure a loaf</b> that contracted <b>third-</b>degree impurity, <b>as there is no</b> explicit <b>verse from the Torah</b> stating <b>that it is impure. But</b> now <b>Rabbi Akiva, your disciple, brings a verse from the Torah</b> indicating <b>that it is impure, as it is stated: “Whatever is in it shall be impure.”</b>
Furthermore, <b>on that same day Rabbi Akiva interpreted</b> the verses with regard to the Levite cities as follows: One verse states: <b>“And you shall measure outside the city for the east side two thousand cubits…</b>this shall be for them the open land outside the cities” (Numbers 35:5), <b>and another verse states:</b> “And the open land around the cities, which you shall give to the Levites, shall be <b>from the wall of the city and outward one thousand cubits round about”</b> (Numbers 35:4).
<b>It is impossible to say</b> that the area around the cities given to the Levites was only <b>one thousand cubits, as it is already stated: “Two thousand cubits.” And it is impossible to say</b> that <b>two thousand cubits</b> were left for them, <b>as it is already stated: “One thousand cubits.” How</b> can <b>these</b> texts be reconciled? <b>One thousand cubits</b> are to be set aside as <b>a tract</b> of open land surrounding the city, <b>and</b> the <b>two thousand cubits</b> are mentioned not in order to be given to the Levites, but to indicate the boundary of <b>the Shabbat limit,</b> beyond which it is forbidden to travel on Shabbat. This verse thereby serves as the source for the two-thousand-cubit Shabbat limit.
<b>Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Yosei HaGelili, says</b> otherwise: <b>One thousand cubits</b> were given to the Levites as an open <b>tract</b> of land, that could not be planted or built upon, <b>and two thousand cubits</b> of additional land were given to the Levites for planting <b>fields and vineyards.</b>
Additionally, <b>on that same day Rabbi Akiva interpreted</b> the verse: <b>“Then Moses and the children of Israel sang this song to the Lord, and said, saying”</b> (Exodus 15:1), as follows: <b>As</b> there is <b>no</b> need for <b>the verse to state</b> the word <b>“saying,”</b> because it states the word “said” immediately prior to it, <b>why</b> must <b>the verse state</b> the word <b>“saying”? It teaches that the Jewish people would repeat in song after Moses every single statement</b> he said, <b>as</b> is done when <b>reciting <i>hallel</i>.</b> After Moses would recite a verse, they would say as a refrain: <b>“I will sing to the Lord, for He is highly exalted”</b> (Exodus 15:1). It is <b>for this</b> reason that the word <b>“saying” is stated,</b> in addition to the word “said.”
<b>Rabbi Neḥemya says:</b> The people sang the song together with Moses <b>as</b> is done when <b>reciting <i>Shema</i>,</b> which is recited in unison after the prayer leader begins, <b>and not as</b> is done when <b>reciting <i>hallel</i>.</b>
<b>On that same day Rabbi Yehoshua ben Hyrcanus taught: Job served the Holy One, Blessed be He, only out of love, as it is stated: “Though He will slay me, still I will trust in Him”</b> (Job 13:15). <b>And still, the matter is even,</b> i.e., the verse is ambiguous, as there are two possible interpretations of the verse. Was Job saying: <b>I will await Him,</b> expressing his yearning for God; <b>or</b> should the verse be interpreted as saying <b>I will not await</b> Him. As the word “lo” can mean either “to him” or “not,” it is unclear which meaning is intended here. This dilemma is resolved elsewhere, where <b>the verse states</b> a clearer indication of Job’s intent: <b>“Till I die I will not put away my integrity from me”</b> (Job 27:5). This <b>teaches that he acted out of love.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yehoshua said: Who will remove the dirt from your eyes, Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai,</b> so that you could live and see this? <b>As you taught all your life that Job worshipped the Omnipresent only out of fear, as it is stated:</b> “And that <b>man</b> was <b>wholehearted and upright, and God-fearing, and shunned evil”</b> (Job 1:1); <b>but</b> now <b>Yehoshua</b> ben Hyrcanus, <b>the disciple of your disciple, has taught that</b> Job <b>acted out of love.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> It is stated in the mishna that just as the water evaluates whether the woman was unfaithful, so too, the water evaluates whether <b>he</b> committed this sin. The Gemara asks: <b>To whom</b> does this refer? <b>If we say</b> that it is referring <b>to the husband, what did the husband do</b> that he should be evaluated? <b>And if you would say</b>

Daf 28a

<b>that if he has</b> committed a similar <b>iniquity the water evaluates his actions,</b> this is difficult, as in a case <b>where he has</b> committed a similar <b>iniquity does the water</b> even <b>evaluate her</b> fidelity? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that the verse: <b>“And the man shall be clear from iniquity, and that woman shall bear her iniquity”</b> (Numbers 5:31), indicates that only <b>when the man is clear of iniquity does the water evaluate</b> the fidelity of <b>his wife,</b> but if <b>the man is not clear of iniquity the water does not evaluate</b> the fidelity of <b>his wife?</b>
<b>And</b> if the mishna is <b>rather</b> referring <b>to the</b> alleged <b>paramour,</b> who is also evaluated by the water that the woman drinks, then <b>let</b> the mishna <b>teach as is taught</b> in its <b>latter clause: Just as she is forbidden to</b> her <b>husband, so</b> too <b>is she forbidden to</b> her <b>paramour.</b> Just as there the paramour is mentioned explicitly, so too here, the mishna should have stated: Just as the water evaluates whether she was unfaithful, so too, it evaluates whether the paramour committed this iniquity.
The Gemara answers: The entire mishna <b>actually</b> does refer <b>to the paramour, and</b> the reason he is not mentioned explicitly in <b>the first clause</b> of the mishna is because <b>since</b> it <b>teaches</b> that the water evaluates whether the wife was unfaithful by using the direct object <b>her,</b> it also <b>teaches</b> that the water evaluates whether the paramour committed the by act using the direct object <b>him,</b> without mentioning the paramour explicitly. In <b>the latter clause</b> of the mishna, on the other hand, <b>since</b> it <b>teaches</b> explicitly that the woman is forbidden to her <b>husband,</b> it also <b>teaches</b> explicitly that she is forbidden to her <b>paramour.</b>
§ In the mishna Rabbi Akiva proves that the water evaluates the paramour as well, <b>as it is stated: “And</b> the water that causes the curse <b>shall enter</b> into her” (Numbers 5:24), and: <b>“And</b> the water that causes the curse <b>shall enter</b> into her and become bitter” (Numbers 5:27). <b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages concerning the precise wording of the mishna: <b>Does</b> the mishna <b>state: “Shall enter [<i>ba’u</i>],” “and shall enter [<i>uva’u</i>]”?</b> According to this version of the mishna, it is derived from the superfluous conjoining prefix <i>vav</i> that the paramour is also evaluated by the water. <b>Or,</b> alternatively, <b>does</b> the mishna <b>state: “And shall enter,” “and shall enter,”</b> indicating that this <i>halakha</i> is derived from the repetition of the phrase in two separate verses?
<b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof from Rabbi Akiva’s second statement in the mishna, where he says: <b>Just as she is forbidden to</b> her <b>husband, so</b> too <b>is she forbidden to</b> her <b>paramour, as it is stated: “Is defiled [<i>nitma’a</i>],” “And is defiled [<i>venitma’a</i>]”</b> (Numbers 5:29). Here it seems that Rabbi Akiva derives his interpretation from the superfluous prefix <i>vav</i> rather than from the repetition of the phrase. Therefore, the first derivation should be understood in the same manner.
The Gemara asks: <b>But still, let the dilemma be raised</b> with regard to this <i>halakha</i> too: <b>Does</b> Rabbi Akiva <b>state</b> that the source for the <i>halakha</i> is the mention of the phrase <b>“is defiled,” “is defiled,”</b> in two different verses (Numbers 5:14, 29), <b>or does he state</b> that the <i>halakha</i> is derived from the superfluous <i>vav</i> in the phrase <b>“is defiled,”</b> rendering it <b>“and is defiled”</b> (Numbers 5:29)?
<b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof <b>from</b> the fact <b>that</b> the mishna <b>teaches</b> in <b>the latter clause</b> that <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says:</b> The <b>two times that</b> the wife’s defilement <b>is stated in the passage,</b> namely: “And he warns his wife, <b>and she is defiled”</b> (Numbers 5:14), and the later verse: “When a wife, being under her husband, goes astray <b>and is defiled”</b> (Numbers 5:29), indicate that there are two prohibitions due to her defilement. <b>One is</b> to forbid her <b>to</b> her <b>husband and one is</b> to forbid her <b>to</b> her <b>paramour. By inference</b> from the fact that the dissenting derivation of Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi is from the repetition of the entire phrase, evidently <b>Rabbi Akiva derives</b> this <i>halakha</i> from the superfluous <b><i>vav</i>.</b>
<b>Therefore, according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva,</b> since the phrase “and the water…shall enter” is mentioned three times in the passage, and the prefix <i>vav</i>, written each time, is expounded as though the phrase were mentioned twice, the phrase is treated as though it were <b>written</b> in <b>six verses,</b> as follows.
<b>One</b> of the mentions (Numbers 5:24) is interpreted <b>for</b> the <b>command concerning her,</b> the woman, meaning that God empowered the waters to punish the woman; <b>and one,</b> the prefix <i>vav</i> in that same verse, is expounded <b>for</b> the <b>command concerning him,</b> the paramour, i.e., that he too shall be punished by the water if he is guilty.
<b>One</b> mention of the phrase, in the description of the drinking of the bitter water of a <i>sota</i> (Numbers 5:27), is interpreted <b>for the execution</b> of <b>her</b> punishment, as the punishment will go into effect so long as the process was performed properly; <b>and one,</b> the prefix <i>vav</i> in that verse, is expounded <b>for the execution</b> of <b>his</b> punishment.
<b>One</b> mention (Numbers 5:22) is <b>for her knowledge,</b> i.e., the priest informs her that this punishment will be the result; <b>and one,</b> the prefix <i>vav</i>, is <b>for his knowledge.</b>
<b>But Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi maintains that only <b>three verses</b> worthy of exposition <b>are written</b> with regard to the water entering the woman; he does not derive anything additional from the prefix <i>vav</i> that introduces the various mentions of this matter. He therefore interprets <b>one for</b> the <b>command, and one for the execution, and one for</b> the <b>knowledge,</b> all with regard to the woman herself.
The Gemara asks: <b>And from where does Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi derive the principle in the mishna that <b>just as the water evaluates</b> whether <b>she</b> was unfaithful, <b>so</b> too, <b>it evaluates</b> whether <b>he</b> committed the sin?
The Gemara answers: <b>He derives it from that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>, that the verse: <b>“And cause the belly to swell and the thigh to fall away”</b> (Numbers 5:22), is referring to <b>the belly and thigh of the paramour.</b> Do <b>you say</b> that the intention is <b>the belly and the thigh of the paramour, or is it only the belly and the thigh of the adulteress? When it says</b> later: <b>“And her belly shall swell, and her thigh shall fall away”</b> (Numbers 5:27), <b>the belly and thigh of the adulteress are</b> explicitly <b>stated. And</b> therefore, <b>how do I realize</b> the meaning of the former verse: <b>“And cause the belly to swell, and the thigh to fall away”?</b> Clearly, it is referring to <b>the belly and thigh of the paramour.</b>
<b>And</b> how does <b>the other</b> <i>tanna</i>, Rabbi Akiva, interpret the repetition of verses? The former verse indicates <b>that</b> the <b>priest informs her that</b> her <b>belly</b> will be afflicted <b>first and then</b> her <b>thigh,</b> so <b>as not to cast aspersions on the bitter water</b> of a <i>sota</i>, i.e., to prevent people from claiming that the guilty woman’s death was not due to the bitter water but rather to some other cause. The reason people might claim this is that the priest says to the woman: “The Lord will make you a curse and an oath among your people, when the Lord makes your thigh fall away, and your belly swell” (Numbers 5:21). This seems to imply that her thigh is supposed to be afflicted before her belly. Therefore, when her belly swells first, people might conclude that it is not due to the water. It is for this reason that the priest needs to inform her that her belly will swell first.
<b>And</b> why does <b>the other</b> <i>tanna</i>, Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi, disagree with Rabbi Akiva? The Gemara answers: <b>If</b> it is <b>so</b> that the verse: “And cause the belly to swell, and the thigh to fall away” (Numbers 5:22), is referring to the woman, <b>the verse should have written: Her belly…and her thigh. What</b> is meant by the phraseology of <b>“the belly…and the thigh”? Conclude from it</b> that it is referring <b>to</b> the belly and thigh of <b>the paramour.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And say that the entire</b> verse <b>comes for this,</b> to indicate that the water evaluates the paramour as well, and does not teach the order of the punishment? The Gemara answers: <b>If so,</b> the Torah <b>should have written: His belly…and his thigh. What</b> is the meaning of the general wording: <b>“The belly…and the thigh”? Conclude from it two</b> conclusions: That the paramour is punished and that the priest informs the woman with regard to the order of the punishment.
§ It is stated in the mishna that <b>Rabbi Yehoshua said: That was how Zekharya</b> ben HaKatzav <b>would interpret</b> it. Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi says: The two times that the defilement of the wife is stated in the passage indicate that there are two prohibitions due to her defilement; one is to forbid her to her husband and one is to forbid her to her paramour.
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to the <b>three times that</b> the defilement of the wife <b>is stated in the passage,</b> namely: <b>“If she is defiled”</b> (Numbers 5:27), “and he warns his wife, and <b>she is defiled”</b> (Numbers 5:14), and “when a wife being under her husband goes astray <b>and is defiled”</b> (Numbers 5:29), <b>why</b> are all three necessary? <b>One</b> is to prohibit her <b>to</b> her <b>husband, and one</b> is to prohibit her <b>to</b> her <b>paramour, and one</b> is <b>to</b> prohibit her from partaking of <b><i>teruma</i>,</b> even if she is the wife or daughter of a priest. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Akiva.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yishmael said:</b> It is unnecessary to derive from a verse that it would also be prohibited for this woman to marry a priest, as it can be derived <b><i>a fortiori</i>: If a divorced</b> daughter of a priest, <b>who is permitted to</b> partake of <b><i>teruma</i>, is</b> nevertheless <b>forbidden to</b> marry into the <b>priesthood,</b> then with regard to <b>this</b> <i>sota</i>, <b>who is forbidden</b> to partake <b>of <i>teruma</i>, is it not logical that it is</b> also <b>prohibited</b> for her <b>to</b> marry into the <b>priesthood?</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues by citing additional expositions involving the verse: “And she is defiled” (Numbers 5:14): <b>What</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states</b> with regard to the cases in which a husband can compel his wife to drink the bitter water of a <i>sota</i>: “And he warns his wife, <b>and she is defiled;</b> or if the spirit of jealousy comes upon him, and he warns his wife, <b>and she is not defiled”</b> (Numbers 5:14)? <b>If she is defiled, why does she</b> need to <b>drink?</b> And <b>if she is not defiled, why does he make her drink?</b> The <i>baraita</i> answers: <b>The verse tells you that</b> it is discussing a case when there is <b>uncertainty</b> as to whether the woman was faithful to her husband, yet <b>it is prohibited</b> for her to engage in sexual intercourse with her husband until the matter is clarified.
<b>From here you can derive</b> the <i>halakha</i> in a case of uncertainty <b>with regard to</b> whether the carcass of <b>a creeping animal</b> has imparted ritual impurity: <b>Just as</b> in the case of <b>a <i>sota</i>, where</b> the Torah <b>does not consider unwitting</b> adultery <b>like intentional</b> adultery, <b>and rape</b> is not treated <b>like a willing</b> transgression, because if a married woman committed adultery unwittingly or was raped she is not punished, yet still the Torah <b>considers an uncertain</b> case of adultery <b>like a certain</b> violation inasmuch as the woman is forbidden to her husband until the truth is clarified; so too, with regard to <b>a creeping animal</b> or other agents of ritual impurity, <b>where</b> the Torah <b>does consider unwitting</b> contact with impure items <b>like intentional</b> contact, as one contracts impurity whether or not his contact was intentional <b>and an accident</b> is treated <b>like willing</b> contact, <b>is it not logical that</b> the Torah must also <b>consider an uncertain</b> case of transmission of ritual impurity

Daf 28b

<b>like</b> a case of <b>certain</b> contact with an impure item? Accordingly, any cases of uncertain ritual impurity should be treated like certain impurity.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>And</b> since the case of <i>sota</i> is the source for the <i>halakha</i> with regard to uncertain ritual impurity, the details of the <i>halakha</i> are also derived from the case of <i>sota</i>. Therefore, <b>from the place that you came</b> from, i.e., from the source, it is derived that <b>just as</b> the prohibition with regard to <b>a <i>sota</i></b> applies only when the uncertainty arises in <b>the private domain,</b> i.e., when she has secluded herself with the alleged paramour, <b>so too,</b> uncertain contact with the carcass of <b>a creeping animal</b> renders an item impure only if the contact was in <b>the private domain.</b>
<b>And</b> furthermore, <b>just as a <i>sota</i> is an entity that has awareness</b> in order for her <b>to be asked</b> whether she actually committed adultery, <b>so too,</b> contact with <b>a creeping animal</b> renders an item impure only if it is <b>an entity that has awareness</b> in order for it <b>to be asked,</b> i.e., a person, or an item that may have contracted impurity in a place where a person was present and could have known.
The <i>baraita</i> concludes: <b>And from here</b> the Sages <b>stated</b> that if <b>an entity that has awareness</b> in order for it <b>to be asked</b> may have contracted impurity <b>in the private domain, its uncertain</b> impurity renders it <b>impure;</b> but if it may have contracted impurity <b>in the public domain, its uncertain</b> impurity leaves it <b>pure. And</b> with regard to an entity <b>that lacks awareness</b> in order for it <b>to be asked, whether</b> the uncertainty arose <b>in the private domain or in the public domain, its uncertain</b> impurity is deemed <b>pure,</b> as it is not similar to a <i>sota</i>.
The Gemara begins its discussion of the <i>baraita</i> by inquiring about the exchange between Rabbi Yishmael and Rabbi Akiva: <b>And</b> what was the intention of <b>Rabbi Yishmael?</b> Apparently, he commented on a statement of <b>Rabbi Akiva,</b> who <b>said</b> that it is prohibited for a <i>sota</i> to partake of <b><i>teruma</i>, and he answered him</b> concerning the matter of the woman’s being prohibited to marry into the <b>priesthood,</b> which was not mentioned by Rabbi Akiva at all.
<b>And furthermore, from where does Rabbi Akiva derive</b> that it is prohibited for a <i>sota</i> to marry into the <b>priesthood? And if you would say</b> that with regard to her prohibition against marrying into the <b>priesthood a verse is not necessary,</b>

Daf 29a

<b>as</b> the Torah has already <b>rendered an uncertain</b> case of <b>a woman who has engaged in sexual intercourse with a man forbidden to her by the Torah [<i>zona</i>] as though</b> she is certainly <b>a <i>zona</i>,</b> since a <i>sota</i> is forbidden to her husband after seclusion even though there are no witnesses that she committed adultery, and therefore it should be prohibited for her to marry a priest just like any <i>zona</i>, then with regard to her prohibition against partaking of <b><i>teruma</i> a verse should also not be necessary, as</b> it is prohibited for a <i>zona</i> to partake of <i>teruma</i>. Therefore, the Torah <b>renders an uncertain <i>zona</i> like</b> a certain <b><i>zona</i>.</b>
<b>Rather,</b> it must be explained that <b>according to Rabbi Akiva,</b> the equivalent of <b>four verses</b> worthy of exposition <b>are written</b> with regard to the defilement of a <i>sota</i>, as he maintains that an additional <i>halakha</i> should be derived from the superfluous prefix <i>vav</i> in the verse: “And is defiled [<i>venitma’a</i>]” (Numbers 5:29). Therefore, <b>one</b> verse is written to forbid her <b>to</b> her <b>husband, and one</b> is to forbid her <b>to</b> her <b>paramour, and one</b> is to forbid her <b>to</b> marry into the <b>priesthood, and one</b> is to forbid her <b>to</b> partake of <b><i>teruma</i>.</b>
<b>And Rabbi Yishmael</b> disagrees with Rabbi Akiva, as he does not expound on the superfluous <i>vav</i>, and therefore maintains that only <b>three verses are written: One</b> is to forbid her <b>to</b> her <b>husband, and one</b> is to forbid her <b>to</b> her <b>paramour, and one</b> is to forbid her <b>to</b> partake of <b><i>teruma</i>. And</b> her being prohibited to marry into the <b>priesthood is derived through an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference, as described in the <i>baraita</i>.
The Gemara asks: <b>And from where does Rabbi Yishmael</b> derive <b>that the verse was necessary</b> to teach the prohibition of a <i>sota</i> <b>to</b> partake of <b><i>teruma</i>, and</b> her prohibition against marrying into the <b>priesthood is derived through an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference? <b>Perhaps</b> the verse <b>was necessary</b> in order to teach that it is prohibited for the woman to marry <b>into</b> the <b>priesthood, but <i>teruma</i> is permitted</b> for her?
The Gemara answers: Rabbi Yishmael could have <b>said to you: It is reasonable</b> to derive from this verse a prohibition that is <b>similar to</b> the prohibitions derived from the other verses, i.e., that she is forbidden <b>to</b> her <b>husband and</b> her <b>paramour. Just as</b> she is forbidden to her <b>husband and</b> her <b>paramour</b> even <b>during</b> the <b>lifetime</b> of her husband, <b>so too,</b> the prohibition against partaking of <b><i>teruma</i></b> applies <b>also during</b> the <b>lifetime</b> of her husband, <b>to the exclusion of</b> her prohibition against marrying into the <b>priesthood, which</b> is relevant only <b>after</b> the <b>death</b> of her husband. The reason is that if her husband would divorce her it would be prohibited for her to marry a priest anyway.
<b>And</b> how would <b>Rabbi Akiva</b> respond? The Gemara answers: <b>He is not</b> of the opinion that it is more reasonable to derive a prohibition from the phrase “and is defiled” that is <b>similar to</b> the prohibitions involving her <b>husband and</b> her <b>paramour,</b> and therefore he requires two separate derivations; one for <i>teruma</i> and one for the priesthood.
<b>Or alternatively,</b> perhaps <b>he accepts</b> the opinion that the <i>halakha</i> derived from “and is defiled” should be similar to the prohibitions involving the husband and paramour, but nevertheless, in certain instances with regard to <b>a matter that can be derived through an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference, the <b>verse</b> nevertheless <b>takes the trouble and writes</b> explicitly. Therefore, although unnecessary, two verses are stated, one for <i>teruma</i> and one for the priesthood.
The Gemara continues discussing the <i>baraita</i>. <b>Rav Giddel said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b> The <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>an entity that has awareness</b> in order for it <b>to be asked and</b> an entity that <b>lacks awareness</b> in order for it <b>to be asked</b> in cases of uncertain ritual impurity <b>is derived from this verse: “And the flesh that touches any impure thing shall not be eaten”</b> (Leviticus 7:19). This would seem to indicate that specifically food that is <b>impure</b> for <b>certain is</b> that <b>which shall not be eaten, but</b> food for which it is <b>uncertain</b> whether it is <b>impure and uncertain</b> whether it is <b>pure can be eaten.</b>
However, <b>say the latter clause</b> of the verse: <b>“And as for the flesh, every one that is pure may eat the flesh”</b> (Leviticus 7:19), which would seem to indicate that specifically one who is <b>pure</b> for <b>certain is</b> one <b>who shall eat meat, but</b> one for whom it is <b>uncertain</b> whether he is <b>impure and uncertain</b> whether he is <b>pure shall not eat.</b> The two clauses in the verse seem to contradict one another with regard to the status of uncertain purity.
<b>Rather,</b> must one <b>not conclude from it</b> that the Torah differentiates between two different types of uncertainty? <b>Here,</b> the latter clause, which discusses “every one that is pure,” is referring to an entity <b>that has awareness</b> in order for it <b>to be asked,</b> e.g., a person, who is considered impure if he is uncertain whether he contracted ritual impurity. <b>There,</b> the former clause which discusses impure meat presents the principle of an entity <b>that lacks awareness</b> in order for it <b>to be asked,</b> where an uncertain case of ritual impurity is deemed pure.
The Gemara explains the need for two separate derivations concerning uncertain contractions of ritual impurity: <b>And</b> the derivation <b>that Rav Giddel</b> said that <b>Rav said was necessary, and</b> it <b>was</b> also <b>necessary to derive</b> the principle of uncertain ritual impurity <b>from <i>sota</i>; since if</b> it were derived only <b>through</b> the derivation <b>of Rav, I would say</b> that it makes no difference <b>whether</b> the uncertain contraction of impurity occurred <b>in the private domain or whether</b> it occurred <b>in the public domain.</b> Therefore, it <b>was necessary to derive from <i>sota</i></b> that uncertain impurity is considered impure only in the private domain.
<b>And if</b> it is derived only <b>from <i>sota</i>, I would say</b> that similar to <i>sota</i>, where both the woman and the paramour possess awareness in order to be asked if they committed the act, so too, items with uncertain impurity should not be deemed impure <b>unless there is awareness</b> on the part of both <b>the one who touches</b> the impure item <b>and the one who causes him to touch,</b> i.e., the agent of impurity and the recipient of impurity are both competent people. Therefore, the derivation of Rav was <b>necessary,</b> as it teaches that in an uncertain case of impurity in the private domain, one is deemed impure even if only the contractor of impurity possesses the awareness to be asked.
§ It is stated in the mishna: <b>On that same day Rabbi Akiva interpreted</b> the phrase “shall be impure” in the verse: <b>“And every earthen vessel</b> into which any of them falls, whatever is in it shall be impure [<i>yitma</i>], and you shall break it” (Leviticus 11:33), as indicating that a loaf that has second-degree ritual impurity can render other food with which it comes into contact impure with third-degree impurity. Rabbi Yehoshua related that Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai had predicted that a future generation would purify a loaf that contracted third-degree impurity, as there is no explicit verse in the Torah stating that this degree of impurity exists.
The Gemara asks: <b>But since</b> the loaf <b>does not have</b> an explicit verse stating that it is impure, <b>why</b> did Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai himself maintain that it is <b>impure?</b>
<b>Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Rav says:</b> Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai maintained that although <b>it has no</b> explicit basis <b>from</b> a verse in <b>the Torah, it has</b> proof for its impurity <b>through an <i>a fortiori</i> inference: If</b> even <b>one who immersed</b> in a ritual bath <b>that day</b> and will become completely purified after nightfall, <b>who is</b> therefore <b>permitted to</b> touch <b>non-sacred</b> articles, i.e., he does not transmit impurity to them, <b>disqualifies <i>teruma</i></b> that he touches, then with regard to <b>a loaf</b> that has <b>second-</b>degree impurity as result of contact with an impure item of the first degree, <b>which is disqualified,</b> i.e., it is rendered impure, even if it is <b>non-sacred, isn’t it logical that it should impart third-</b>degree impurity <b>upon <i>teruma</i>?</b>
The Gemara challenges: This <i>a fortiori</i> inference <b>can be refuted. What</b> is unique <b>about one</b> who was ritually impure <b>who immersed that day</b> and is waiting for nightfall for the purification process to be completed <b>is that</b> prior to his immersion, he was <b>a primary source of impurity.</b> He therefore retains his stringent status even after immersion with regard to his capability of disqualifying <i>teruma</i>. The loaf, by contrast, has second-degree impurity from the outset, and should therefore be treated more lightly.
The Gemara answers: <b>Derive</b> this <i>halakha</i>

Daf 29b

<b>from one who immersed that day</b> who was never a primary source of impurity, as he was rendered impure only <b>through</b> contact with <b>a creeping animal,</b> and nevertheless he disqualifies <i>teruma</i> upon contact.
The Gemara continues to challenge the inference: <b>What</b> is unique <b>about</b> the impurity of <b>one who immersed that day</b> who was rendered impure <b>through</b> contact with <b>a creeping animal is that</b> this impurity can apply to either a person or a utensil, as people and utensils are capable of achieving purity through immersion, and <b>within its type</b> there can be <b>a primary source of impurity.</b> A loaf, however, is food, which can never be a primary source of impurity. It can only be rendered impure as a secondary source of impurity.
The Gemara answers: The <i>halakhot</i> of <b>an earthenware vessel can prove</b> that the fact that there are primary sources of impurity within its type is not a relevant factor. An earthenware vessel can never become a primary source of impurity, and nevertheless, if it is impure it disqualifies <i>teruma</i> upon contact.
The Gemara challenges: <b>What</b> is unique <b>about an earthenware vessel is that</b> unlike a loaf, it <b>can render</b> items <b>impure</b> or it can itself become impure <b>from its airspace.</b> An earthenware vessel is the only vessel that does not require direct contact with another item in order to contract or transfer impurity, but can effect or contract impurity through its airspace.
The Gemara answers: <b>One who immersed that day can prove</b> that being able to render items impure through airspace is not a relevant factor. Such an item disqualifies <i>teruma</i>, but imparts impurity only through direct contact and not through its airspace.
<b>And the derivation has reverted</b> to its starting point. <b>The aspect of this</b> case <b>is not like the aspect of that</b> case <b>and the aspect of that</b> case <b>is not like the aspect of this</b> case, as each case has its own unique stringencies. However, <b>their common denominator is that non-sacred</b> food they come into contact with <b>is permitted,</b> i.e., is not rendered impure, <b>but they disqualify <i>teruma</i>.</b> Therefore, Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai infers <i>a fortiori</i> that <b>all the more so, a loaf</b> that contracted <b>second-</b>degree impurity, <b>which is disqualified,</b> i.e., rendered impure, even if it is <b>non-sacred,</b> should also <b>disqualify <i>teruma</i></b> with which it comes into contact. This was Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai’s logical <i>a fortiori</i> inference that led him to rule that a loaf of second-degree impurity status disqualifies <i>teruma</i>.
<b>And another generation,</b> i.e., the later generation that Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai predicted would deem <i>teruma</i> that came into contact with second-degree impurity pure, would <b>refute</b> this inference as follows: <b>What</b> is unique <b>about their common denominator is that</b> both cases <b>have a stringent aspect</b> that does not exist in other impure items.
<b>And Rabban Yoḥanan ben Zakkai</b> maintained that <i>teruma</i> that came into contact with second-degree impurity is pure, as he <b>would not refute</b> an inference that is based on two sources due to the fact that both sources have <b>a stringent aspect,</b> since each source’s stringency is not shared by the other.
§ <b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Ḥagiga</i> 3:18) that <b>Rabbi Yosei said: From where</b> is it derived with regard <b>to sacrificial</b> food with <b>fourth-</b>degree ritual impurity <b>that it is disqualified</b> although it is not capable of imparting impurity to other items?
<b>It is</b> derived through <b>logical</b> inference: <b>Just as one who is lacking atonement,</b> e.g., a <i>zav</i> or leper who immersed at the conclusion of his period of impurity but has not yet brought an offering for his atonement, <b>who is permitted</b> to partake <b>of <i>teruma</i>,</b> nevertheless <b>disqualifies sacrificial</b> food if he comes into contact with it, so too, with regard to an item of <b>third-</b>degree impurity status, <b>which disqualifies <i>teruma</i></b> and is therefore more severe than one who is lacking atonement, <b>is it not logical that it should render sacrificial</b> food with which it comes into contact as having <b>fourth-</b>degree impurity?
The <i>baraita</i> concludes: <b>And we have</b> therefore <b>derived</b> that <b>third-</b>degree impurity applies <b>to sacrificial</b> food <b>from</b> an explicit verse in <b>the Torah, and</b> we have derived that <b>fourth-</b>degree impurity applies to sacrificial food <b>by</b> means of the above <b><i>a fortiori</i></b> inference.
The Gemara asks: <b>From where in the Torah do we</b> derive that third-degree impurity applies <b>to sacrificial</b> food? The Gemara answers: <b>As it is written: “And the flesh that touches any impure thing shall not be eaten”</b> (Leviticus 7:19). <b>Are we not dealing</b> in the verse with meat <b>that touches</b> even an item of <b>second-</b>degree impurity? <b>And the Merciful One states</b> that it <b>“shall not be eaten,”</b> indicating that it assumes third-degree impurity. Therefore, <b>fourth-</b>degree impurity can be derived <b>by</b> means of the <b><i>a fortiori</i></b> inference of Rabbi Yosei, <b>as we stated</b> above.
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> With regard to <b>the reasoning</b> behind <b>the Distinguished</b> Rabbi Yosei’s <i>a fortiori</i> inference, <b>I do not know what it is, as the response to his</b> inference <b>is</b> right <b>by its side: Food whose</b> impurity <b>came from</b> contact with <b>one who immersed that day can prove</b> that a degree of impurity that disqualifies <i>teruma</i> does not necessarily impart fourth-degree impurity to sacrificial food, <b>as</b> this food <b>disqualifies <i>teruma</i></b> upon contact, <b>but it does not impart fourth-</b>degree impurity <b>upon sacrificial</b> food.
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Teharot</i> 1:4) that <b>Abba Shaul says:</b> With regard to <b>one who immersed that day,</b> until sunset he is treated as one who is impure with <b>first-</b>degree impurity <b>vis-à-vis sacrificial</b> food, in that he is able <b>to render two</b> items of sacrificial food <b>impure and to disqualify one</b> additional item. In other words, the first item of sacrificial food that he touches assumes the status of a second-degree impurity. A second item that comes into contact with the first one assumes third-degree impurity. A third item that comes into contact with the second assumes fourth-degree impurity and is therefore disqualified from being eaten, though it cannot impart impurity to other items.
<b>Rabbi Meir says:</b> One who immersed that day is considered im-pure with second-degree impurity, even vis-à-vis sacrificial food, and as such <b>renders</b> only <b>one</b> item <b>impure and disqualifies one</b> additional item. <b>And the Rabbis say: Just as he</b> merely <b>disqualifies <i>teruma</i> foods and <i>teruma</i> liquids,</b> without transferring to them impurity that can then be transferred further, <b>so too, he</b> merely <b>disqualifies sacrificial foods and sacrificial liquids.</b> In other words, the impurity imparted by one who immersed that day can reach only third-degree impurity and not fourth-degree impurity, contrary to the opinion of Rabbi Yosei.
<b>Rav Pappa objects to</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan’s argument: <b>From where</b> is the presumption derived <b>that Rabbi Yosei holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis? Perhaps he holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Abba Shaul, who says</b> that one who immersed that day is able <b>to render two</b> items of sacrificial food <b>impure, and to disqualify one</b> additional item.
The Gemara answers: <b>If it enters your mind</b> that Rabbi Yosei <b>holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Abba Shaul, he should have brought</b> proof <b>for</b> the existence of a <b>fourth</b> degree of ritual impurity <b>with regard to sacrificial</b> food <b>from</b> the case of <b>food whose</b> impurity <b>came from one who immersed that day,</b> as follows:
<b>Just as</b> with regard to <b>food whose</b> impurity <b>came from one who immersed that day, while the one who immersed that day is himself permitted to</b> consume <b>non-sacred</b> food, nevertheless <b>you say</b> that the food <b>imparts fourth-</b>degree impurity status <b>upon sacrificial</b> food, then with regard to <b>food</b>

Daf 30a

<b>whose third-</b>degree impurity <b>came from</b> contact with an item of <b>second-</b>degree impurity, in <b>which</b> case the item with the <b>second-</b>degree impurity <b>is itself forbidden,</b> i.e. impure, even if it is <b>non-sacred</b> food, <b>isn’t it logical</b> to infer <b>that it should</b> be able to <b>impart fourth-</b>degree impurity <b>upon sacrificial</b> food?
<b>And if you would say</b> that the reason Rabbi Yosei did not employ this <i>a fortiori</i> inference is <b>because it can be refuted</b> as follows: <b>What</b> is unique <b>about one who immersed that day is that</b> prior to his immersion he was <b>a primary source of impurity,</b> this cannot be, as Rabbi Yosei <b>brought</b> proof for the existence of a fourth degree of impurity <b>from</b> the case of <b>one who has not yet</b> brought an <b>atonement</b> offering, who was also a primary source of impurity prior to his immersion, <b>and</b> Rabbi Yosei clearly <b>did not refute</b> the proof due to this factor. Therefore, the reason Rabbi Yosei did not employ an <i>a fortiori</i> inference from the case of food that contracted impurity from one who immersed that day is clearly that he disagrees with the opinion of Abba Shaul. Consequently, Rabbi Yoḥanan concluded that he cannot understand Rabbi Yosei’s reasoning.
§ <b>Rabbi Asi said</b> that <b>Rav said, and some say Rabba ben Isi said</b> that <b>Rav said: Rabbi Meir, and Rabbi Yosei, and Rabbi Yehoshua, and Rabbi Elazar, and Rabbi Eliezer all hold that</b> an item of second-degree ritual impurity status <b>cannot impart</b> third-degree ritual impurity status <b>to non-sacred items.</b> Rav proceeds to prove this by attributing support from the rulings of each of these <i>tanna’im</i>.
<b>Rabbi Meir</b> is of this opinion, <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Para</i> 11:5): <b>Anything that requires immersion in water by rabbinic law renders sacrificial</b> food <b>impure</b> upon contact, with second-degree impurity, <b>and disqualifies <i>teruma</i>,</b> meaning that it renders the <i>teruma</i> itself impure, but not to the extent that the <i>teruma</i> can render other <i>teruma</i> impure. <b>And</b> anything that requires immersion in water by rabbinic law <b>is permitted for non-sacred</b> food <b>and for</b> the second <b>tithe,</b> i.e., it does not render these items impure. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. But the Rabbis prohibit</b> one who has this degree of impurity from partaking of the second <b>tithe.</b> From the fact that Rabbi Meir permits him to partake of the second tithe, it is inferred that he maintains that an item of second-degree impurity cannot impart third-degree impurity upon non-sacred items.
It is evident that <b>Rabbi Yosei</b> is of this opinion from <b>that which we have stated</b> above, that he derives that sacrificial food can contract fourth-degree impurity, <b>because if he holds</b> that non-sacred items can contract third-degree impurity, <b>he should have derived</b> through his <i>a fortiori</i> inference that there is <b>fourth-</b>degree impurity <b>vis-à-vis <i>teruma</i> and fifth-</b>degree impurity <b>vis-à-vis sacrificial</b> food, since each of these categories has a unique level of impurity.
<b>Rabbi Yehoshua</b> is of this opinion, <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Teharot</i> 2:2): <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: One who eats food</b> with <b>first-</b>degree impurity assumes <b>first-</b>degree impurity. One who eats food with <b>second-</b>degree impurity assumes <b>second-</b>degree impurity. One who eats food with <b>third-</b>degree impurity assumes <b>third-</b>degree impurity. <b>Rabbi Yehoshua says: One who eats food</b> with <b>first-</b>degree impurity <b>or</b> food with <b>second-</b>degree impurity assumes <b>second-</b>degree impurity. One who eats food with <b>third-</b>degree impurity assumes <b>second-</b>degree impurity <b>vis-à-vis sacrificial</b> food, <b>and</b> he does <b>not</b> assume <b>second-</b>degree impurity <b>vis-à-vis <i>teruma</i>.</b>
Eating an item with third-degree impurity is possible only in the case of non-sacred items, as eating impure <i>teruma</i> or sacrificial food is prohibited. However, generic non-sacred food cannot contract third-degree impurity at all. Therefore, the case of one who eats food with third-degree impurity refers specifically <b>to non-sacred</b> food items <b>that were prepared</b> as if their level of purity were <b>on the</b> level of the <b>purity of <i>teruma</i>.</b> By means of a vow, one can establish the purity status of non-sacred food items to be treated on the level of purity necessary for <i>teruma</i>.
The Gemara infers from Rabbi Yehoshua’s statement that <b>yes,</b> one is able to prepare items as if their level of purity were <b>on the</b> level of the <b>purity of <i>teruma</i>;</b> but one is <b>not</b> able to prepare items as if their level of purity were <b>on the</b> level of the <b>purity of sacrificial</b> food, and such items would not contract third-degree impurity.
The Gemara concludes: <b>Apparently,</b> Rabbi Yehoshua <b>holds</b> that an item of second-degree impurity <b>cannot impart</b> third-degree impurity <b>upon</b> ordinary <b>non-sacred items</b> that were not prepared on the level of the purity of <i>teruma</i>.
<b>Rabbi Elazar</b> is of this opinion, <b>as it is taught</b> in a mishna (<i>Teharot</i> 2:7): <b>Rabbi Elazar says:</b> The <b>three of these</b> are <b>equal</b> in their ability to impart ritual impurity to other items: An item of <b>first-</b>degree impurity, whether it is an item <b>of sacrificial</b> food, <b>or of non-sacred</b> food, <b>or of <i>teruma</i>.</b>
<b>With regard to sacrificial</b> food, such an item <b>renders impure two</b> additional levels of contact, enabling the items that contracted ritual impurity from it to transfer that impurity to items that they in turn touch afterward. <b>And</b> it <b>disqualifies one</b> level afterward, imparting upon the food fourth-degree impurity, which cannot impart impurity to a fifth item.
<b>With regard to <i>teruma</i>,</b> an item of first-degree impurity <b>renders impure one</b> additional level of contact, i.e., it imparts second-level impurity to <i>teruma</i> food with which it comes into contact, <b>and</b> that item in turn <b>disqualifies one</b> additional level afterward, as that <i>teruma</i> food imparts third-degree impurity upon <i>teruma</i>.
<b>And with regard to non-sacred</b> food, an item of first-degree impurity merely <b>disqualifies one</b> additional level of non-sacred food. Evidently, non-sacred items cannot go beyond a second-degree impurity.
<b>Rabbi Eliezer</b> also agrees with this principle, <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Ḥalla</i> 2:8): <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: <i>Ḥalla</i> can be taken from ritually pure</b> dough <b>on</b> behalf of <b>ritually impure</b> dough. <b>How so?</b> If there are <b>two batches of dough, one</b> of which is <b>pure and one</b> of which is <b>impure,</b> one <b>takes</b> the required <b>amount of</b> dough for separating <b><i>ḥalla</i></b> for both of the batches <b>from the</b> pure <b>dough when its <i>ḥalla</i> has not</b> yet <b>been separated</b> for itself, <b>and</b> then <b>places less than an egg-bulk</b> of dough, which is not susceptible becoming ritually impure due to its size, <b>in the middle,</b> between the impure dough and the pure dough set aside for being used as the separated <i>ḥalla</i>. This joins all of the dough together, <b>so that</b> one can fulfill the requirement <b>to take</b> dough for separating <i>ḥalla</i> <b>from</b> dough <b>that is situated near</b> the dough it comes to exempt.

Daf 30b

<b>And the Rabbis prohibit</b> separating <i>ḥalla</i> in this manner.
<b>And it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that Rabbi Eliezer even allows the ritually pure dough placed in the middle to be as large as <b>an egg-bulk,</b> even though dough of that size is susceptible to the <i>halakhot</i> of ritual impurity.
