The Gemara further qualifies: <b>And this statement</b> applies in <b>a courtyard</b> adjacent to <b>a grave whose partitions</b> are <b>delineated, but</b> if there is <b>only a corpse</b> lying about without any enclosures, it certainly <b>occupies</b> and affects a surrounding area of four cubits.
§ The mishna teaches among the statements that were said before battle: <b>“What man is there that has betrothed a wife</b> and has not taken her? Let him go and return to his house” (Deuteronomy 20:7). <b>The Sages taught: “That has betrothed”</b> is referring to <b>one who betroths a virgin, and</b> to <b>one who betroths a widow, and</b> to <b>one</b> whose <i>yevama</i> is <b>a widow awaiting her <i>yavam</i>,</b> i.e., this man in the military ranks, to perform levirate marriage. <b>And even</b> if there are <b>five brothers, and one of them dies in the war, they all return</b> for the widow. In addition, the verse could have singled out one who has <b>not taken</b> a wife. When the verse specifies: <b>“and has not taken her,”</b> this <b>excludes</b> men in forbidden marriages: <b>A widow</b> who is betrothed <b>to a High Priest;</b> either <b>a divorcée or a <i>yevama</i> who performed <i>ḥalitza</i> [<i>ḥalutza</i>],</b> who is betrothed <b>to a common priest; a <i>mamzeret</i> or a Gibeonite woman</b> who is betrothed <b>to an Israelite;</b> or <b>an Israelite woman</b> who is betrothed <b>to a <i>mamzer</i> or to a Gibeonite.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Shall we say</b> that this opinion is <b>not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei HaGelili? As, if</b> it were in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei HaGelili, he says:</b> When the verse singles out <b>“fearful and fainthearted”</b> (Deuteronomy 20:8), <b>this</b> is referring to <b>one who is afraid because of sins that he has.</b> According to this interpretation, one who marries a woman forbidden to him should return home from war because of his guilt, which seems to contradict the opinion mentioned above that such a man does not return from the ranks.
The Gemara answers: <b>Even</b> if <b>you say</b> that the ruling is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei HaGelili,</b> such a man does not return, <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabba. As Rabba said:</b> A man is <b>never liable</b> for a forbidden marriage <b>until he engages in sexual intercourse</b> with his wife. With regard to the forbidden marriages of a High Priest, the Torah states: “A widow, or a divorcée, or a profaned woman, or a harlot, these he shall not take…And he shall not profane his seed among his people” (Leviticus 21:14–15). <b>What is the reason</b> that <b>“he shall not take”</b> one of these women as a wife? It is <b>due to: “He shall not profane</b> his seed” by engaging in sexual intercourse. <b>Due to that</b> reason, <b>he is not flogged until he engages in sexual intercourse</b> with a woman who is forbidden to him. Therefore, even according to Rabbi Yosei HaGelili, one who merely betroths a forbidden wife might not return home from the ranks.
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> (<i>Tosefta</i> 7:20-21): The Torah states: “What man is there <b>that has built”</b> (Deuteronomy 20:5), and then <b>“that has planted”</b> (Deuteronomy 20:6), and finally <b>“that has betrothed”</b> (Deuteronomy 20:7). <b>The Torah</b> has <b>taught</b> a person <b>the desired mode of behavior: A person should build a house,</b> then <b>plant a vineyard, and afterward marry a woman. And even</b> King <b>Solomon said in his wisdom: “Prepare your work outside, and make it fit for yourself in the field; and afterward build your house”</b> (Proverbs 24:27). The Sages explained: <b>“Prepare your work outside”; this</b> is <b>a house. “And make it fit for yourself in the field”; this</b> is <b>a vineyard. “And afterward you shall build your house”; this</b> is <b>a wife.</b>
<b>Alternatively,</b> this verse may be understood as relating to Torah study: <b>“Prepare your work outside”; this</b> is the study of <b>Bible. “And make it fit for yourself in the field”; this</b> is the study of <b>Mishna. “Afterward you shall build your house”; this</b> is the study of <b>Gemara,</b> the analysis of and deliberation over the statements of the Sages. <b>Alternatively: “Prepare your work outside”; this</b> is the study of <b>Bible and Mishna. “And make it fit for yourself in the field”; this</b> is the study of <b>Gemara. “Afterward you shall build your house”; these</b> are <b>good deeds. Rabbi Eliezer, son of Rabbi Yosei HaGelili, says: “Prepare your work outside”; this</b> is the study of <b>Bible, and Mishna, and Gemara. “And make it fit for yourself in the field”; these</b> are <b>good deeds. “Afterward you shall build your house”; expound</b> upon new understandings of Torah <b>and receive reward,</b> which is possible only after the initial steps.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And these</b> are the men <b>who do not return</b> from the ranks: <b>One who builds a gateway,</b> or an enclosed veranda, or a balcony…Rabbi Yehuda says: Even one who rebuilds a house as it stood originally would not return. A Sage taught (<i>Tosefta</i> 7:18): <b>If</b> one <b>adds one</b> additional <b>row of stones [<i>dimos</i>] to</b> the original structure, <b>he returns</b> from the ranks. That is enough to render it a new building. The mishna further teaches: <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: Even one who builds a</b> new <b>brick house in the Sharon would not return.</b> A Sage <b>taught:</b> This is <b>because</b> the owners <b>renew it twice in</b> a period of <b>seven years,</b> and it is therefore not considered a permanent structure.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>These</b> are the men <b>who do not</b> even <b>move from their places:</b> One who <b>built a house and dedicated it</b> within the year; one who planted a vineyard and used its fruit for less than a year; one who marries his betrothed and one who marries his <i>yevama</i>, as it is stated: “When a man takes a new wife, he shall not go out with the army, neither shall he be charged with any business; he shall be free for his house one year, and shall cheer his wife whom he has taken” (Deuteronomy 24:5). With respect to a new husband’s exemption, <b>the Sages taught</b> a number of <i>halakhot</i> from this verse. From the term: <b>“A new wife” I have</b> derived <b>only</b> that a man returns only for <b>a new</b> virgin <b>wife. From where</b> do I derive that it applies to <b>a widow or a divorcée? The verse states: “A wife,” in any case. If so, what</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states: “A new wife?”</b> It is referring to <b>one who is new for him, excluding one who remarries his divorcée, who is not new to him.</b>
<b>The Sages taught</b> with regard to the verse: <b>“He shall not go out with the army”</b> (Deuteronomy 24:5); one <b>might</b> have thought it is <b>with the army that he does not go out, but</b> he does go to <b>supply water and food</b> to the army <b>and to repair the roads</b> for them. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Neither shall he be charged with any business.”</b> One <b>might</b> have thought <b>that I include even one who has built a house and has not dedicated it,</b> or <b>one who has planted a vineyard and has not used its fruit,</b> or <b>one who has betrothed a woman and has not taken her</b> as his wife? <b>The verse states:</b> “Neither shall <b>he</b> be charged with any business”; <b>you do not charge him with any</b> responsibilities, <b>but you do charge others.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>And since</b> the Torah <b>states: “Neither shall he be charged</b> with any business,” <b>why do I</b> need to be taught: <b>“He shall not go out with the army”?</b> The Gemara answers: The Torah adds this clause <b>so that he will violate two prohibitions</b> if he goes out to war: “He shall not go out with the army,” and: “Neither shall he be charged.”
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The mishna continues its discussion of the speech given before battle. <b>“And the officers shall speak further to the people,</b> and they shall say: What man is there that is fearful and fainthearted? Let him go and return unto his house” (Deuteronomy 20:8). <b>Rabbi Akiva says: “That is fearful and fainthearted”</b> is to be understood <b>as it indicates, that</b> the man is <b>unable to stand in the battle ranks and to see a drawn sword</b> because it will terrify him. <b>Rabbi Yosei HaGelili says: “That is fearful and fainthearted”; this is one who is afraid because of the sins that he has;</b> he, too, returns. <b>Therefore, the Torah provided him</b> with <b>all these</b> additional reasons for exemption from the army so he can ascribe <b>his leaving to</b> one of <b>them.</b> In this way, the sinner may leave the ranks without having to publicly acknowledge that he is a sinner.
<b>Rabbi Yosei says:</b> With regard to one who has betrothed a woman forbidden to him, including <b>a widow</b> betrothed <b>to a High Priest; a divorcée or a <i>yevama</i> who performed <i>ḥalitza</i></b> [<b><i>ḥalutza</i></b>] betrothed <b>to a common priest; a <i>mamzeret</i> or a Gibeonite woman</b> betrothed <b>to an Israelite;</b> or <b>a daughter of an Israelite</b> betrothed <b>to a <i>mamzer</i> or a Gibeonite; this</b> man <b>is he</b> whom the verse calls <b>“fearful and fainthearted.”</b> He fears that his sin will jeopardize his safety in the war.
The mishna continues its discussion. The verse states: <b>“And it shall be, when the officers conclude speaking to the people, that captains of legions shall be appointed at the head of the people”</b> (Deuteronomy 20:9). The mishna adds: <b>As well as at the rear of the people.</b> The officers <b>station guards [<i>zekifin</i>] in front of them, and other</b> guards <b>behind them, and</b> they have <b>iron rods [<i>kashilin</i>] in their hands. And</b> with regard to <b>anyone who attempts to turn back</b> and flee from the war, the guard <b>has license to beat [<i>lekape’aḥ</i>] his legs</b>

Daf 44b

<b>because the beginning of fleeing</b> is <b>a downfall</b> on the battlefield, <b>as it is stated: “Israel has fled before the Philistines, and there has been also a great slaughter among the people”</b> (I Samuel 4:17), <b>and</b> likewise <b>it says further on: “And the men of Israel fled from before the Philistines, and fell down slain</b> in Mount Gilboa” (I Samuel 31:1).
The mishna adds: <b>In what</b> case <b>are</b> all of <b>these statements,</b> with regard to the various exemptions from war, <b>said?</b> They are said <b>with regard to elective wars. But in wars</b> whose mandate is <b>a mitzva, everyone goes, even a groom from his room and a bride from her wedding canopy. Rabbi Yehuda said: In what</b> case <b>are</b> all of <b>these statements,</b> with regard to the various exemptions from war, <b>said?</b> They are said <b>with regard to wars</b> whose mandate is <b>a mitzva. But in obligatory wars, everyone goes, even a groom from his room and a bride from her wedding canopy.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: With regard to their understanding that the “fearful and fainthearted” is referring to one harboring sins, <b>what</b> difference <b>is there between</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei and</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei HaGelili?</b> The Gemara answers: There is a practical difference <b>between them</b> with regard to <b>a sin</b> which violates a prohibition <b>by rabbinic law.</b> According to Rabbi Yosei HaGelili, one who has violated a rabbinic law returns home, whereas Rabbi Yosei maintains that one returns home only if he has violated a Torah law, as in the case of a priest who has married a divorcée.
The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion <b>is that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If one <b>spoke between</b> donning the <b>phylactery</b> of the arm <b>and</b> the <b>phylactery</b> of the head, he has <b>a sin on his hands, and due to</b> that sin <b>he returns from the ranks of</b> soldiers waging <b>war. In accordance with whose opinion</b> does this man return? It is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei HaGelili,</b> who maintains that one returns even due to a minor transgression.
The Gemara asks: <b>Who</b> is the <i>tanna</i> who <b>taught this</b> <i>halakha</i> <b>that the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If <b>one heard the sound of trumpets and trembled;</b> or he heard <b>the knocking of shields and</b> he <b>trembled;</b> or he heard <b>the sharpening of swords, and urine</b> was <b>trickling down his knees</b> in fear, <b>he returns</b> from the battlefront. <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion is this? <b>Shall we say</b> that <b>it is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva,</b> who interprets “fearful and fainthearted” literally, <b>and</b> it is <b>not</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei HaGelili?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>In this</b> case <b>even Rabbi Yosei HaGelili</b> would <b>concede</b> that he should return, <b>because it is written:</b> “What man is there that is fearful and fainthearted? Let him go and return…<b>lest his brethren’s heart melt as his heart”</b> (Deuteronomy 20:8). Someone so clearly frightened invariably spreads his fear to those around him.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>“And it shall be, when the officers conclude</b> speaking to the people, that captains of legions shall be appointed at the head of the people” (Deuteronomy 20:9), and that the guards have the license to beat the legs of anyone who attempts to turn back and flee from the war, because the beginning of fleeing is a downfall. The Gemara is puzzled by the language of the mishna: <b>This</b> phrase: <b>Because the beginning of fleeing</b> is <b>a downfall,</b> appears to be backward. The mishna <b>should have</b> said the opposite: <b>Because the beginning of the downfall</b> is the act of <b>fleeing.</b> The Gemara concedes: Indeed, <b>say</b> that the mishna means: <b>Because the beginning of the downfall</b> is the act of <b>fleeing.</b>
The mishna teaches: <b>In what</b> case <b>are these statements said?</b> They are said <b>with regard to elective wars,</b> as opposed to obligatory wars or wars whose mandate is a mitzva. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says</b> concerning the various categories of war: The <b>elective</b> war referenced <b>by the Rabbis is</b> the same as a war whose mandate is <b>a mitzva</b> referenced <b>by Rabbi Yehuda,</b> and the war that is <b>a mitzva</b> mentioned <b>by the Rabbis is</b> the same as the <b>obligatory</b> war mentioned <b>by Rabbi Yehuda.</b> Therefore, Rabbi Yehuda is merely stating that the wars which the Rabbis call elective are to be seen as mandated by a mitzva.
<b>Rava said:</b> With respect to <b>the wars</b> that <b>Joshua</b> waged <b>to conquer</b> Eretz Yisrael, <b>all agree</b> that they were <b>obligatory.</b> With respect to <b>the wars</b> waged by <b>the House of</b> King <b>David for</b> the sake of <b>territorial expansion, all agree</b> that they were <b>elective</b> wars. <b>When they disagree,</b> it is with regard to preventative wars that are waged <b>to reduce the gentiles so</b> that they will <b>not come</b> and wage war <b>against them.</b> One <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Yehuda, <b>called this</b> type of war <b>a mitzva, and</b> one <b>Sage,</b> the Rabbis, <b>called</b> it <b>an elective</b> war. There is <b>a practical difference</b> between these opinions with respect <b>to</b> the principle: <b>One who is engaged in a mitzva is exempt from</b> performing another <b>mitzva.</b> According to Rabbi Yehuda, one fighting in this kind of war is exempt from performing another mitzva.

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> In certain cases of unsolved murder, the Torah prescribes a ritual performed with <b>a heifer whose neck is broken.</b> During the course of this ritual, the judges say a confession <b>in the sacred tongue,</b> Hebrew, <b>as it is stated</b> in the verse: <b>“If one be found slain in the land</b> which the Lord your God has given you to possess it, lying in the field, and it is not known who has smitten him; <b>then your Elders and your judges shall come forth”</b> (Deuteronomy 21:1–2). What is the procedure for this ritual? <b>Three members of the High Court [Sanhedrin] that is in Jerusalem would go out</b> to see the corpse. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: Five</b> would go out, <b>as it is stated: “Your Elders,”</b> in the plural form, indicating at least <b>two;</b> and it is written: <b>“And your judges,”</b> in the plural form, indicating another <b>two</b> judges; <b>and a court may not</b> be comprised of <b>an even</b> number of judges because they need to be able to issue a majority ruling. Consequently, <b>they add to them one more</b> Elder.
If the corpse <b>was found concealed in a pile</b> of stones, <b>or hanging on a tree, or floating on the surface of the water,</b> then the judges <b>would not break the neck</b> of the heifer, <b>as it is stated:</b> “If one be found slain <b>in the land”</b> (Deuteronomy 21:1), <b>and not concealed in a pile</b> of stones; <b>“lying”</b> on the ground <b>and not hanging on a tree; “in the field,” and not floating on the surface of the water.</b>
If a corpse <b>was found close to the border</b> of the country, <b>or</b> close <b>to a city in which the majority of its</b> inhabitants <b>are gentiles, or</b> close <b>to a city that is without</b> a rabbinical <b>court</b> of twenty-three judges, then the judges <b>would not break the</b> heifer’s <b>neck.</b> Additionally, the Elders <b>measure</b> the distance from the corpse <b>only to a city that has</b> a rabbinical <b>court</b> with twenty-three judges.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the first sentence in the mishna <b>saying?</b> The mishna is attempting to prove that the verses read during the ritual of breaking a heifer’s neck are to be recited in Hebrew, yet the verse does not offer any proof of this. <b>Rabbi Abbahu said</b> that <b>this is what</b> the mishna <b>is saying:</b> The confession is recited in Hebrew, <b>as it is stated</b> with regard to the ritual of the heifer whose neck is broken: <b>“And they shall speak and say”</b> (Deuteronomy 21:7), <b>and later it is stated</b> with regard to the curses stated on Mount Ebal: <b>“And the Levities shall speak and say”</b> (Deuteronomy 27:14). <b>Just as</b> the word <b>“speak” that is said later</b> with regard to the Levites is referring to a speech that <b>is</b> recited <b>in the sacred tongue, so too here</b> the declaration is recited <b>in the sacred tongue.</b>
The mishna continues to answer the question of: <b>And how</b> is the ritual of the <b>heifer whose neck is broken ordered?</b> The mishna states: It is written in the Torah: <b>“If one be found slain in the land</b> which the Lord your God has given you to possess it, lying in the field, and it is not known who has smitten him; <b>then your Elders and your judges shall come forth,</b> and they shall measure unto the cities which are round about him that is slain” (Deuteronomy 21:1–2). <b>Three members of the High Court that is in Jerusalem would go forth. Rabbi Yehuda says: Five.</b>
<b>The Sages taught: “And your Elders and judges shall come forth.” “Your Elders”</b> indicates <b>two; “and your judges”</b> also indicates <b>two; and a court may not</b> be composed of <b>an even</b> number of judges, so <b>they add to them one more</b> judge. Therefore, <b>there are five</b> judges. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda. Rabbi Shimon says: “Your Elders”</b> indicates <b>two, and</b> as <b>a court may not</b> consist of <b>an even</b> number of judges <b>they add to them one more.</b> Therefore, <b>there are three.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> according to <b>Rabbi Shimon as well, isn’t it written: “And your judges”;</b> why does he not agree that this indicates a need for two more judges? The Gemara answers: <b>He requires that</b> additional word “Elders” in order <b>to</b> teach that they must be <b>the distinguished among your judges,</b> i.e., judges from the Sanhedrin. <b>And</b> how does <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> who derives from the phrase “and your judges” that there is a need for two additional judges, know that they need to be from the Sanhedrin? <b>He derives it from</b> an extra letter. The verse could have simply stated: <b>The Elders.</b> Instead, it adds a letter and states: <b>“Your Elders,”</b> to teach that they must be the distinguished among the Elders of Israel.
<b>And Rabbi Shimon</b> could say: <b>If the Merciful One had written</b> in the Torah: <b>The Elders, I would say</b> that it includes <b>even the Elders of the marketplace,</b> meaning any honorable people. Therefore, <b>the Merciful One writes</b> in the Torah: <b>“Your Elders,”</b> to indicate specifically Torah Sages, who are revered by all. <b>And if the Merciful One had written</b> in the Torah only: <b>“Your Elders,” I would say</b> that it includes <b>even</b> Elders from <b>a lesser Sanhedrin.</b> Therefore, <b>the Merciful One</b> also <b>writes: “And your judges,” to</b> teach that they must be <b>the distinguished among your judges,</b> from the Great Sanhedrin.
<b>And</b> what is the source of <b>Rabbi Yehuda</b> that the judges may not come from a lesser Sanhedrin? <b>He derives</b> this <i>halakha</i> from a verbal analogy between <b>“Elders,”</b> written here, and <b>“Elders,”</b> written with regard to the offering that the Sanhedrin brings when the nation has sinned as a result of a mistaken ruling. This offering is brought by <b>“the Elders of the congregation”</b> (Leviticus 4:15). <b>Just as there,</b> “the Elders of the congregation” are <b>the distinguished among the congregation,</b> from the Great Sanhedrin, <b>so too, here</b> the verse is referring to <b>the distinguished among the congregation.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>If he derives</b> this <i>halakha</i> from this verbal analogy, then <b>he should derive the entire matter from there,</b> including the requirement for five judges. If so, <b>why do I</b> need the phrase <b>“your Elders and your judges”? Rather,</b> it must be that Rabbi Yehuda does not derive the <i>halakha</i> from this verbal analogy. Instead, he understands that the phrase “your Elders” indicates two, and that “your judges” indicates that they must be the distinguished among your judges, and the letter <b><i>vav</i></b> in the phrase <b>“and your judges [<i>veshofetekha</i>]” is to</b> add two more to <b>the count</b> of the judges. <b>And Rabbi Shimon</b> does not accept this derivation

Daf 45a

because <b>he does not learn</b> anything from the letter <b><i>vav</i>.</b> Rather, <b>what is the Merciful One saying</b> in the Torah with the phrase “and your judges”? He is referring to <b>the distinguished that are among your judges.</b> The Gemara raises an objection: <b>However, if that is so,</b> if every plural form in the verse adds another two judges, then when it later states: <b>“And they shall go out,”</b> this should indicate another <b>two.</b> And: <b>“And they shall measure”</b> (Deuteronomy 21:2), should indicate yet another <b>two.</b> This means that <b>according to</b> the reasoning of <b>Rabbi Yehuda there are nine</b> judges enumerated <b>here,</b> while <b>according to</b> the reasoning of <b>Rabbi Shimon there are seven</b> judges enumerated <b>here.</b>
The Gemara responds: <b>He requires that</b> verse: “And they shall go out…and they shall measure,” <b>for that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>“And they shall go out,”</b> serves to emphasize that <b>they</b> themselves go out, <b>and not their agents,</b> i.e., they may not appoint an agent to measure the distance but must do it themselves. <b>“And they shall measure,”</b> teaches <b>that even</b> if the corpse <b>was found clearly</b> near <b>to</b> a particular <b>city they would</b> nevertheless <b>measure</b> the distance, <b>as</b> it is <b>a mitzva to engage in</b> this act of <b>measurement.</b>
§ The Gemara comments: <b>The mishna is not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov says: “Your Elders,” this is the Sanhedrin; “your judges,” this is the king and the High Priest.</b> According to Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov, the king and the High Priest need to participate in the ritual of breaking the neck of the heifer. From where is it learned that they are called judges? <b>A king</b> is called a judge, <b>as it is written: “A king by justice establishes the land”</b> (Proverbs 29:4). <b>A High Priest</b> is called a judge, <b>as it is written:</b> “If there arise a matter too hard for you in judgment…<b>And you shall come to the priests the Levites, and to the judge who shall be</b> in those days” (Deuteronomy 17:8–9).
<b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: Is it so that <b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov disagrees</b> only <b>with regard to a king and a High Priest,</b> contending that they too must be present at the measurement, <b>but</b> that <b>with regard to the Sanhedrin he holds either in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda</b> that five Elders are needed <b>or in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon,</b> that three suffice? <b>Or, perhaps he also disagrees with regard to the Sanhedrin</b> and claims that the ritual is not performed <b>unless there is the entire Sanhedrin</b> participating.
<b>Rav Yosef said: Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof from a <i>baraita</i>: If <b>a rebellious Elder found</b> the Sanhedrin not in the Hewn Chamber, which was their usual place, but <b>in Beit Pagei,</b> near Jerusalem, <b>and he rebelled against them</b> there, by teaching in opposition to their ruling, one <b>might</b> have thought <b>his rebellion should be</b> considered <b>a rebellion,</b> rendering him liable to punishment. <b>The verse</b> therefore <b>states: “And you shall arise and go up to the place”</b> (Deuteronomy 17:8), which <b>teaches that</b> it is <b>the place</b> where the Sanhedrin resides that <b>causes</b> the <i>halakha</i> of a rebellious Elder to take effect.
The Gemara analyzes this statement: In this case, <b>how many</b> members of the Sanhedrin were there <b>who went out</b> to Beit Pagei? <b>If we say that</b> only <b>a minority of them went out,</b> why should he be considered a rebellious Elder? <b>Perhaps those</b> judges <b>who are inside</b> the Hewn Chamber <b>hold in accordance with his</b> opinion, which would mean that the Elder in question ruled with the majority. <b>Rather,</b> it <b>is obvious that they all went out.</b>
The assumption that they all went out leads to the following question: <b>And for what</b> purpose did they all leave the Hewn Chamber? <b>If</b> it was <b>for an optional matter, may they leave? But isn’t it written: “Your navel [<i>shorerekh</i>] is like a round goblet, let no mingled wine be wanting”</b> (Song of Songs 7:3). This verse is interpreted as referring to the Sanhedrin, the ministers [<i>sarei</i>] of Israel who sit in a semicircle, like half of a round goblet. The words “let no mingled wine be wanting” teach <b>that if one of</b> the great Sanhedrin <b>needed to leave,</b> then <b>if there are</b> still present in the Hewn Chamber <b>twenty-three</b> members, <b>corresponding</b> to the number of <b>a lesser Sanhedrin, he may leave; and if not, he may not leave.</b> This indicates that it is prohibited for the entire Sanhedrin to leave for an optional matter.
<b>Rather</b> it <b>is obvious</b> that they left <b>for a matter of a mitzva. For what</b> particular mitzva did they venture out? <b>Is it not for the measurement</b> associated <b>with</b> the ritual of <b>the heifer, and it is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov?</b> This would prove that Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov holds that the entire Sanhedrin goes out to measure the distance from the corpse to the nearby cities. <b>Abaye said to</b> Rav Yosef: <b>No,</b> this is not a proof, as <b>perhaps</b> they went out for a different mitzva, <b>to expand the city</b> of Jerusalem <b>or the courtyards</b> of the Temple, <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Sanhedrin</i> 2a): <b>They may expand</b> the sanctified area of <b>the city</b> of Jerusalem <b>or</b> of <b>the</b> Temple <b>courtyards only with the court of seventy-one.</b>
It <b>is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rav Yosef,</b> that Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov disagrees even with regard to the number of judges: If the rebellious Elder <b>found</b> the Sanhedrin <b>in Beit Pagei and rebelled against them, for example, if they went out for the measurement</b> of the ritual <b>of the heifer or to expand</b> the area of <b>the city or</b> that of <b>the courtyards,</b> one <b>might</b> have thought that <b>his rebellion should be a rebellion.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states</b> with regard to the requirement to follow the rulings of the Sanhedrin: <b>“And you shall arise and go up to the place”</b> (Deuteronomy 17:8). This <b>teaches that the place causes</b> the <i>halakha</i> of a rebellious Elder to take effect, indicating that the entire Sanhedrin participates in the measurement of the distance from the corpse to the cities.
§ The mishna teaches: If the corpse <b>was found concealed in a pile</b> of stones <b>or hanging on a tree,</b> they would not perform the ritual of the heifer. The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say that the mishna is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda and not</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis.</b> This is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to the <i>halakha</i> that sheaves of grain forgotten in the fields must be left for the poor: <b>“And you forget a sheaf in the field”</b> (Deuteronomy 24:19). This <b>excludes a concealed</b> sheaf; it is not considered forgotten and it may be collected by the owner, even if he did forget it. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda. And the Rabbis say:</b> The phrase <b>“in the field”</b> serves <b>to include the concealed</b> sheaves, and these must be left for the poor. Rabbi Yehuda holds, like the ruling in the mishna here, that when the verse states “in the field” it excludes a concealed corpse.
<b>Rav said: You</b> can <b>even say</b> that the mishna is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> as <b>here</b> they expound the <i>halakha</i> <b>based on the context of the verse,</b> and <b>there</b> too they expound the <i>halakha</i> <b>based on the context of the verse.</b>
In this case, <b>as it is written: “If one be found slain”</b> (Deuteronomy 21:1), the default assumption is that the <i>halakha</i> applies no matter <b>where it is found.</b> When the verse then states: <b>“In the ground,”</b> it must be serving to <b>exclude</b> a matter, i.e., <b>a concealed</b> corpse. <b>And</b> similarly, <b>there,</b> in the case of forgotten sheaves, they also expound <b>based on the context of the verse, as it is written: “When you reap your harvest in your field, and you forget a sheaf”</b> (Deuteronomy 24:19). This indicates that <b>forgotten</b> sheaves <b>are similar to</b> those <b>of</b> the <b>harvest: Just as</b> the <b>harvest</b> is performed <b>with revealed</b> objects, i.e., the growing grain, <b>so too,</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>forgetting</b> pertain <b>to revealed</b> sheaves. Consequently, when <b>the Merciful One writes</b> in the Torah <b>“in the field,”</b> it must be in order <b>to include the concealed</b> sheaves.
In light of this explanation, the Gemara asks: <b>According to Rabbi Yehuda as well, let him derive</b> that covered sheaves are not included <b>from</b> the fact that <b>forgotten</b> sheaves <b>are similar to</b> those <b>of</b> the <b>harvest.</b> Why does he have to derive it from the words “in the field”? The Gemara answers: <b>Yes, it is indeed so,</b> he does not derive that <i>halakha</i> from the phrase “in the field.”
The Gemara therefore inquires: <b>But</b> then <b>why do I</b> need the phrase <b>“in the field,”</b> which indicates either an exclusion or an inclusion? The Gemara answers: <b>He requires</b> it in order <b>to include the forgotten</b> stalks <b>of standing</b> grain. Produce that one forgot to reap is considered forgotten, even if it is still attached to the ground. The Gemara asks: <b>And the Rabbis, from where</b> do they learn the <i>halakha</i> of <b>forgotten</b> stalks <b>of standing</b> grain? The Gemara answers: <b>They derive</b> it <b>from</b> the verse: <b>“When you reap your harvest in your field,</b> and you forget,” which indicates that the <i>halakha</i> includes one who forgot to harvest part of his field.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> what does <b>Rabbi Yehuda</b> derive from this verse? The Gemara answers: It is <b>necessary for him</b> in order <b>to</b> learn <b>that which Rabbi Abbahu says</b> that <b>Rabbi Elazar</b> says, <b>as Rabbi Abbahu says</b> that <b>Rabbi Elazar says:</b> This verse <b>excludes sheaves that floated</b> through the air from his field <b>into another’s field.</b> Such sheaves are not classified as forgotten and may be retrieved later by the owner. <b>And the Rabbis,</b> from where do they derive this <i>halakha</i>? They derive it <b>from</b> the fact that the verse could have stated: <b>In the field,</b> but instead states <b>“in your field,”</b> to include sheaves situated only in one’s own field. The Gemara continues to ask: <b>And</b> what does <b>Rabbi Yehuda</b> learn from this phrase? The Gemara answers: <b>He does not learn</b> anything from the difference between: <b>In the field,</b> and <b>“in your field,”</b> as he holds it is not a significant difference.
<b>Rabbi Yirmeya raises a dilemma:</b> If <b>sheaves floated into his field,</b> i.e., if one sheaf landed on another sheaf or on another item, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is the <b>airspace of a field considered to be like</b> it is the <b>field</b> itself, in which case one sheaf on top of another meets the legal requirements to be deemed forgotten? <b>Or,</b> is it <b>not considered like</b> the <b>field,</b> in which case one sheaf on top of another does not meet the legal requirements to be deemed forgotten?
<b>Rav Kahana said to Rav Pappi, and some say Rav Kahana</b> said it <b>to Rav Zevid: Let him resolve</b> the dilemma <b>from</b> the statement <b>of Rabbi Abbahu,</b> who <b>says that Rabbi Elazar says:</b> This <b>excludes sheaves that floated into another’s field.</b> If they floated into <b>another’s field, yes,</b> Rabbi Elazar said that they are not deemed forgotten, <b>but</b> if they floated <b>into his</b> own <b>field,</b> they are <b>not</b> discussed by Rabbi Elazar, and therefore they are deemed forgotten.
The Gemara refutes this proof: <b>And according to your reasoning</b> that the <i>halakha</i> of the <i>baraita</i> is limited to the case stated, one could infer: In a case where the sheaves drifted <b>into another’s field,</b> then if <b>they were floating, yes,</b> they are not deemed to be forgotten, but if <b>they were resting</b> directly on the ground in another’s field, <b>no,</b> they are deemed to be forgotten. <b>But</b> this cannot be the <i>halakha</i>, as <b>we require</b> the sheaves to be <b>“in your field,” and</b> these sheaves are <b>not</b> in his field.
<b>Rather,</b> you must admit that the words: <b>Into another’s field,</b> include not only those sheaves which are lying on other items, <b>but even</b> those <b>resting</b> directly on the ground. <b>And</b> the reason <b>that</b> Rabbi Abbahu <b>states: Floated,</b> is that <b>you find</b> a situation where sheaves end up in the field of another <b>only in</b> a case where <b>they floated</b> through the air and fell there. Since the term floated was not used to teach a <i>halakha</i>, no inference can be made from this <i>baraita</i> with regard to sheaves that floated in one’s own field.
The Gemara again attempts to resolve the dilemma: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof from a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Pe’a</i> 3:7): In the case of <b>a sheaf that had been held</b> by its owner in order <b>to take it to the city, and he placed it on top of another</b> sheaf, <b>and he forgot</b> both sheaves, <b>the lower</b> sheaf is deemed to be <b>a forgotten sheaf, and the upper one is not</b> deemed to be <b>a forgotten sheaf. Rabbi Shimon ben Yehuda says in the name of Rabbi Shimon: Both of them are not</b> deemed to be <b>forgotten sheaves; the lower one because it is concealed, and the upper one because it is floating</b> and not directly touching the field. The Gemara points out: <b>They disagree only with regard to</b> the <b>lower</b> sheaf, <b>but with regard to</b> the <b>upper</b> one <b>all agree</b> that <b>it is not</b> deemed <b>a forgotten sheaf.</b> This demonstrates that a sheaf located in the airspace of the field is not deemed to be forgotten.
The Gemara rejects this proof: <b>There it is different, as, since</b> the owner <b>had held it</b> to take it to the city, <b>he acquired it.</b> The reason it is not deemed to be a forgotten sheaf is not because it is not touching the field but because the owner had already acquired that particular sheaf before forgetting it. The Gemara raises an objection to this explanation: <b>If so, why specifically</b> state the case of a sheaf that was placed <b>on top of another? The same would hold true</b> in a case where one put the sheaf down directly <b>in the field as well,</b> as he has already acquired it. The Gemara answers: <b>Yes, it is indeed so, and</b> the reason <b>that</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches: On top of another,</b> is <b>due to the lower</b> sheaf rather than the upper one, in order to teach the dispute concerning the lower sheaf.
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> Rabbi Shimon <b>said: Because it is floating.</b> How can one then say that the reason for his ruling is because the owner of the field has acquired it? The Gemara answers: <b>Say</b> instead: <b>Because it is like</b> it is <b>floating.</b> In other words, it is as though the upper sheaf had not been put down at all, but remains in the owner’s hands.
The Gemara tells of a related incident. On one occasion <b>Abaye said: I am hereby</b> as sharp and expert <b>as ben Azzai,</b> who taught Torah <b>in the markets of Tiberias,</b> and am ready to answer any question that might be posed to me. <b>One of the Sages said to Abaye,</b> with regard to the ritual of the heifer whose neck is broken: If there were <b>two slain people</b> found <b>one on top of the other</b> not precisely aligned, <b>from where does one measure</b> the distance to the surrounding cities?
The various aspects of the dilemma are as follows: <b>Is a substance</b> in contact <b>with</b> the <b>same</b> type of <b>substance</b> considered to be <b>concealed, and is a substance</b> in contact <b>with</b> the <b>same</b> type of <b>substance not</b> considered to be <b>floating,</b> which would mean that the lower, concealed corpse is ignored because one does not measure the distance from a concealed corpse, as taught in the mishna, <b>and one measures</b> the distance <b>from the upper one? Or perhaps a substance</b> in contact <b>with</b> the <b>same</b> type of <b>substance is</b> considered to be <b>floating, and a substance</b> in contact <b>with</b> the <b>same</b> type of <b>substance is not</b> considered to be <b>concealed, and he measures</b> the distance <b>from the lower one,</b> because it is not considered to be concealed, but not from the upper one, which is considered to be floating.
<b>Or perhaps a substance</b> in contact <b>with</b> the <b>same</b> type of <b>substance is</b> considered to be <b>concealed, and a substance</b> in contact <b>with</b> the <b>same</b> type of <b>substance is</b> considered to be <b>floating, and he</b> therefore <b>does not measure</b> the distance <b>from the lower one nor does he measure</b> the distance <b>from the upper one.</b> According to this last option, in such a situation no measuring is done, and the ritual is not performed.
Abaye <b>said to him:</b>

Daf 45b

<b>You learned it</b> in the <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Pe’a</i> 3:7): In the case of <b>a sheaf that had been held</b> by its owner in order <b>to take it to the city, and he placed it on top of another</b> sheaf, <b>and he forgot</b> both sheaves, <b>the lower</b> sheaf is deemed to be <b>a forgotten sheaf, and the upper one is not</b> deemed to be <b>a forgotten sheaf. Rabbi Shimon ben Yehuda says in the name of Rabbi Shimon: Both of them are not</b> deemed to be <b>forgotten sheaves; the lower one because it is concealed and the upper one because it is floating</b> and not directly touching the field. The Sages <b>assumed that these <i>tanna’im</i></b> in the <i>baraita</i> basically <b>hold in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> who <b>says</b> that the phrase <b>“in the field”</b> serves <b>to exclude a concealed</b> sheaf.
The Gemara continues Abaye’s statement: <b>What, is it not</b> the case <b>that they disagree about this, as</b> one <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Shimon, <b>holds</b> that <b>a substance</b> in contact <b>with</b> the <b>same</b> type of <b>substance is</b> considered to be <b>concealed,</b> and therefore the lower sheaf is not deemed to be a forgotten sheaf, <b>and</b> one <b>Sage,</b> the Rabbis, <b>holds</b> that <b>it is not</b> considered to be <b>concealed,</b> which means it is deemed to be a forgotten sheaf. The same dispute would apply to the two corpses.
The Gemara refutes this claim: <b>No, if they hold in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> then it could be <b>that everyone agrees</b> that <b>a substance</b> in contact <b>with</b> the <b>same</b> type of <b>substance is</b> considered to be <b>concealed,</b> and they would maintain this with regard to the two corpses as well. <b>And here they disagree with regard to</b> the issue that is the subject of <b>the dispute of Rabbi Yehuda and the Rabbis,</b> in <b>that</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis</b> of this <i>baraita</i> is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis</b> there, which claims that the <i>halakha</i> of a forgotten sheaf applies even to a concealed sheaf, <b>and Rabbi Shimon ben Yehuda</b> holds <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> who maintains that a concealed sheaf is exempt from the <i>halakha</i> of forgotten sheaves.
The Gemara asks: <b>If</b> that is <b>so,</b> that their dispute is with regard to the <i>halakha</i> of concealed sheaves, <b>why</b> did they <b>specifically</b> disagree in the case of a sheaf that was <b>on top of another</b> sheaf; <b>the same would hold true even</b> in a case where the sheaf was concealed <b>in dirt and pebbles?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Yes, it is indeed so.</b> They disagree with regard to all concealed sheaves, <b>and</b> their dispute is stated with regard to a case of one sheaf on top of another in order <b>to convey to you the far-reaching</b> nature of the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yehuda, who says</b> that <b>even a substance</b> that is in contact <b>with</b> the <b>same</b> type of <b>substance is</b> considered <b>concealed.</b> Therefore, a substance concealed in a different type of matter is all the more so considered concealed.
§ The Gemara returns to discuss when the ritual of breaking the neck of the heifer is performed. <b>The Sages taught,</b> expounding the verse “If one be found slain in the land which the Lord your God has given you to possess it, lying in the field” (Deuteronomy 21:1): <b>“Slain”</b> indicates one killed by a sword, <b>but not</b> one who was <b>strangled; “slain,” but not</b> one who was found <b>twitching</b> in his death throes; <b>“in the land,” but not concealed in a pile</b> of stones; <b>“lying,” but not hanging on a tree; “in the field,” but not floating on the surface of the water. Rabbi Elazar says: In all these</b> cases, <b>if</b> a person <b>was slain</b> by the sword, the judges <b>break the neck</b> of the heifer, and it does not matter where the corpse was found.
<b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Yehuda says</b> that the Sages <b>said to Rabbi Elazar: Do you not concede that if he was strangled and left in a garbage</b> heap, <b>that they do not break the</b> heifer’s <b>neck? Apparently, “slain”</b> is a precise term that means slain <b>but not strangled.</b> If you accept that, <b>here too</b> the words <b>“in the land”</b> should indicate: In the land, <b>but not concealed in a pile</b> of stones; <b>“fallen”</b> should indicate: Fallen <b>but not hanging on a tree;</b> and <b>“in the field”</b> should indicate in the field <b>but not floating on the surface of the water. And Rabbi Elazar</b> holds that those other situations are not excluded, and that because in that first case the Torah <b>writes “slain” an extra</b> time in the next verse: “About him that is slain” (Deuteronomy 21:2), this repetition teaches that a victim of strangulation is not included in this <i>halakha</i>.
§ The mishna taught: If a corpse <b>was found close to the border</b> of the country <b>or</b> close <b>to a city in which the majority</b> of its inhabitants <b>are gentiles,</b> the judges would not break the heifer’s neck, <b>as it is written: “If one be found</b> slain” (Deuteronomy 21:1). This <b>excludes</b> places where murdered bodies <b>are</b> commonly <b>found,</b> such as the aforementioned locations.
The mishna taught: <b>Or</b> if the victim was discovered close <b>to a city that is without</b> a rabbinical <b>court</b> of twenty-three judges, they would not measure the distance to that city. The Gemara explains: This is <b>because</b> the verse <b>requires “the Elders of that city”</b> (Deuteronomy 21:3), <b>and</b> this is <b>not</b> the case here; therefore the rite was not performed. The mishna also taught that the Elders <b>measure</b> the distance from the corpse <b>only to a city</b> that contains a rabbinical court of twenty-three judges. The Gemara asks: This <b>is obvious. Since</b> the mishna <b>taught</b> that they do not measure the distance <b>to a city that does not have</b> a rabbinical <b>court</b> of twenty-three judges, <b>I know</b> that <b>they measure</b> the distance <b>only to a city that has</b> a rabbinical <b>court</b> of twenty-three judges.
The Gemara answers: <b>This</b> <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches us</b> the <i>halakha</i> <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>From where</b> is it derived <b>that if</b> the corpse <b>was found close to a city that does not have</b> a rabbinical <b>court</b> of twenty-three judges, <b>that they leave</b> the city aside <b>and measure</b> the distance from the corpse <b>to a city that has</b> a rabbinical <b>court</b> of twenty-three judges? <b>The verse states: “And the Elders of that city shall take”</b> (Deuteronomy 21:3), which indicates that the Elders of a city are involved <b>in any case,</b> and the measurement is taken even if it is not to the city closest to the body.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If the slain person <b>is found precisely between two cities,</b> the inhabitants of <b>the two of them bring two heifers</b> total; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Eliezer. And</b> the inhabitants of <b>Jerusalem do not bring a heifer whose neck is broken,</b> even if Jerusalem is the city closest to the slain victim. If the <b>head of</b> the corpse <b>was found in one place and his body</b> was found <b>in a different place, they bring the head next to the body;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Eliezer. Rabbi Akiva says:</b> They bring <b>the body next to the head. From where</b> on the body <b>would they measure</b> the distance? <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: From his navel. Rabbi Akiva says: From his nose. Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov says: From the place where he became a slain</b> person, which is <b>from the neck.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reasoning of Rabbi Eliezer,</b> that when a body is found precisely between two cities, the inhabitants of each city bring a heifer? His ruling is based on two factors. First, <b>he holds</b> that <b>it is possible to measure precisely</b> and that it is a real possibility to determine that both cities are exactly the same distance from the corpse. <b>And</b> second, he interprets the term <b>“That is nearest”</b> (Deuteronomy 21:3), to be referring not only to one city. It can <b>even</b> be understood as: <b>That are nearest,</b> so that the <i>halakhot</i> apply to more than one city.
The mishna taught: <b>And</b> the inhabitants of <b>Jerusalem do not bring a heifer whose neck is broken.</b> The Gemara explains: This is <b>because the verse states:</b> “If one be found slain in the land which the Lord your God has given <b>you to possess it”</b> (Deuteronomy 21:1), <b>and</b> this <i>tanna</i> <b>holds</b> that <b>Jerusalem was not divided among the tribes</b> in the division of Eretz Yisrael. It was not given as a possession to any particular person but belongs to all; therefore the <i>halakha</i> of the heifer whose neck is broken does not apply to it.
§ With regard to the <i>halakha</i> of a corpse whose <b>head was found in</b> one <b>place</b> and its body elsewhere, the Gemara asks: <b>With regard to what</b> <i>halakha</i> <b>do they disagree? If we say they disagree with regard to</b> whether the <b>measurement</b> is taken from the head or the body, <b>from</b> the fact <b>that the latter clause teaches: From where would they measure</b> the distance, <b>it may be inferred that</b> in <b>the first clause we are not dealing with measurement. Rabbi Yitzḥak said: They disagree with regard to</b> a different matter, the question of whether <b>a corpse with no one to bury it [<i>met mitzva</i>] acquires its place,</b> meaning if an unattended corpse must be buried where it is found.
<b>And this is what</b> the mishna <b>is saying:</b> With regard <b>to burying him,</b> the victim <b>acquires his place,</b> and he is buried there. The mishna continues: <b>And</b> in a case <b>where his head is found in one place and his body</b> is found <b>in a different place, they bring the head next to the body</b> and bury him there; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Eliezer. Rabbi Akiva says:</b> They bring <b>the body next to the head.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>With regard to what do they disagree?</b> They both agree that he should be buried in the place where he was killed, but one <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Eliezer, <b>holds</b> that <b>his body fell in its place,</b> and it was <b>the head that rolled away and fell. And</b> one <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Akiva, <b>holds</b> that <b>his head fell where it fell,</b> and <b>it was the body that went and continued</b> onward. Therefore, the body is brought to the head.
§ The mishna taught that there is a dispute concerning the question: <b>From where</b> on the body <b>would they measure</b> the distance? The Gemara asks: <b>With regard to what do they disagree?</b> One <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Akiva, <b>holds:</b> A person’s <b>life is</b> sustained <b>mainly in his nose,</b> in his respiratory system. <b>And</b> one <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Eliezer, <b>holds: His life is mainly in</b> the area of <b>his navel,</b> in his digestive system.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Shall we say that these <i>tanna’im</i> are like those <i>tanna’im</i>,</b> who had a dispute as it is taught in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>From where is an embryo formed? From its head, and so</b> the verse <b>states: “Out of my mother’s womb You pulled me [<i>gozi</i>]”</b> (Psalms 71:6). <b>And</b> the proof that “<i>gozi</i>” is referring to the head is from the verse that <b>states: “Cut off [<i>gozi</i>] your hair, and cast it away”</b> (Jeremiah 7:29). In this verse, the term <i>gozi</i> relates to the hair of the head. <b>Abba Shaul says:</b> An embryo is formed <b>from its navel, and it sends its roots forth.</b> This dispute concerning the initial formation of an embryo also appears to depend on where the main source of life in a person is.
The Gemara refutes this comparison: <b>You</b> can <b>even say</b> that both <i>tanna’im</i> of the mishna agree with <b>Abba Shaul,</b> as <b>Abba Shaul says</b> his opinion <b>only with regard to the forming</b> of an embryo, <b>that when an embryo is formed,</b> it is <b>formed from its middle. But with regard to life, everyone,</b> i.e., both <i>tanna’im</i> in the <i>baraita</i>, agree that <b>it is in his nose, as it is written: “All in whose nostrils was the breath of the spirit of life”</b> (Genesis 7:22).
The mishna taught another opinion. <b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov says:</b> The distance should be measured <b>from the place where</b> the victim <b>became a slain</b> person, <b>from his neck.</b> The Gemara poses a question: <b>What is the reason of Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>As it is written: “To lay you upon the necks of the wicked who are to be slain”</b> (Ezekiel 21:34), which shows that being slain occurs at the neck.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The mishna continues to describe the ritual. After they would take the measurement, <b>the Elders of Jerusalem took their leave and went</b> away. <b>The Elders of the city</b> that is closest to the corpse <b>bring a heifer from cattle, which has not pulled a yoke. But a blemish does not disqualify it,</b> because, unlike the description of the red heifer, the Torah does not state that it must be without blemish. <b>And they bring it down to a stream</b> that is <b><i>eitan</i>. <i>Eitan</i></b> in this context means <b>as</b> the word generally <b>indicates,</b> powerful. The stream must have <b>a forceful</b> flow. The mishna comments: <b>Even if it is not forceful, it is a valid</b> site for the ritual. <b>And they break the neck of</b> the heifer <b>from behind with a cleaver. And</b> with regard to <b>its place,</b> where the heifer was standing when its neck was broken, <b>it is prohibited</b> for that ground <b>to be sown or to be worked, but</b> it is <b>permitted to comb flax there or to cut stones there.</b>
<b>The Elders of that city</b> would then <b>wash their hands in water in</b> the <b>place of the breaking of the neck of the heifer, and they would recite: “Our hands did not spill this blood, nor did our eyes see”</b> (Deuteronomy 21:7). The mishna explains: <b>But did it enter our minds that the Elders of the court are spillers of blood,</b> that they must make such a declaration? <b>Rather,</b> they mean to declare <b>that</b> the victim <b>did not come to us and</b> then <b>we let him take his leave without food, and we did not see him and</b> then <b>leave him</b> alone to depart <b>without accompaniment.</b> They therefore attest that they took care of all his needs and are not responsible for his death even indirectly.

Daf 46a

<b>And the priests recite: “Forgive, Lord, Your people Israel, whom You have redeemed, and suffer not innocent blood to remain in the midst of Your people Israel”</b> (Deuteronomy 21:8). <b>They did not have to recite</b> the conclusion of the verse: <b>“And the blood shall be forgiven for them,”</b> as this is not part of the priests’ statement, <b>but</b> rather <b>the Divine Spirit informs them: When you shall do so, the blood is forgiven for you.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> With regard to the mishna’s statement that the heifer is not disqualified by a blemish, the Gemara suggests: <b>And a blemish should disqualify in</b> the case of the <b>heifer, by</b> means of <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference: <b>And if</b> in the case of the red heifer, <b>which is not disqualified</b> by <b>years,</b> as it may be of any age, and yet <b>a blemish disqualifies it,</b> then <b>a heifer</b> for this ritual, <b>which is disqualified by years,</b> as it is valid only until two years of age, <b>is it not logical that a blemish should disqualify it?</b> The Gemara answers: It <b>is different there,</b> in the case of the red heifer, <b>as the verse states: “Wherein [<i>bah</i>] has no blemish”</b> (Numbers 19:2). This serves as an exclusion and teaches that it is only <b>with regard to it [<i>bah</i>]</b> that <b>a blemish is disqualifying, but a blemish is not disqualifying with regard to</b> the <b>heifer</b> of the ritual of the breaking of the neck.
The Gemara asks: <b>However, if that is so,</b> if the word “<i>bah</i>” precludes a derivation by an <i>a fortiori</i> inference, then any <b>other labor</b> performed with the red heifer, apart from pulling a yoke, <b>should not disqualify it.</b> While the verse disqualifies a red heifer only if it pulled a yoke, as it states: “And upon which never came a yoke” (Numbers 19:2), a similar <i>a fortiori</i> inference could be learned from the heifer whose neck is to be broken to disqualify a red heifer that has performed any labor. However, since the verse states with regard to the heifer whose neck is to be broken: “That has not been worked with [<i>bah</i>]” (Deuteronomy 21:3), this indicates that labor is disqualifying only for “<i>bah</i>,” a heifer whose neck is to be broken, but not for a red heifer.
<b>Why,</b> then, does <b>Rav Yehuda say</b> that <b>Rav says:</b> If <b>he placed a bundle [<i>uda</i>] of sacks on</b> a red heifer<b>,</b>the heifer is immediately <b>disqualified</b> from being used as the red heifer; <b>and</b> as <b>for the heifer</b> whose neck is broken, it is not disqualified by such labor <b>until it pulls</b> and moves the burden, as the verse states: “That has not pulled a yoke” (Deuteronomy 21:3). Why does bearing the weight of the bundle disqualify the red heifer? The Gemara explains: The <i>halakha</i> with regard to the red <b>heifer is different, as we learn</b> by a verbal analogy between the word <b>“yoke”</b> used with regard to the red heifer and the word <b>“yoke”</b> used with regard <b>to</b> the <b>heifer</b> whose neck is broken that any labor disqualifies the former.
The Gemara raises an objection: If there is a verbal analogy between the red heifer and the heifer that will have its neck broken, then the <i>halakha</i> that a blemish should disqualify the heifer whose neck is broken <b>should also be derived</b> from the usage of <b>“yoke”</b> with regard to the heifer whose neck is broken and <b>from</b> the usage of <b>“yoke”</b> with regard <b>to</b> the red <b>heifer.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>The Merciful One has excluded</b> this possibility by placing in the verse the word <b>“<i>bah</i>,”</b> which indicates that disqualification due to blemish applies only to the red heifer and not to the heifer whose neck is broken.
The Gemara counters this claim: <b>In</b> the verse concerning <b>a heifer</b> whose neck is broken, the Torah <b>also writes “<i>bah</i>”;</b> it should be the case that forms of labor other than pulling a yoke are disqualifying only with regard to it and not with regard to the red heifer. The Gemara answers: <b>That</b> word “<i>bah</i>” <b>is required by</b> Rav Yehuda in order <b>to exclude sacred</b> offerings, i.e., <b>which are not disqualified by labor,</b> and one may bring an animal that has been used for labor as an offering. <b>It might enter your mind to say</b> that this <b>should be derived by an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference <b>from a heifer</b> whose neck is broken, as follows: <b>And if</b> with regard to <b>a heifer</b> whose neck is broken, <b>which is not disqualified by a blemish, labor</b> nevertheless <b>disqualifies it,</b> then with regard to <b>sacred</b> offerings, <b>which are disqualified by a blemish, is it not right that labor should disqualify them?</b> In order to counter this argument, the word “<i>bah</i>” teaches us that a sacred offering is not disqualified by labor.
With regard to this suggested <i>a fortiori</i> inference, the Gemara observes that <b>it can be refuted</b> in the following manner: <b>What</b> about the fact that <b>a heifer</b> whose neck is broken <b>is disqualified by years,</b> as once it reaches two years of age it is no longer classified as a heifer? As it is clear that the heifer whose neck is to be broken carries some restrictions that do not apply to sacred offerings, perhaps being disqualified by labor is another such restriction. The Gemara refutes this argument: <b>Is that to say that there are no sacred</b> offerings <b>that are disqualified by years?</b> There are several offerings that may be brought only in their first or second year, and <b>where the verse is necessary</b> to teach that sacred offerings are not disqualified by labor, it is with regard to <b>those sacred</b> offerings <b>that are disqualified by years.</b>
The Gemara raises an objection: <b>But is</b> the <i>halakha</i> <b>that sacred</b> offerings <b>are not disqualified by labor derived from this</b> verse? <b>It is derived from elsewhere.</b> The verse states with regard to sacred offerings: <b>“Blind, or broken, or maimed, or having a wart, or scabbed, or scurvy, you shall not offer these to the Lord”</b> (Leviticus 22:22). This verse serves to create an exclusion, teaching that it is <b>these</b> that <b>you may not sacrifice, but you may sacrifice sacred</b> animals <b>that have been used for labor.</b> The Gemara answers: It <b>was necessary</b> to state the <i>halakha</i> twice. <b>It might enter your mind to say</b> that <b>this</b> <i>halakha</i>, that one may sacrifice animals that have been used for labor, <b>applies only</b> in a case where <b>they were used for permitted labor, but</b> if they were used for <b>prohibited labor,</b> e.g., on Shabbat, you might <b>say that it is prohibited</b> to bring <b>them</b> as offerings. In order to refute this argument, it is <b>necessary</b> to state the <i>halakha</i> again.
The Gemara poses another question: <b>But this</b> <i>halakha</i> that prohibited labor does not disqualify offerings <b>is also derived from here,</b> a verse with regard to the sacrifice of blemished animals: <b>“And from the hand of a stranger you shall not offer the bread of your God from any of these, because…</b>there is a blemish in them” (Leviticus 22:25). This verse emphasizes that it is only <b>“these,”</b> i.e., blemished animals, that <b>you may not sacrifice, but you may sacrifice sacred</b> animals <b>that have been used for labor.</b> Since this verse is discussing the possibility of accepting offerings from a gentile, who presumably also performed prohibited labor with the animal, this demonstrates that prohibited labor does not disqualify animals from being sacrificed as offerings.
The Gemara answers: It <b>was necessary</b> to teach this <i>halakha</i> a third time. <b>It might enter your mind to say: This</b> <i>halakha</i>, that labor does not disqualify offerings, <b>applies</b> only where <b>one performed labor with them when they were non-sacred</b> and afterward dedicated them as offerings, <b>but</b> if <b>one performed labor with them when they were</b> already <b>sacred</b> animals, you might <b>say that it is prohibited</b> to bring <b>them</b> as offerings. In order to refute this argument, it is <b>necessary</b> to teach this <i>halakha</i> in three separate places.
§ The Gemara returns to discuss <b>the</b> matter <b>itself: Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Rav says:</b> If <b>one placed a bundle of sacks on</b> a red heifer, <b>it is disqualified. And</b> as <b>for a heifer</b> whose neck is broken, it is not disqualified <b>until it pulls</b> a burden. The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: It states with regard to the red heifer: “That upon which never came <b>a yoke”</b> (Numbers 19:2). <b>I have</b> derived <b>only a yoke; from where</b> do I derive that other types of labor also disqualify the animal? <b>You</b> can <b>say</b> the following <b><i>a fortiori</i></b> inference: <b>And if</b> with regard to <b>a heifer</b> whose neck is broken, <b>which is not disqualified by a blemish, other</b> types of <b>labor disqualify it,</b> then with regard to a red <b>heifer, which is disqualified by a blemish, is it not right that other</b> types of <b>labor should disqualify it?</b>
<b>And if it is your wish to say</b> that this <i>a fortiori</i> inference is unsound, you can learn this <i>halakha</i> by a verbal analogy: <b>It is stated here,</b> with regard to the red heifer, <b>“yoke”</b> (Numbers 19:2), <b>and it is stated there,</b> with regard to the heifer whose neck is broken, <b>“yoke”</b> (Deuteronomy 21:3). <b>Just as there, other</b> types of <b>labor disqualify it, so too here,</b> in the case of the red heifer, <b>other</b> types of <b>labor disqualify</b> it.
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is the meaning of: <b>If it is your wish to say?</b> What potential problem with the <i>a fortiori</i> inference necessitates the verbal analogy? The Gemara explains: <b>And perhaps you would say</b> that the <i>a fortiori</i> inference <b>can be refuted</b> in the following manner: <b>What</b> is unique <b>about a heifer</b> whose neck is broken is <b>that it is disqualified by years,</b> which is not the case for a red heifer. <b>Alternatively,</b> one could suggest that <b>sacred</b> offerings <b>will prove</b> that this inference should not be made, <b>as a blemish is disqualifying with regard to them, but labor is not disqualifying with regard to them.</b>
As the <i>a fortiori</i> inference can be refuted in either of these ways, there is a need for the verbal analogy: <b>It is stated here “yoke,” and it is stated there “yoke.” Just as there,</b> in the case of <b>a heifer</b> whose neck is broken, <b>other</b> types of <b>labor</b> disqualify it, <b>so too, other</b> types of <b>labor</b> disqualify a red heifer. The Gemara raises an objection to this verbal analogy: <b>And from the place that you came</b> you can offer an alternative exposition: <b>Just as below,</b> in the case of a heifer whose neck is broken, it is not disqualified by carrying a burden <b>until it pulls</b> the yoke, <b>so too here,</b> a red heifer should not be disqualified <b>until it pulls</b> the yoke, contrary to the statement of Rav.
The Gemara answers the objection to the statement of Rav from the <i>baraita</i>: It <b>is</b> a dispute among <b><i>tanna’im</i>, as there are those who cite</b> the source of <b>this</b> <i>halakha</i>, that labor disqualifies a red heifer, by verbal analogy <b>from a heifer</b> whose neck is broken, and therefore the red heifer is disqualified only if it pulls the burden. <b>There are</b> also <b>those who cite</b> the source of <b>this</b> <i>halakha</i> <b>from</b> a red <b>heifer itself,</b> and consequently they disqualify the red heifer even if it did not pull the yoke.
This is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to a red heifer: From the term <b>“yoke” I have</b> derived <b>only</b> that <b>a yoke</b> disqualifies a red heifer; <b>from where</b> do I derive the <b>other</b> types of <b>labor? The verse states: “That upon which never came a yoke”</b> (Numbers 19:2). The verse could be read with a pause after the word “came,” which would teach that it is disqualified <b>in any case,</b> no matter what labor was performed with it. <b>If so, what</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states “yoke,”</b> if all forms of labor disqualify it? It teaches us that <b>a yoke</b> placed on the animal <b>disqualifies</b> it <b>whether</b> the yoke was on the animal <b>at the time of</b> performing <b>labor</b> or <b>whether</b> it was on the animal <b>not at the time of</b> performing <b>labor,</b> i.e., it was merely placed on the animal. However, <b>other</b> types of <b>labor</b> actions <b>disqualify</b> animals <b>only at the time of</b> actually performing <b>labor.</b> Rav ruled in accordance with this opinion.
The Gemara raises an objection: <b>And</b> perhaps one can <b>say</b> a different exposition of the verse: <b>“That upon which never came”</b> is <b>a generalization</b> that disqualifies the animal after any type of labor, while <b>“yoke”</b> is <b>a detail.</b> There is <b>a generalization and a detail,</b> and the principle of halakhic exposition in that case is that <b>there is nothing in the generalization other</b> than <b>what is in the detail.</b> Therefore, with regard to <b>a yoke, yes,</b> it will disqualify an animal from being used as a red heifer; but with regard to <b>anything else, no,</b> it will not disqualify the animal. The Gemara answers: <b>“That</b> upon which never came” <b>is an amplification,</b> and the addition of this term results in this verse not belonging to the category of generalizations and details.
The Gemara comments: <b>And a case like this is also taught in</b> a <i>baraita</i> <b>with regard to a heifer</b> whose neck is broken: From the word <b>“yoke” I have</b> derived <b>only</b> that <b>a yoke</b> disqualifies; <b>from where</b> do I derive the <b>other</b> types of <b>labor? The</b> same <b>verse states: “That has not been worked with”</b> (Deuteronomy 21:3), to teach that it is disqualified <b>in any case,</b> no matter what labor was performed with it. <b>If so, what</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states “yoke”?</b> It serves to teach us that <b>a yoke</b> placed on the animal <b>disqualifies</b> it <b>whether</b> the yoke was on the animal <b>at the time of</b> performing <b>labor or whether</b> it was on the animal <b>not at the time of performing labor,</b> i.e., it was merely placed on the animal, whereas <b>other</b> types of <b>labor</b> actions <b>disqualify</b> animals <b>only at the time of</b> actually performing <b>labor.</b>
The Gemara raises an objection: <b>And</b> perhaps one can <b>say</b> a different exposition of the verse: <b>“That has not been worked with”</b> is <b>a generalization</b> that disqualifies the animal after any type of labor, while <b>“yoke”</b> is <b>a detail.</b> There is <b>a generalization and a detail,</b> and the principle of halakhic exposition in that case is that <b>there is nothing in the generalization other</b> than <b>what is in the detail,</b> which means: With regard to <b>a yoke, yes,</b> it will disqualify an animal, but with regard to <b>anything else, no,</b> it will not disqualify it. The Gemara answers: The phrase <b>“that</b> has not been worked with” <b>is an amplification,</b> and the addition of this term results in this verse not belonging to the category of generalizations and details.
<b>Rabbi Abbahu said: I asked of Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> This <b>pulling of a yoke</b> that disqualifies a heifer whose neck is broken, <b>with how much,</b> i.e., how far, must the animal pull the yoke for it to be disqualified? <b>He said to me: Like</b> the measure of the size of <b>a full yoke. A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: Does this mean according <b>to its length or</b> according <b>to its width? One of the Sages, and Rabbi Ya’akov was his name, said to them: It was explained to me personally by Rabbi Yoḥanan himself: The pulling of a yoke is</b> according <b>to its width,</b> which is <b>a handbreadth.</b>
The Gemara poses a question: <b>And</b> since he stated a fixed measurement, <b>let him</b> merely <b>state: A handbreadth.</b> Why was it necessary to add that this is the width of a yoke? The Gemara answers: <b>This teaches us</b> that <b>the measure</b> of <b>a yoke</b> along its width <b>is a handbreadth. What difference is there</b> in knowing this fact? This teaches that <b>in</b> the case of <b>commercial transactions,</b> a buyer may retract his purchase if the yoke he was given is less than a handbreadth wide.
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Shaul says: For what</b> reason <b>did the Torah say</b> to <b>bring a heifer</b> whose neck is broken <b>to a stream? The Holy One, Blessed be He, said: Let something that did not produce fruit,</b> i.e., a heifer that has not given birth, <b>come and</b> have its neck <b>be broken at</b> a stream that flows forcefully, which is <b>a place that does not produce fruit, and atone for</b> the murder of <b>one who was not given an opportunity to produce fruit.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is this <b>fruit</b> that he was not given an opportunity to produce? <b>If we say</b> it refers to <b>being fruitful and multiplying,</b> i.e., that the killer prevented him from having more children, <b>but if that is so,</b> in the case <b>of an elderly person or a eunuch, so too</b> will you say <b>that we do not break</b> the heifer’s neck because they could not have had any more children even had they lived? <b>Rather,</b> the fruit are <b>mitzvot,</b> as the killer deprived the victim of the opportunity to perform additional mitzvot.
The mishna taught: <b>And they bring it down to a stream</b> that is <b><i>eitan</i>. <i>Eitan</i></b> in this context means <b>as</b> the word generally <b>indicates,</b> forceful. <b>The Sages taught: From where</b> is it derived <b>that <i>eitan</i> is forceful?</b> It is <b>as</b> it <b>is stated:</b>

Daf 46b

<b>“Firm [<i>eitan</i>] is your dwelling-place, and your nest is set in the rock”</b> (Numbers 24:21), <b>and it states: “Hear, O you mountains, the Lord’s controversy, and the enduring rocks [<i>eitanim</i>], the foundations of the earth”</b> (Micah 6:2). The use of the word in these verses indicates that “<i>eitan</i>” means something hard, like a rock or a mountain. <b>Others say</b> a different explanation of the word <i>eitan</i>: <b>From where</b> is it derived <b>that <i>eitan</i> means old? As</b> it <b>is stated: “It is an ancient [<i>eitan</i>] nation, a nation from of old”</b> (Jeremiah 5:15).
§ The mishna taught: <b>And they break the neck [<i>orfin</i>] of</b> the heifer <b>from behind with a cleaver.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reason</b> that the Sages understood that the heifer is killed in this manner? They <b>derive</b> that the term <b><i>arifa</i>,</b> which describes what is done to the heifer, refers to breaking the back of the neck, <b>from</b> the term <b><i>arifa</i></b> stated with regard to the <b>bird</b> brought as <b>a sin-offering</b> (see Leviticus 5:8).
§ The mishna taught further: <b>And</b> with regard to <b>its place,</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> for that ground <b>to be sown or to be worked. The Sages taught:</b> The verse: <b>“Which may be neither worked nor sown”</b> (Deuteronomy 21:4) is referring <b>to the past,</b> that is, a place which has not previously been worked or sown. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yoshiya. Rabbi Yonatan says:</b> It speaks <b>of the future,</b> meaning it is prohibited to sow or work the land from that point onward.
<b>Rava said:</b> As <b>for the future, everyone agrees</b> that it is prohibited to sow or work the land, <b>as it is written</b> “neither worked <b>nor sown”</b> in the future tense. <b>When they disagree</b> is with regard to <b>the past. Rabbi Yoshiya,</b> who disqualifies a place that was sown beforehand, <b>holds: Does it state: And shall not be worked,</b> in the form of a future command? <b>And Rabbi Yonatan</b> responds: <b>Does it state: And was not worked,</b> in the past tense? <b>And Rabbi Yoshiya</b> answers: The term <b>“which” indicates the past. And</b> as for <b>Rabbi Yonatan,</b> in his opinion the term <b>“which” is</b> a term of <b>amplification,</b> as will be explained later in the Gemara, and it is not referring to the past.
§ The mishna taught: <b>But it is permitted to comb flax there or to cut stones there. The Sages taught:</b> From the phrase <b>“which may be neither worked nor sown,” I have</b> derived <b>only sowing; from where</b> do I derive that <b>other</b> types of <b>labor</b> are also prohibited? <b>The verse states: “Which may be neither worked,”</b> indicating that it may not be worked <b>in any manner.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>If so, why does the verse</b> also need to <b>state “nor sown”?</b> It is in order <b>to say to you: Just as sowing is unique</b> in <b>that it is</b> labor performed <b>on the land itself, so too, all</b> labor <b>that is</b> performed <b>on the land itself</b> is prohibited. This <b>excludes combing flax and cutting stones, which are not</b> done <b>on the land itself.</b>
The Gemara raises an objection: <b>And</b> perhaps one can <b>say</b> a different exposition: <b>“Which may be neither worked”</b> is <b>a generalization,</b> and <b>“nor sown” a detail.</b> When the Torah writes <b>a generalization and a detail, there is nothing in the generalization other</b> than <b>what is in the detail,</b> i.e., the detail serves to impose a limit on the generalization. Consequently, the verse is teaching that with regard to <b>sowing, yes,</b> it is prohibited, but with regard to <b>anything else, no,</b> it is not prohibited. The Gemara again answers: The term <b>“which” is an amplification,</b> and the addition of this term results in this verse not belonging to the category of generalizations and details.
§ The mishna taught that <b>the Elders of the city</b> would then <b>wash their hands. The Sages taught:</b> With regard to the verse: <b>“And all the Elders of that city, who are nearest to the slain man, shall wash their hands over the heifer whose neck was broken in the valley”</b> (Deuteronomy 21:6), one might have thought <b>that</b> there is <b>no</b> need for <b>the verse to state: “Whose neck was broken,”</b> because there is no heifer mentioned other than the one whose neck was broken. <b>And what</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states: “Whose neck was broken”?</b> It serves to teach us that they wash their hands <b>over the place where the heifer’s neck was broken.</b>
The verse further states: <b>“And they shall say: Our hands did not spill this blood, nor did our eyes see”</b> (Deuteronomy 21:7). The mishna explains: <b>But did it enter our minds that</b> the Elders of <b>the court are spillers of blood,</b> that they must make such a declaration? <b>Rather,</b> they mean to declare: The victim <b>did not come to us and</b> then <b>we let him take his leave without food, and we did not see him and</b> then <b>leave him</b> alone to depart <b>without accompaniment.</b> They therefore attest that they took care of all his needs and are not responsible for his death even indirectly.
<b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Meir would say: There is coercion with regard to accompaniment,</b> i.e., one who does not want to accompany another is nevertheless required to do so, <b>as the reward for accompaniment is without measure.</b> The proof of the importance of accompaniment is from a verse, <b>as it is stated</b> with regard to when the Jewish people laid siege to the city of Bethel: <b>“And the watchers saw a man come out of the city, and they said to him: Show us, please, the entrance into the city, and we will deal kindly with you”</b> (Judges 1:24), <b>and it is written: “And he showed them the entrance to the city”</b> (Judges 1:25). <b>And what kindness did they perform with him?</b> It is <b>that they killed the entire city by the sword, but that man and his family they sent</b> free.
The Gemara elaborates on the reward received in that story. The next verse states: <b>“And the man went to the land of the Hittites, and he built a city, and he called its name Luz; that is its name to this day”</b> (Judges 1:26). <b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: This <b>is</b> the city <b>Luz where sky blue</b> wool <b>is dyed.</b> It <b>is</b> the same city <b>Luz where,</b> although <b>Sennacherib came</b> and exiled many nations from place to place, he <b>did not disarrange</b> and exile <b>its</b> inhabitants; <b>Nebuchadnezzar,</b> who conquered many lands, <b>did not destroy it; and even the angel of death has no permission to pass through it. Rather, its Elders, when they have decided that they have reached the end</b> of life, <b>go outside the</b> city <b>wall and die.</b>
